Nazm and Ma'na: The Dual Nature of Qur'anic Speech
Analyzing why 'nazm' is used and the scope of Qur'an as both wording and meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between kalam lafzi (verbal speech) and kalam nafsi (mental speech), and apply that distinction to the Hanafi ruling on reading Qur'an translations outside prayer.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is both verbal speech and eternal meaning; only the meaning (kalam nafsi) is uncreated.

This passage explains why the author uses 'nazm' instead of 'lafz' and clarifies the relationship between the created translation of the Qur'an and the uncreated divine attribute of speech. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi rulings, such as the permissibility of reading the Qur'an in Persian for a junub outside prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of Qur'anic speech
Recognize that 'nazm' refers to the verbal arrangement and 'ma'na' refers to the meaning. The passage distinguishes between the created translation (stories) and the eternal meaning (commands, prohibitions).
Kalam lafzi is created; kalam nafsi is eternal.
2
Understand why 'nazm' is used
Note that 'nazm' is used out of respect as it implies orderly arrangement, unlike 'lafz' which means 'casting'. This shows the reverence for the Qur'anic text.
The author deliberately chooses respectful terminology.
3
Trace the legal application
Abu Hanifa considers both wording and meaning outside prayer, so reading a Persian translation is permissible. In prayer, only the original Arabic is considered due to an obstacle (the need for specific recitation).
The ruling differs based on context (prayer vs. other).
4
Evaluate the counter-opinion
Later Hanafi scholars prohibited it out of caution, but the original position is preferred because it aligns with the dual nature of the Qur'an.
Tarjih: the original ruling remains mufta bihi.
5
Summarize the usul principle
The distinction between kalam nafsi and its translation is essential; the translation is created while the attribute is eternal. This is an application of the Hanafi stance on the nature of the Qur'an.
Do not confuse translation with the eternal attribute.

Source Text

تقسيم وجوه النظم
لا يلتفت إلا إلى الذات، فلا طعن عليه في أنّه كيف يُجوّز القراءة. بالفارسي مع
أي ذاته تعالى
القدرة
على العربي المنزل، وأما فيما سوى الصلاة، فهو يُراعي جانبهما جميعًا. وإنما أطلق النظم مكان اللفظ رعاية للأدب؛ لأن النظم في اللغة جمع اللؤلؤ في السلك، واللفظ هو الرمي، وإن كان النظم يطلق في العرف على الشعر أيضًا، وينبغي أن يعلم أن النظم إشارة إلى الكلام اللفظي، والمعنى إلى الكلام النفسي، ولكن المعنى الذي هو ترجمة النظم حادث كالنظم؛ لأنه عبارة عن قصة يوسف وإخوته، وعن فرعون وغرقه مثلاً، وكل ذلك حادث.
ثم هو دال على أمر الله تعالى ونهيه وحكمه، وخبره، وهو قديم بلا ريب عندنا، فتنبه له.
النظم
الصلاة
سوى الصلاة فهو : أي الإمام أبو حنيفة والله يراعي جانبي اللفظ والمعنى جميعًا، فلا يحرم للجنب والحائض حينئذ قراءة القرآن بالفارسية، ولا مس مصحف كتب بها، وأما بعض المتأخرين فقالوا: يحرمان لهما احتياطا. (القمر) سوى الصلاة جواب سؤال تقريره أنه ما الدليل على أن مسلك الإمام أبي حنيفة والله ليس بكون القرآن اسما للمعنى فقط بل هو قائل بكونه اسما للنظم والمعنى جميعا، وتقرير الجواب: أن الدليل على ذلك أنه فيما سوى يراعي جانب اللفظ والمعنى جميعًا، فرعايته المعنى خاصا في الصلاة لعارض ذكر سابقا.(السنبلي) الكلام النفسي فيه أما أولاً ؛ فلأنه غير مطابق لغرض الأصولي، فإن غرضه متعلق بترجمة اللفظي، وهو المطابق لكلامهم من تقسيمهم: النظم باعتبار وضع النظم للمعنى، واستعمال اللفظ في المعنى، وظهور المعنى وخفائه وكيفية دلالة اللفظ على المعنى وغير ذلك. وأما ثانيًا، فلأنه يخالف ما قال الشارح سابقا، ولا أنه اسم للمعنى فقط إلخ؛ لكونه مناديًا على أن المراد بالمعنى ترجمة اللفظي، لا الكلام النفسي ، ثم اعلم أن الكلام النفسي عبارة عن صفة قديمة قائمة بذات الله تعالى منافية للسكوت والخرس يدل عليها الكلام اللفظي دلالة عقلية. (القمر) ولكن المعنى إلخ: دفع للتوهم الناشئ من الكلام السابق وهو أن يكون ترجمة النظم قديمة، فإن هذه الترجمة معنى كما أن الكلام النفسي معنى وهو قديم فهي أيضًا قديمة. (القمر) ولكن المعنى إلخ جواب سؤال تقريره: أنه لعل المراد بالمعنى الذي قال فيه الشارح: أنه إشارة إلى الكلام النفسي ترجمة النظم؛ لأنه متبادر، فيلزم خلاف الإجماع والمشهور، وهو أن الكلام النفسي قديم، فإن ترجمته حادث فأجاب بما حاصله أن ذلك ليس بمراد بالمعنى المذكور بل هو غير الترجمة، وهو مدلول النظم من أمره تعالى ونهيه وحكمه، وخبره إلخ (السنبلي) وهو : أي كل واحد من هذه الأمور قديم عندنا خلافًا لمن ذهب إلى حدوث صفاته تعالى . (القمر) فتنبه له إلخ أي ليظهر لك الفرق بين الكلام النفسي وترجمة النظم. (السنبلي)
Division of aspects of nazm: He does not pay attention except to the essence, so there is no objection against him as to how he permits reading in Persian despite the Arabic revelation. But other than prayer, he considers both aspects together. He used nazm instead of lafz out of respect; because nazm in language is the stringing of pearls on a thread, while lafz is casting. Although nazm is also used in custom for poetry. And one should know that nazm refers to verbal speech, and ma'na to mental speech. But the ma'na that is the translation of nazm is created like nazm; because it expresses the story of Yusuf and his brothers, and of Pharaoh and his drowning, etc., all of which is created. Then it indicates the command of Allah, His prohibition, His ruling, and His information, and that is eternal without doubt in our view, so be alert. Nazm, prayer, other than prayer: He, i.e., Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, considers both aspects of lafz and ma'na together. So it is not prohibited for a junub or menstruating woman to read the Qur'an in Persian, nor to touch a mushaf written in it. But some later scholars said: both are prohibited out of caution. (al-Qamar) 'Other than prayer' is an answer to an implied question: what is the proof that Imam Abu Hanifa's position is not that the Qur'an is a name for ma'na only, but that he holds it is a name for both nazm and ma'na? The answer: the proof is that other than prayer he considers both lafz and ma'na together; his consideration of ma'na specifically in prayer is due to an obstacle mentioned earlier. (al-Sanbali) As for mental speech: first, it does not match the purpose of the usuli, because his purpose is related to the translation of verbal speech, which matches their division: nazm with respect to placing nazm for ma'na, using lafz for ma'na, the clarity and obscurity of ma'na, how lafz indicates ma'na, and other matters. Second, it contradicts what the commentator said earlier, that it is not a name for ma'na only, etc., because it indicates that the intended ma'na is the translation of verbal speech, not mental speech. Then know that mental speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah, incompatible with silence and muteness, which verbal speech indicates by rational indication. (al-Qamar) But the ma'na, etc.: a rebuttal of the misconception arising from the previous speech that the translation of nazm is eternal. For this translation is a ma'na just as mental speech is a ma'na and is eternal, so it too would be eternal. (al-Qamar) But the ma'na, etc.: an answer to an implied question: perhaps the intended ma'na in which the commentator said it refers to mental speech is the translation of nazm, because it is preponderant, so it would contradict consensus and the famous view that mental speech is eternal, while its translation is created. So he answered with the summary that the intended ma'na is not that, but rather it is other than the translation, and it is the import of nazm: His commands, prohibitions, rulings, and information, etc. (al-Sanbali) And it: i.e., each of these matters is eternal in our view, contrary to those who hold His attributes are created. (al-Qamar) So be alert to it, etc.: i.e., so that the difference between mental speech and the translation of nazm becomes clear to you. (al-Sanbali)
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and legal analysis of the terms nazm (arrangement) and lafz (utterance) and their relation to the Qur'an. The commentator explains that the author used nazm instead of lafz out of respect, because lafz literally means 'casting' while nazm implies beautiful arrangement like stringing pearls. The key point is that the Qur'an consists of both verbal speech (kalam lafzi) and mental speech (kalam nafsi). The verbal speech is created (hadith) because it consists of stories of prophets and events, while the mental speech is eternal (qadim) as an attribute of Allah. This distinction affects rulings: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that outside of prayer, reading the Qur'an in Persian is permissible (not haram) because he considers both the wording and meaning – the meaning is still conveyed even if the wording changes. However, in prayer, considering meaning alone is not enough due to a specific obstacle. Later scholars prohibit it out of caution. The passage also refutes the misconception that the translation of nazm is eternal, clarifying that the eternal ma'na is not the translation but the divine attributes themselves – His commands, prohibitions, rulings, and information. A common misunderstanding is confusing the created translation with the eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi).

Key Terms
النظم (al-nazm): The verbal arrangement (lafz) of the Qur'an, like stringing pearls; refers to verbal speech (kalam lafzi).
المعنى (al-ma'na): The meaning intended by the nazm; can refer to the translation (created) or the eternal attribute (mental speech).
الكلام النفسي (al-kalam al-nafsi): An eternal attribute of Allah, subsisting in His essence, indicated by verbal speech rationally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a junub reading the Qur'an in Persian outside of prayer according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) It is prohibited because translation is not the Qur'an.
B) It is permissible because the meaning is still considered.
C) It is disliked but not haram.
D) It is permissible only if the person cannot read Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an is both verbal arrangement (nazm) and eternal meaning (kalam nafsi); understanding this distinction is essential for proper fiqh rulings on translations and recitation.

The Fourfold Classification of Word Usage in Hanafi Usul
Defining haqiqah, majaz, sarih, and kinayah in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqah and majaz, and between sarih and kinayah, and explain the Hanafi usul position that sarih and kinayah apply to both literal and figurative words.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, every word usage is one of four kinds: haqiqah sarih, haqiqah kinayah, majaz sarih, or majaz kinayah.

Words in legal texts are not all used in the same way. This lesson introduces the foundational classification of word usage in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The passage from Noor al-Anwar defines haqiqah, majaz, sarih, and kinayah, and clarifies a key Hanafi principle: the explicitness of a word (sarih/kinayah) is independent of whether it is literal or figurative (haqiqah/majaz). This yields four categories, not two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes of classification
The passage presents two dichotomies: haqiqah vs. majaz (based on original vs. transferred meaning) and sarih vs. kinayah (based on explicitness). These axes are independent; any word can be placed in their intersection.
In Hanafi usul, the classification is applied after usage, not before.
2
Define haqiqah and majaz
A word used in its original, conventionally assigned meaning is haqiqah. If used in a transferred meaning due to a relational connection (alaqah), it is majaz.
Haqiqah is the default; departure requires a qarinah.
3
Define sarih and kinayah
A word is sarih if its meaning is clear and explicit without need for interpretation. Kinayah is used when the meaning is implied indirectly, requiring inference from context.
Kinayah here is a type of usage, not a subset of majaz.
4
Combine the two axes to form four categories
Apply the sarih/kinayah distinction to both haqiqah and majaz. For example, a word used literally but with an implicit meaning is haqiqah kinayah; a figurative word with a clear meaning is majaz sarih.
This quadruple division is unique to Hanafi usul; in balaghah, kinayah is only figurative.
5
Contrast with balaghah terminology
Scholars of balaghah consider kinayah a type of majaz. Hanafi usul maintains that kinayah can occur with both haqiqah and majaz, emphasizing that explicitness is orthogonal to literalness.
The text clarifies that the author's division is quadruple, not binary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الثالث باعتبار استعمال اللفظ في المعنى] والثالث في وجوه استعمال ذلك النظم، أي التقسيم الثالث في طرق استعمال ذلك النظم المذكور سابقاً من أنه استعمل في معناه الموضوع له أو غيره، أو استعمل مع انكشاف معناه أو استتاره. وهي أربعة أيضًا الحقيقة والمجاز، والصريح والكناية؛ لأنه إن استعمل في معناه الموضوع له فهو حقيقة، أو في غير الموضوع له فمجاز ، ثم كل منهما إن استعمل بعلاقة بانكشاف معناه فهو الصريح، وإلا فهو الكناية. بالتأمل أي بالنظر بعد استحضار معانيه بملاحظة السياق والقرائن. (القمر) يتعلق بالكلام فإن ظهور المراد والوقوف عليه يكون بالكلام. (القمر) يتعلق بكلمة إلخ فإن دلالة اللفظ على معنى واحد أو كثير، وكذا استعمال اللفظ بانكشاف معناه واستتاره، إنما يكون بالكلمة كما أن ظهور المراد والوقوف عليه الذين يكونان في الثاني والرابع يتعلقان بالكلام. (السنبلي) النظم المذكور أي الدال على المعنى وهذا إيماء إلى أن اللام في قول المصنف "النظم" للعهد. (القمر) إن استعمل إلخ فيه إيماء أن اللفظ قبل الاستعمال لا يسمى حقيقةً ولا مجازاً، ولا صريحا ولا كناية، وللتفصيل مقام آخر (القمر) ثم كل منهما إلخ الغرض منه على ما هو الظاهر أن الصريح والكناية يجريان في كل واحد الحقيقة والمجاز لا كما قال أرباب البيان من أن الكناية مقابل المجاز، فالتقسيم الثالث رباعي ليس بثنائي، وليس الغرض منه الإيراد على المصنف بأن الصريح والكناية قسمان للحقيقة والمجاز لا لأصل المقسم، فالتقسيم ثنائي، فقول المصنف: وهي أربعة في غير موضعه كما لا يخفى تأمل. (القمر) فهو الكناية فالكناية في اصطلاح هذا الفن هو التعبير عن الشيء بلفظ لا يكون صريحًا، وفي اصطلاح علم البيان: عبارة عن استعمال اللفظ في الموضوع ،له والانتقال إلى لازمه أو ملزومه على اختلاف الرأيين. (القمر) من
The third division according to the usage of a word in meaning. The third is about the modes of using that structure: that is, the third division concerns the ways of using the aforementioned structure (which indicates meaning) — whether it is used in its original meaning or in another, or used with its meaning being unveiled or concealed. These are also four: al-haqiqah, al-majaz, al-sarih, and al-kinayah. Because if it is used in its original meaning, it is haqiqah; if in other than its original meaning, it is majaz. Then each of these [haqiqah and majaz], if used with a connection (alaqah) and its meaning is unveiled, it is sarih; otherwise, it is kinayah. (al-Qamar) 'By reflection' i.e. by consideration after bringing to mind its meanings, taking note of context and clues. (al-Qamar) 'Relates to speech' because the manifestation of the intended meaning and understanding it is through speech. (al-Qamar) 'Relates to a word' etc. The indication of a word for one or many meanings, and likewise the usage of a word with unveiling or concealing of its meaning, pertains to a word, just as the manifestation of the intended meaning and understanding it (which occur in the second and fourth divisions) relate to speech. (al-Sunbuli) 'The aforementioned structure' i.e. that which indicates meaning, implying that the definite article in the author's phrase 'al-nazm' is for a known reference. (al-Qamar) 'If it is used' etc. It implies that a word before usage is not called haqiqah or majaz, nor sarih or kinayah. There is another place for detail. (al-Qamar) 'Then each of them' etc. The purpose, as apparent, is that sarih and kinayah apply to both haqiqah and majaz, not as the scholars of balaghah say that kinayah is opposite to majaz. Thus the third division is quadruple, not binary. And the purpose is not to object to the author that sarih and kinayah are subdivisions of haqiqah and majaz and not of the original division, making the division binary. So the author's statement 'they are four' is not misplaced, as is evident upon reflection. (al-Qamar) 'Then it is kinayah': kinayah in the terminology of this discipline (usul) is expression of something by a word that is not explicit. In the terminology of balaghah, it is the usage of a word in its original meaning and transition to its necessary implication or its cause, according to the two views. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar introduces the third division of words based on their usage in meaning. The author defines four categories: haqiqah (literal), majaz (figurative), sarih (explicit), and kinayah (implicit). A word is haqiqah if used in its original meaning as per linguistic convention, and majaz if used in a transferred meaning. Then, regardless of whether a word is haqiqah or majaz, if its meaning is clear and direct, it is sarih; if the meaning is indirectly understood, it is kinayah. The text clarifies a key Hanafi usul point: sarih and kinayah apply to both haqiqah and majaz, contrary to the science of balaghah (rhetoric), where kinayah is considered a subset of majaz only. In Hanafi usul, the classification is quadruple (four categories) not binary, because each of haqiqah and majaz can be either sarih or kinayah. The marginal note by al-Qamar emphasizes that a word is only categorized after usage; before usage, it is simply a word. This division is fundamental for understanding how Hanafi jurists analyze legal texts to derive rulings.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): A word used in its original, conventionally established meaning.
المجاز (al-majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original due to a relational connection (alaqah).
الصريح (al-sarih): A word whose meaning is clear and explicit without need for interpretation.
الكناية (al-kinayah): A word whose meaning is implied indirectly, not explicit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, a word used in its original meaning but whose intended meaning is not immediately clear is called:
A) Haqiqah sarihah
B) Haqiqah kinayah
C) Majaz sarih
D) Majaz kinayah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul classification of word usage is quadruple, combining literal/figurative with explicit/implicit.

The Imperative Does Not Imply Repetition: Proof and Refutation
Linguistic proof of amr's singularity and refutation of repetition objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi linguistic proof that the imperative does not imply repetition and apply the distinction between command and cause to repeated acts of worship.

Core Idea
The imperative commands one act; repetition arises from causes, not the command.

This passage tackles a foundational principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: does a command (amr) require you to perform the act only once, or repeatedly? The Hanafi school argues that the imperative’s linguistic structure restricts it to a single occurrence. But what about obligations like daily prayers? The author answers by distinguishing the command itself from the causes that trigger repeated performance. This lesson walks through the proof and addresses a common objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
The author states the chosen position: the imperative does not imply repetition (tikrar) nor can it admit it.
This is a departure from some other schools that hold amr implies repetition unless specified otherwise.
2
Understand the Linguistic Proof
The imperative is an abbreviation of 'I request from you [act]' where the source infinitive (masdar) is a single instance (fard). Since a single instance cannot denote number, the command cannot imply repetition.
Key phrase: 'لأن صيغة الأمر مختصرة من طلب الفعل بالمصدر الذي هو فرد'
3
Distinguish Types of Oneness
The text clarifies that 'oneness' can be genuine individuality (fardiyya) as in a single strike, or generic individuality (jinsiyya) as in divorce where a single pronouncement can cover multiple acts (e.g., three talāq in one utterance considered as one notional act).
The dual is excluded from both types.
4
Analyze the Objection about Repeated Worship
The objection: if commands do not imply repetition, how are acts like prayer, fasting, and zakat repeated?
5
Grasp the Cause-Command Distinction
The author replies: repetition arises from recurring causes (asbāb), not from the command itself. Prayer is repeated because the time (cause) recurs; fasting because Ramadan (cause) recurs; zakat because nisab (cause) recurs. Hajj is only once because its cause (the House) is singular.
This distinguishes the obligation itself (wujūb) from the obligation of performance (wujūb al-adā').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله
ثم أورد المصنف له دليلاً على ما هو المختار عنده، فقال: لأن صيغة الأمر مختصرة من طلب الفعل بالمصدر الذي هو فرد أي إنما لا يقتضي الأمر التكرار؛ لأنه مختصر من طلب الفعل بالمصدر فقولك: "اضرب" مختصر من "أطلب منك الضرب"، وقوله: "صلوا" مختصر من "أطلب منكم الصلاة"، وقوله: "طلقي" مختصر من "افعلي فعل الطلاق"، والمصدر المختصر منه فرد لا يحتمل العدد وكيف يحتمله.
ومعنى التوحد مرعى في ألفاظ الوحدان فالفعل المختصر منه أولى أن لا يحتمل العدد، جمع الواحد.
وبهذا القدر تمّ الدليل على الأصل الكلي.
ثم قوله: وذلك بالفردية والجنسية، والمثنى بمعزل عنهما، أي التوحيد.
بيان تغيير إلخ قلت ودليله أنهم قالوا: إذا اقترن بالصيغة ذكر العدد لا بالصيغة حتى لو قال لامرأته: "طلقتك ثلاثا أو واحدة وقد ماتت قبل ذكر العدد لم يقع شيء، وليس الفرق بين طلقتك وطلقي إلا بأن المصدر أي طلاقًا ثابت في الأول اقتضاء وفي الثاني لغة كذا في "التلويح" وبعض حواشيه. (السنبلي)
ما هو المختار إلخ وهو أن الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله المحشي بالمصدر إلخ الباء متعلق بالطلب واللام عوض عن المضاف إليه أي مصدر ذلك الأمر، وعمم المصدر ليشمل المعرف والمنكر. (القمر)
أي إنما لا يقتضي إلخ إيماء إلى أن قول المصنف محله: لأن صيغة إلى دليل لأصل الدعوى، وليس دليلاً لقوله ولا تعمل إلخ كما فهمه بعض الشراح، وإلا بقي الدعوى بلا دليل (القمر) أي إنما لا يقتضي إلخ لما كان من ظاهر كلام الماتن أنه دليل لقوله السابق ولا تعمل نية الثنتين إلخ لسبقه متصلا، فدفعه وقال: ليس كذلك بل هو دليل لأصل الدعوى وهو عدم اقتضاء التكرار، وعدم احتماله ولو فهم منه دليله ضمنًا أيضًا. (السنبلي) أطلب منك إلخ المراد منه المعنى الإنشائي لا الخبري، وإلا فالاختصار منه في محل المنع تدبر.(القمر) من
والمصدر المختصر منه فرد إلخ: هذا إيماء إلى أن قول المصنف الذي هو فرد صفة للمصدر. (القمر) منه أي من المصدر الذي هو فرد (القمر) على الأصل الكلي: أي أن الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله. (القمر) بالفردية إلخ بأن يكون اللفظ فردًا حقيقيا، وإما بالجنسية بأن يكون فردا اعتباريًا، والمثنى بمعزل منهما أي بمكان بعيد من الواحد الحقيقي والاعتباري (إفاضة الأنوار ٣٣،٣٢)

بيان للمثال المختص أعني قوله: "طلقي نفسك؛ لأن الطلاق هو الذي يتصف بالجنسية، والفرد الحكمي ومعزلية المثنى وأما ما سواه فلا يعلم فيه الفرد الحكمي إلا في آخر العمر. العبادات فبأسبابها لا بالأوامر، جواب سؤال يرد علينا، وهو أن الأمر إذا لم يقتض التكرار ولم يحتمله، فبأي وجه تتكرر العبادات مثل الصلاة والصيام وغير ذلك، فيقول: إن ما تكرر من العبادات ليس بالأوامر بل بالأسباب؛ لأن تكرار السبب يدل على تكرار المسبب فأيان وجد الوقت وجب الصلاة، ومتى يأتي رمضان يجب الصيام، ومهما قدر على ملك المال وجبت الزكاة، ولهذا لم يجب الحج في العمر إلا مرة؛ لأن البيت واحد لا تكرار فيه. لا يقال: إن الوقت سبب لنفس الوجوب، والأمر إنما هو سبب لوجوب الأداء،
بيان للمثال إلخ: دفع دخل تقريره: أن كل أفراد الجنس لا يعلم إلا بموت الإنسان فاستحال رعاية معنى التوحد بطريق الجنسية فكيف يصح قول المصنف: وذلك بالفردية والجنسية فأجاب بأن قول المصنف هذا ليس عاما لجميع ألفاظ الوحدان بل هو مخصوص بالمثال الخاص أي الطلاق، فإن كل أفراد جنسه يمكن تحققه قبل اختتام العمر وهو ثلاث (السنبلي والفرد الحكمى إيماء إلى أن المراد بالجنسية في المتن: الفرد الحكمي، والمراد بالفردية: الفرد الحقيقي، فالتوحد يكون بالفرد الحكمي والفرد الحقيقي، والطلاق له فرد حقيقي وفرد حكمي وهو المجموع من الثلاث في الحرة والاثنين في الأمة، وأما ما سوى الطلاق كالسرقة والصلاة فلا يعلم فيه الفرد الحكمي أي المجموع إلا في آخر العمر، فإنه ليس له حد معين حتى يجعل الجملة في حكم فرد واحد، فإذا انتهى العمر يعلم الفرد الحكمي أي المجموع (القمر) يرد: أي من جانب القائلين بالتكرار. (القمر) ليس بالأوامر وإلا لاستغرقت العبادات الأوقات كلها لدوام الأمر، واللازم باطل بالإجماع فكذا الملزوم، وأما الملازمة؛ فلأنه ليس في اللفظ إشعار بوقت وليس بعض الأوقات أولى بالتعين من البعض. (القمر) على ملك المال: أي بقدر النصاب الشرعي. (القمر) لأن البيت إلخ وهو سبب الحج بدليل أنه يضاف إليه، فيقال: حج البيت. (القمر) لنفس الوجوب إلخ تفصيله أن لنا خطاب وضع يكون الوقت سببا للوجوب، فثبوت الفعل حقا مؤكدًا على الذمة من هذا الخطاب وهو الوجوب، ولنا خطاب تكليف بالاقتضاء، فطلب الفعل بإيقاعه في هذا الخطاب وهو وجوب الأداء، وثبت من هذا أن لا طلب في الوجوب بل في وجوب الأداء، فليس العين من الوقت الذي هو سبب لنفس الوجوب مغنيا عن الأمر الذي هو سبب لوجوب الأداء بل لابد منه. (القمر)
Section on the Specific. The command does not necessitate repetition nor does it admit it. Then the author adduced a proof for what is his chosen position, saying: Because the imperative form is abbreviated from the request of the act by the infinitive, which is a single instance. That is, the command does not necessitate repetition only because it is abbreviated from the request of the act by the infinitive. So your saying 'strike' is abbreviated from 'I request from you striking', and His saying 'pray' is abbreviated from 'I request from you prayer', and his saying 'divorce' is abbreviated from 'perform the act of divorce'. And the infinitive from which it is abbreviated is a single instance that does not admit number, and how could it admit it? And the meaning of oneness is observed in the words of individuals, so the verb abbreviated from it is even more deserving of not admitting number. With this much the proof for the universal principle is complete. Then his saying: And that is by individuality and genericness, and the dual is remote from both, i.e., unification. Explanation of change etc. I said: And its proof is that they said: If number is connected with the imperative form, not by the imperative form, such that if he said to his wife 'I divorce you thrice' or 'once' and she died before mentioning the number, nothing occurs. And the difference between 'I divorce you' and 'divorce' is only that the infinitive, i.e., divorce, is established in the first by implication and in the second linguistically. Thus in al-Talwih and some marginal notes. (al-Sinbali) etc. The proof: And the infinitive abbreviated from it is a single instance etc.: This indicates that his saying 'which is a single instance' is an attribute of the infinitive. (al-Qamar) etc. Explanation of the specific example, i.e., his saying: 'Divorce yourself'; because divorce is what qualifies by genericness and the notional individual and the remoteness of the dual. As for other than that, the notional individual is not known except at the end of life. Acts of worship are by their causes, not by commands, in answer to an objection raised against us: that if the command does not necessitate repetition nor admit it, how then are acts of worship like prayer, fasting, etc. repeated? He says: What is repeated from acts of worship is not by commands but by causes; because repetition of the cause indicates repetition of the effect. Whenever time exists, prayer becomes obligatory; whenever Ramadan comes, fasting becomes obligatory; whenever one possesses wealth to the extent of the legal nisab, zakat becomes obligatory. For this reason, Hajj is only obligatory once in a lifetime because the House is one, with no repetition. It cannot be said that time is a cause for the very obligation itself, while the command is only a cause for the obligation of performance... etc.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage establishes the Hanafi principle that the imperative form (amr) does not demand repetition (tikrar) nor can it admit it. The author first provides the linguistic proof: the imperative is an abbreviation of 'I request from you [act]' where the source infinitive is a single instance (fard). Since a single act cannot imply number, the command cannot imply repetition. The text then clarifies that 'oneness' is observed either through genuine individuality (fardiyya) or through generic individuality (jinsiyya), and the dual is excluded. Following this, the author addresses a potential objection: if commands do not imply repetition, how do repeated acts of worship (e.g., daily prayers, annual fasting) exist? The answer is that these repetitions arise not from the command itself but from the repeated occurrence of their causes (asbab): time for prayer, Ramadan for fasting, possession of nisab for zakat. Hajj is only once because its cause (the House) is singular. The discussion further refines the distinction between the obligation itself (wujud) and the obligation of performance (ada'), emphasizing that the cause provides the former but the command still provides the latter. This is a key Hanafi usul point that separates the form of the command from the factors that make an action repeatedly required.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): The imperative form, which is abbreviated from 'request of the act' using a source infinitive that indicates a single instance.
فرد (fard): A single instance or individual; the source infinitive is considered a 'fard' meaning one occurrence.
جنسية (jinsiyya): Genericness or conceptual individuality; a notional single instance that encompasses multiple potential acts (e.g., the three divorces in a single pronouncement considered as one notional act).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument, why does the imperative form 'do X' not imply repetition?
A) Because the verb is conjugated in the singular.
B) Because the command is abbreviated from a source infinitive which is a single instance.
C) Because repetition is specified by context.
D) Because commands are always understood as once only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the imperative form commands a single act; repetition of obligations is due to recurring causes, not the command itself.

Types of Qada: Rational and Non-Rational Counterparts
Explains the two types of qada in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational and non-rational counterparts in qada and apply the Hanafi principle to determine the validity of compensation for missed obligations.

Core Idea
Qada can be performed with a counterpart that is rationally similar or one deemed equivalent by Shari'ah alone.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the obligation remains on liability until performed or discharged. To compensate for missed obligations, the school identifies two types of counterparts: rational (مثل معقول) where similarity is perceived by intellect, and non-rational (مثل غير معقول) where similarity is established only by divine law. This distinction is crucial for rulings like fidya for fasting. In this lesson, we explore the definitions, evidence, and the inter-madhhab debate with the Shafi'is.

Guided Path
1
Define qada and its necessity
Understand that missed obligations remain on the liability until performed or discharged by the right-holder. This is the basis for requiring qada.
Qada is distinct from ada (performance within time).
2
Identify the rational counterpart
Recognize that when the compensator is similar in form and wisdom, the intellect perceives the similarity without need for Shari'ah. Example: fasting for a missed fast.
This is the default type of qada.
3
Identify the non-rational counterpart
Recognize that when the compensator is not similar in form, but Shari'ah declares equivalence, it is a non-rational counterpart. Example: fidya for fasting.
The intellect does not perceive similarity but also does not contradict the Shari'ah ruling.
4
Examine the Hanafi proof for non-rational counterpart
The intellect is one of the proofs of Shari'ah and cannot contradict it. Thus Shari'ah can legislate equivalence between acts that differ in form. The Quranic verse (2:184) is interpreted with an implicit 'la' to support fidya for the permanently unable.
The consensus of the Companions also supports this ruling.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Most Shafi'is reject qada with non-rational counterpart, requiring that the compensator be rationally similar. The practical difference: fidya is not valid qada in the Shafi'i school.
The disagreement is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul principles.

وهذه الفدية لكل يوم هو نصف صاع من بُرِّ، أو دقيقة، أو سويقة، أو زبيب، أو صاع من تمر، أو شعير للشيخ الفاني الذي يعجز عن الصوم؛ لأجل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِين﴾ على أن تكون كلمة "لا" مقدرة أي لا يطيقونه، أو تكون الهمزة فيه للسلب أي يسلبون الطاقة ليدل على الشيخ الفاني، وأما إذا حملت على ظاهرها فهي منسوخة على ما قيل: إن في بدء الإسلام كان المطيق مخيرًا بين أن يصوم
نصف صاع إلخ الصاع ما يسع خمسة أرطال أو ثلاثا برطل المدينة وهو ثلثون أستارا، والأستار ستة دراهم ونصف، فإذا ضربنا ستة ونصفا في مائة وستين كان الحاصل ألفا وأربعين درهما كذا قال الطحطاوي. (القمر) للشيخ الفاني إلخ إنما سمي به لفناء قوته وقدره القهستاني حيث قال: وهو من جاوز الخمسين، والأصح عدم التقدير والمدار على العجز وإليه أشار الشارح بقوله : الذي يعجز إلخ، فالفدية في حقه قائمة مقام الصوم ليحصل بأدائها ثواب كثواب الصوم كما أقيم التراب مقام الماء ليحصل باستعماله طهارة كطهارة الماء. (القمر) فدية طعام مسكين إلخ قلت ونظير تقدير لا في القرآن قوله تعالى: يُبَيِّنُ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ أَنْ تَضِلُّوا أَي أَن لا تضلوا، قال الإمام الزاهدي: هذا التأويل غير صحيح؛ لأنه تعالى قال : وأَن تَصُومُوا خَيْرٌ لَكُمْ ومثل هذا الندب لا يرد في حق العاجز . (السنبلي على أن تكون إلخ تطبيق الدليل على الفرع.(القمر) مقدرة وهذا كما في قوله تعالى: يُبَيِّنُ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ أَنْ تَضِلُّوا أي لأن لا تضلوا، وفي قوله تعالى: وَأَلْقَى فِي الْأَرْضِ رَوَاسِيَ أي جبالاً أن تميد أي لأن لا تميد بكم، ومثله كثير. (القمر) أو تكون إلخ معطوف على تكون، ثم اعلم أنه قال السيد طفيل أحمد البلحرامي له: إن همزة السلب في الأفعال سماعية لا قياسية، وليس في لغة أن همزة الإطاقة للسلب إلا أنه قال به شمس الأئمة كذا في "سجة المرجان". (القمر)
ليدل إلخ أي إنما اخترنا أحد التأويلين وهما تقدير كلمة "لا" وكون الهمزة للسلب ليدل إلخ. (القمر) فهي منسوخة: وحينئذ فوجوب الفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني بإجماع الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين. (القمر) فهي منسوحة إلخ : على هذا معنى الآية، وعلى المطيقين الذين لا عذر لهم أن افطروا فدية، وكان الأغنياء يفطرون ويفدون، ثم نسخ ذلك بقوله تعالى: فَمَنْ شَهِدَ مِنْكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ ويؤيده ما روي عن ابن عمر وسلمة الأكوع أنها منسوخة، وقرأ بعضهم: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ ﴾، وجعل "أن تصوموا لكم" معطوفًا على الكلام الأول؛ وهو قوله تعالى: كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ والخير بمعنى البر لا الأخير ويمكن أن يكون معطوفا على قوله : لا يطيقونه، فيكون معناه لا يطيقونه بحسب الظاهر بطريق خير بمعنى اليسر وإن كان ممكنا على طريق العسر، فحينئذٍ الصيام خير لهم، فيكون وجوب الفدية بالنص. (السنبلي)
Section on the Specific. Explanation of the types of qada (compensation) and what is in the meaning of performance: that it be contrary to it. The meaning of rational counterpart is: that its similarity is perceived by the intellect without consideration of the Sharia, and the non-rational counterpart is: that similarity is perceived only by the Sharia, and the intellect is incapable of grasping its modality, not that the intellect contradicts it. This qada necessarily requires a new cause by agreement, and the disagreement is only about qada with a rational counterpart, like fasting for fasting. This is an example of qada with a rational counterpart: i.e., qada of fasting for fasting, for it is rational because the obligation does not fall from the liability except by performance or by the right-holder relinquishing it, and as long as neither has occurred, it remains on his liability. Other than the cause of performance. And fidya (ransom) for it: this is an example of qada with a non-rational counterpart, for the intellect does not perceive the fidya in exchange for fasting because there is no similarity between them in form; it is apparent and without meaning because fasting is starving the self, and fidya is feeding. Fasting and fidya. That it be contrary to it: i.e., it contains the meaning of performance. (Al-Qamar) And the meaning of rational counterpart etc.: clarification of the intended meaning of counterpart: a thing similar to the obligation in the wisdom and objective of the Lawgiver. If they are identical in type, the intellect perceives the similarity before the coming of the Sharia because the principle regarding things identical in type is that they do not differ in wisdom and the Lawgiver's objective. The ruling differs for things identical in type only due to an incidental factor. If they are not identical in type, the intellect does not judge similarity in wisdom for things differing in type, so similarity is perceived only by the Sharia. The first is the rational counterpart, and the second is the non-rational counterpart. (Al-Qamar) Not that the intellect etc.: i.e., it is not intended that the non-rational counterpart means that the intellect negates similarity and decisively judges that it is not a counterpart to the obligation in wisdom and the Lawgiver's objective because the intellect is one of the proofs of the Sharia, and the Sharia proofs do not contradict each other. Thus the intellect permits the Sharia to make two differing things identical in wisdom. (Al-Qamar) And this qada: i.e., qada with a non-rational counterpart. (Al-Qamar) And the disagreement is only between us and most of Imam al-Shafi'i's followers. (Al-Qamar) Like qada etc.: an indication that the genitive construct in the author's speech is deleted to make the example correct. (Al-Qamar) And fidya for it: fidya is the substitute by which one frees himself from an undesirable consequence directed at him. (Al-Qamar) Starving the self: hunger is more general than hunger of the private parts and hunger of the stomach, and it is also more general than the usual hunger and thirst. (Al-Qamar) And fidya: also fasting involves tiring the self by restraint, and fidya involves decreasing wealth. (Al-Muhashi) And this fidya for each day is half a sa' of wheat, or its flour, or its groats, or raisins, or a sa' of dates or barley for the decrepit old man who is unable to fast, due to the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And upon those who are able to do so, a fidya of feeding a poor person.' On the condition that the word 'la' (not) is implicit, i.e., they are not able to do so, or the hamza is for negation, i.e., they are deprived of ability, to indicate the decrepit old man. If it is interpreted according to its apparent meaning, it is abrogated as it is said that at the beginning of Islam, the able person had a choice between fasting or paying fidya. Half a sa' etc.: the sa' is a measure that holds five ratls or three ratls of the Madinan ratl, which is thirty ustars; the ustarl is six and a half dirhams. If we multiply six and a half by one hundred and sixty, the result is one thousand and forty dirhams. Thus said al-Tahtawi. (Al-Qamar) For the decrepit old man etc.: he is called so due to the extinction of his strength and power. Al-Qahistani said: he is one who has passed fifty, but the correct view is that there is no age limit and the criterion is inability. The commentator indicated this by his saying: 'who is unable.' So fidya in his case stands in place of fasting, so that its performance yields reward like the reward of fasting, just as earth is made a substitute for water to attain purification similar to purification with water. (Al-Qamar) Fidya: feeding a poor person etc.: I say, a parallel to the implicit 'la' in the Quran is the saying of Allah: 'Allah makes clear to you lest you go astray' i.e., so that you do not go astray. Imam al-Zahidi said: this interpretation is incorrect because He said: 'And that you fast is better for you,' and such a recommendation is not given in the case of the incapable. (Al-Sunbali) 'On the condition that it is implicit' etc.: application of the proof to the branch. (Al-Qamar) Implicit, and this is like His saying: 'Allah makes clear to you that you should not go astray' i.e., so that you do not go astray, and His saying: 'And He cast into the earth firm mountains' i.e., mountains, lest it should sway with you, i.e., so that it does not sway with you. There are many such examples. (Al-Qamar) 'Or it is' etc.: connected to 'it is'. Then know that Sayyid Tufayl Ahmad al-Balhram said: the hamza of negation in verbs is auditive, not analogical, and in the language there is no such use of the hamza for 'ability' for negation, except that Shams al-A'imma held it. Thus in 'Sajj al-Marjan'. (Al-Qamar) 'To indicate' etc.: i.e., we have chosen one of the two interpretations, namely the implicitness of the word 'la' and the hamza being for negation, to indicate. (Al-Qamar) 'It is abrogated': then the obligation of fidya for the decrepit old man is by consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them all. (Al-Qamar) 'It is abrogated' etc.: according to this the meaning of the verse is: 'And upon those who are able but have no excuse, they may break the fast and pay fidya.' The rich would break the fast and pay fidya. Then that was abrogated by His saying: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' This is supported by what was narrated from Ibn 'Umar and Salama al-Akwa' that it is abrogated. Some read: 'And upon those who are able to do so,' and made 'that you fast' connected to the first statement, which is His saying: 'Fasting is prescribed for you,' and 'khair' (better) means virtue, not the comparative. It is possible that it is connected to His saying: 'they are not able to do so,' so the meaning would be: they are not able to do so apparently in the way of ease, though possible in the way of hardship, and then fasting is better for them, so the obligation of fidya is by the text. (Al-Sunbali)
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary from a Hanafi usul work, discussing the classification of qada (compensatory performance) in relation to missed obligations. It distinguishes between two types of counterparts: rational (مثل معقول) and non-rational (مثل غير معقول). A rational counterpart is one where the intellect perceives the similarity between the original obligation and the compensator without needing Shari'ah to declare it, such as fasting in place of a missed fast. The reasoning is that the obligation remains on the liability until it is performed or discharged by the right-holder. A non-rational counterpart is one where the Shari'ah declares the similarity even though the intellect does not perceive it, such as fidya (ransom) in place of a missed fast. Fidya involves feeding others, which is not rationally similar to fasting (self-deprivation). The Hanafi school holds that qada with a non-rational counterpart is valid, and it requires a new cause (e.g., inability to fast) by agreement. The disagreement with most Shafi'is is over this type of qada. The passage also discusses the specific ruling of fidya for a decrepit old person who cannot fast, based on the Quranic verse (2:184). It explains that the verse is interpreted with an implicit 'la' (not) or with a negating hamza to refer to those who are unable. This fidya is half a sa' of various staple foods per day. The obligation of fidya for such a person is established by the consensus of the Companions. The verse is considered abrogated for the able person, but remains in effect for the permanently incapable. This shows how usul principles (definition of 'customary', 'rational' vs 'legal' equivalence) are applied to derive rulings.

Key Terms
المثل المعقول (al-mithl al-ma'qul): The counterpart that is rationally perceived as similar to the original obligation before considering Shari'ah.
المثل غير المعقول (al-mithl ghayr al-ma'qul): The counterpart whose similarity to the original is established only by Shari'ah, not by reason.
القضاء (al-qada'): Compensatory performance of a missed obligatory act after its time has passed.
الفدية (al-fidya): A substitute payment (food) given in place of fasting by those permanently unable to fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is an example of qada with a 'non-rational counterpart'?
A) Performing a missed prayer by praying the same prayer
B) Giving fidya for a missed fast
C) Fasting for a missed fast
D) Repeating a missed pilgrimage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows qada with a non-rational counterpart (like fidya for fasting) based on the principle that the Shari'ah can establish equivalence even when the intellect does not perceive it.

The Necessity of Goodness in the Commanded Act
Explains necessity of goodness in the commanded act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three meanings of al-husn and al-qubh, and identify the Hanafi position on whether moral good and evil are determined by reason or revelation.

Core Idea
Moral good (husn) in the third sense is known only through divine command, not independent reason.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar establishes that the commanded act (al-ma'mur bihi) must inherently possess goodness because the Commander (Allah) is wise. It introduces a key distinction between three meanings of 'good' and 'evil,' and contrasts the Hanafi/Ash'ari view with the Mu'tazila on the role of reason in determining morality. This foundation is essential for understanding how legal obligations are derived in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of al-husn
List the three senses: natural agreeableness (e.g., joy), perfection (e.g., knowledge), and praiseworthiness (moral good, e.g., worship).
The first two are universally agreed to be known by reason.
2
Recognize the point of dispute
The dispute concerns the third sense: who determines what is morally good and evil?
Mu'tazila say reason; Hanafi/Ash'ari say Allah through revelation.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof
The Commander is wise, so He would not command evil. However, we only know the goodness of an act through the command itself.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) that the command creates the moral quality for us.
4
Contrast with the Mu'tazila view
The Mu'tazila hold that reason can independently determine moral good and evil; revelation then conforms to reason.
This leads to differences in whether obligations can be known before revelation.
5
Grasp the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Without revelation, the Hanafi says no act is obligatory or prohibited; the Mu'tazila says some acts (e.g., justice, lying) are inherently obligatory or prohibited.
This affects the scope of taklif before the coming of prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان حسن المأمور به]
ثم لما فرغ المصنف له عن بيان أنواع الأداء والقضاء شرع في بيان حسن المأمور به، فقال: ولابد للمأمور به من صفة الحسن ضرورة أن الأمر حكيم يعني لابد أن يكون المأمور وهو الله تعالى شانه به حسنًا عند الله تعالى قبل الأمر، ولكن تعرف ذلك بالأمر ضرورة أن الأمر حكيم، 
وإنما تصير إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن منافع البضع تكون متقومة عند التفريق والإزالة في الخلع إذا افتدت المرأة، وخلصتها من الزوج؛ والعائد في "تصير" يرجع إلى منافع البضع (القمر)
بالنص: هو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا فِيمَا افْتَدَتْ بهِ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٢٩) (المحشي
أو طاوعت أي مكنت وزنت فإن المزني بها تحرم على أباء الزاني كذا في "مجمع البركات". (القمر) فحينئذ أي حين الارتداد ومطاوعة ابن الزوج (القمر) يبطل إلخ كذا في "الهداية" في كتاب الرجوع عن الشهادة. (القمر) أخذا إلخ فكان الشاهدان غاصبين نصف المهر معنى، فلا يتوجه أن يقال: إن تضمين الشهود الراجعين نصف المهر في الطلاق قبل الدخول يدل على أن ملك النكاح متقوم. (القمر) 
ولا بد للمأمور به إلخ هذا من قضايا الشرع، وأما من حيث اللغة فقول القائل: "اشرب حمراً" على سبيل الإلزام أمر (القمر) من صفة الحسن: أي من صفة هي الحسن، اعلم أن الحسن و القبح يطلق على ثلاث معان: على ملائم الطبع ومنافره كالفرح والغم وعلى صفة كمال وصفة نقصان كالعلم والجهل، وعلى متعلق المدح والذم كالعبادة والمعصية ولا خلاف أنهما بالمعنيين الأولين عقليان وأما بالثالث فعند المعتزلة الحاكم بالحسن والقبح هو العقل، وعندنا هو الله . [ إفاضة الأنوار ٤٥] ذلك أي كون المأمور به حسنًا. (القمر)
Explanation of the goodness of the commanded act. Then, after the author finished explaining the types of performance and judgment, he began explaining the goodness of the commanded act, saying: The commanded act must have the attribute of goodness, necessarily because the Commander is wise. That is, it must be that the commanded (and He is Allah, exalted is His status) is good in the sight of Allah before the command, but [we] know this through the command, necessarily because the Commander is wise. And explanation of the phrase 'and it becomes [valuable] in khul' by text contrary to analogy' is a refutation of a presumed objection: that the benefits of the sexual organ become valuable at separation and removal in khul' when the woman ransoms herself and frees herself from the husband. The referent of 'becomes' refers to benefits. By the text: He said: 'There is no sin upon them in what she ransoms herself with' (2:229). Or if she committed adultery, i.e., permitted and committed adultery, then the woman who commits adultery becomes forbidden to the father of the adulterer, as in 'Majma' al-Barakat'. Then, i.e., at the time of apostasy and the woman's submission to the husband's son. It becomes void etc. This is in 'al-Hidayah' in the book of retraction from testimony. They took etc. So the two witnesses were effectively usurpers of half the mahr. So it cannot be said that guaranteeing the recanting witnesses half the mahr in divorce before consummation indicates that the right of marriage is valuable. And the statement 'the commanded act must have the attribute of goodness' is from matters of Shari'ah; as for language, the saying of one: 'Drink red wine' as a binding command is a command. From the attribute of goodness: i.e., from an attribute that is goodness. Know that 'al-husn' and 'al-qubh' are used in three senses: conforming to nature and its opposite, like joy and grief; on the attribute of perfection and deficiency, like knowledge and ignorance; and on the object of praise and blame, like worship and sin. There is no disagreement that they are intellectual in the first two senses. As for the third, according to the Mu'tazila, the arbiter of good and evil is the intellect, while according to us it is Allah. [Ifadat al-Anwar 45]. That is, the goodness of the commanded act.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage introduces the usul principle that the commanded act (al-ma'mur bihi) must inherently possess the attribute of goodness (husn). The proof is that the Commander (Allah) is wise, so He would not command evil. However, we know the goodness of an act only through the command itself. The commentary distinguishes between three meanings of 'husn' (good) and 'qubh' (evil): (1) agreeable or repugnant to nature (e.g., joy and grief), (2) perfection or deficiency (e.g., knowledge and ignorance), and (3) praiseworthy or blameworthy (e.g., worship and sin). In the first two senses, good and evil are known by reason; in the third sense, the Hanafi-Ash'ari position is that Allah determines what is good and evil, in contrast to the Mu'tazila who hold that reason can independently determine morality. This is a foundational distinction in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): An attribute of an act; in this context, it has three meanings: natural agreeableness, perfection, and praiseworthiness (the third being moral good).
المأمور به (al-ma'mur bihi): That which is commanded by Allah; the commanded act.
القبح (al-qubh): Evil; has three corresponding meanings: disagreeability, deficiency, and blameworthiness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, how is the goodness of a commanded act known?
A) By independent human reason.
B) By the command itself because the Commander is wise.
C) By consensus of the community.
D) By analogy with natural inclinations.

Key Takeaway
Moral good (husn) in the sense of praiseworthiness is known only through divine command, not through independent reason, establishing the primacy of revelation in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Types of حسن (Goodness) in Commanded Acts
Explaining the three categories of حسن in commanded acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three categories of ḥusn (intrinsic, attached, and for another) and apply them to classify a commanded act, while recognizing the methodological tolerances in the matn.

Core Idea
Not all commanded acts are good in themselves; some are good only because they serve a higher purpose.

In this lesson, we refine our understanding of how commanded acts acquire goodness (ḥusn). The matn presents a tripartite classification, but the commentary reveals subtle tolerances and deeper nuances. By analyzing the categories and the critical evaluation of examples, you will develop precision in using these usul terms and appreciate the Hanafi emphasis on rigorous conceptual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of ḥusn
List the three categories: ḥusn li-maʿnā fī nafsihi (good in itself), mulḥaq bihi (attached to intrinsic good), and ḥusn li-ghayrihi (good for another). Note the examples given: tasdiq and ṣalāh for the first; zakāh, ṣawm, and ḥajj for the second; wuḍūʾ and jihād for the third.
These categories are used to classify the commanded acts in terms of their source of goodness.
2
Understand the 'three tolerances' (musāmaḥāt)
The commentator points out three tolerances in the matn's formulation: (1) expansion in the definition, (2) treating 'attached' as a separate category, (3) omitting the restriction 'for another' in the third category. Grasp how each tolerance affects the precision of the classification.
Critical refinement aims to tighten the definitional boundaries.
3
Analyze the examples for each category
Examine how wuḍūʾ, jihād, and ṣalāt al-janāzah are used as examples of ḥusn li-ghayrihi. Note the commentator's critique: wuḍūʾ's other (prayer) is not achieved directly; jihād's other is achieved directly; funeral prayer has a dual nature (praise of Allah is intrinsic, supplication is for another).
These examples illustrate the practical application of the categories.
4
Evaluate the controversies and clarifications
Consider the objections: wuḍūʾ can be recommended for itself (e.g., for the sermon); jihād involves apparent harm that must be justified by its end; funeral prayer resembles idolatry but is justified by the right of the Muslim. Understand how the Hanafi school resolves these issues using ends-based reasoning.
This step develops critical thinking about the classification.
5
Apply the classification to a new act
Given a new commanded act (e.g., saʿy to Friday prayer), determine its category using the principles from the text. Justify your classification with reference to the structure of ḥusn.
Practice consolidates learning.

بيان أنواع الحسن
جامعًا فينبغي أن يقول: بعد ما كان حسنًا لمعنى في نفسه، أو ملحقا به، أو لغيره حتى يكون المعنى أن المأمور به بعد ما كان حسنًا لمعنى في نفسه كالتصديق والصلاة أو ملحقا به كالزكاة والصوم والحج أو لغيره كالوضوء والجهاد صار حسنًا لمعنى آخر، وهو كونه مشروطا بالقدرة، فلهذا القدرة صارت أوامر الشرع كلها حسنًا للغير، ولكن الحسن لمعنى في نفسه، والملحق به صار جامعًا؛ لكونه لعينه ولغيره، ولهذا قيده بهما، بخلاف ما كان لغيره، فإنه اجتمع فيه الحسن لغيره من جهتين لأجل الغير المعين، ولأجل القدرة فلا يخرج عن كونه لغيره، ولعله لهذا لم يقيده به. ثم بعد هذه المسامحات الثلاثة قد تسامح في أمثلته حيث قال: كالوضوء، والجهاد، والقدرة التي يتمكن بها العبد من أداء ما لزمه، فالوضوء مثال للمأمور به الذي لا يتأدى الغير بأدائه،
أي الواسطة
فإنه في نفسه تبريد وتنظيف للأعضاء، وإضاعة للماء، وإنما حسن لأجل أداء الصلاة

بيان أنواع الحسن والصلاة مما لا يتأدى بنفس فعل الوضوء، بل لا بد لها من فعل آخر قصدًا توجد به
الصلاة، وإذا نوى في هذا الوضوء كان منويًا، وقربة مقصودة يثاب عليها.
والجهاد مثال للمأمور به الذي يتأدى الغير بأدائه، فإنه في نفسه تعذيب عباد الله، وتخريب بلاد الله، وإنما حسن لأجل إعلاء كلمة الله يحصل بمجرد فعل الجهاد لا بفعل آخر بعده. وكذلك إقامة الحدود في نفسها تعذيب، وإنما حسن لزجر الناس من المعاصي، والزجر يحصل بمجرد إقامة الحدود لا بفعل آخر بعده. وكذلك صلاة الجنازة في نفسها بدعة مشابهة لعبادة الأصنام، وإنما حسنت لأجل قضاء حق المسلم، وهو يحصل بمجرد صلاة الجنازة لا بفعل بعدها، فهذه الوسائط
قضاء حق المسلم
نعم
التعذيب
فعل آخر أي الركوع والقيام والسجود وغيرها. (المحشي) كان منويًا إلخ إلا أن الصلاة تستغني عن النية في الوضوء حتى يصح الوضوء بغير نية في حق جواز الصلاة فمن هذه الحيثية ليس الوضوء قربة مقصودة، وحسنة لغيره وهو الصلاة أفاد بحر العلوم (أي) مولانا عبد العلي ليه أن في التمثيل بالوضوء شائبة من الخفاء، فإن الوضوء بما هو طهارة حسن وإن كان له حسن آخر من جهة مشروطه أي الصلاة كيف والدوام على الوضوء مندوب شرعًا، وليس لإقامة الصلاة فإن أوقات مندوبية الطهارة وقت الخطبة وسائر الأوقات المكروهة، والأصلح في التمثيل السعي إلى الجمعة، فإنه إنما حسن لأجل صلاة الجمعة فتدبر. (القمر) تعذيب عباد الله إلخ : هذا مسلم لكن لا نسلم قبح هذا التعذيب لم لا يجوز أن يكون حسنًا لذاته الذي هو غير الجهاد لا حسن فيه فتدبر. (القمر) لأجل إعلاء كلمة الله إلخ أي الجهاد ليس حسنًا بنفسه، قال عليه "الآدمي بنيان الرب ملعون من هدم بنيانه، وإنما حسن لأجل إعلاء كلمة الله قال في "التلويح": قال فخر الإسلام إنهما أي الجهاد وصلاة الجنازة إنما صارا حسنين لمعنى كفر الكافر، وإسلام الميت، وذلك معنى منفصل عن الجهاد والصلاة. (السنبلي) مشابهة إلخ لحضور الميت الذي هو كالحجر بين يدي المصلين.(القمر) لأجل قضاء حق المسلم إلخ : اعلم أولاً أن صلاة الجنازة تشتمل على أمرين: ثناء الله تعالى وهو حسن لعينه، ودعاء الميت وهو حسن لواسطة قضاء حق المسلم، فتسميته صلاة الجنازة حسنة لغيرها بالنظر إلى جزء معناها كذا قال أعظم العلماء (أي مولانا عبد السلام ، وثانيا : أنه إنما قيد بالإسلام؛ لأن الميت لو لم يكن مسلما كانت الصلاة عليه قبيحة منهية عنها؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تُصَلِّ عَلَى أَحَدٍ مِنْهُمْ مَاتَ أَبَدا (التوبة: ٨٤) (القمر)
Section on al-Khas (the specific)
131
Explanation of the types of goodness (husn)
Comprehensively, it should say: after it was good for a meaning in itself, or attached to it, or for other than it, such that the meaning is that the commanded act, after it was good for a meaning in itself like tasdiq (affirmation) and prayer, or attached to it like zakat, fasting, and hajj, or for other than it like wudu and jihad, became good for another meaning, which is its being conditioned by ability. For this ability, all the commands of the Shari'ah became good for other than themselves. But the good for a meaning in itself and the attached to it became comprehensive because they are for themselves and for other than themselves; this is why the author restricted it to these two, unlike that which is for other than it, for in it the good for other than itself is gathered from two aspects: for the specific other and for ability; so it does not leave its being for other than itself, and perhaps for this reason he did not restrict it to it. Then after these three tolerances, he tolerated in his examples where he said: like wudu, jihad, and the ability by which the servant is able to perform what is obligatory upon him. Wudu is an example of the commanded act whose other is not performed by its performance (i.e., the intermediary). For in itself it is cooling and cleaning of the limbs and wasting water; it is only good for the sake of performing prayer (like other coolings). He restricted it to these two, i.e., the author restricted his statement 'or it is good for a good in its condition' to the good for a meaning in itself and the attached to it. (al-Qamar) The specific other: i.e., the other specifically related to the commanded act, like prayer for wudu, and raising the word of Allah for jihad, and analogize on this. (al-Qamar) And for ability: and it is not shared among the five categories. (al-Qamar) He did not restrict it to it, i.e., the author did not restrict his statement 'or it is good for a good in its condition' to the good for other than it. (al-Qamar) These tolerances etc.: the first is the tolerance of expansion, the second is the tolerance of making it a category, and the third is the tolerance of omitting the restriction of 'for other than it'. (al-Qamar) He tolerated in his examples because wudu and jihad are examples of the commanded act that became good by the goodness of the other, and ability is an example of the other by which the commanded act became good, and it is not an example of the commanded act. The statement that the addendum is omitted and the meaning is 'and conditioned by ability' etc. is not free from affectation. (al-Qamar) For the sake of performing prayer etc.: wudu is not an intended act of worship because it falls away when prayer falls; so it does not need intention in its being a means to prayer, because prayer only needs wudu in consideration of its being purification, not in consideration of its description of being worship; what needs intention is this description, i.e., its being worship. Similarly, sa'y (walking) is commanded by His statement 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah' (al-Jumu'ah:9) which is walking and moving feet, and in itself it is not good; it is only good for establishing the Friday prayer which is performed by another act after it. (al-Sanbali)

[PAGE_133]
Section on al-Khas
132
Explanation of the types of goodness: and prayer does not come about by the very act of wudu; rather, it necessarily has another act (the intentional performance) by which prayer exists. And if one intends in this wudu, it becomes intended and an intended act of worship for which reward is given.
Jihad is an example of the commanded act whose other is performed in its performance, for in itself it is torturing the servants of Allah and ruining the lands of Allah; it is only good for raising the word of Allah, which is achieved by the mere act of jihad, not by another act after it. Similarly, the establishment of prescribed punishments is in itself torture; it is only good for deterring people from sins, and deterrence is achieved by the mere establishment of the punishment, not by another act after it. Similarly, the funeral prayer is in itself an innovation resembling idol worship; it is only good for fulfilling the right of the Muslim, which is achieved by the mere funeral prayer, not by an act after it. These means... (the right of the Muslim, yes, the torture). Another act: i.e., bowing, standing, prostrating, etc. (al-Muhashi) 'It becomes intended' etc., except that prayer dispenses with intention in wudu so that wudu is valid without intention regarding the permissibility of prayer; from this aspect, wudu is not an intended act of worship and is good for other than itself, namely prayer. Bahr al-Ulum (i.e., our master Abd al-Ali) stated that in representing with wudu there is a hint of obscurity, because wudu as purification is good, even if it has another good from the aspect of its condition (i.e., prayer); how can it be that continuous wudu is recommended by Shari'ah not for establishing prayer? For the recommended times of purification are the time of the sermon and all other disliked times. The better example is hastening to the Friday prayer, for it is only good for the sake of Friday prayer, so reflect. (al-Qamar) Torturing the servants of Allah etc.: This is conceded, but we do not concede the ugliness of this torture; why might it not be good in itself? That which is not jihad has no good in it, so reflect. (al-Qamar) For raising the word of Allah etc.: i.e., jihad is not good in itself. He (peace be upon him) said: 'The human is the building of the Lord; cursed is the one who destroys His building.' It is only good for raising the word of Allah. He said in al-Talwih: Fakhr al-Islam said that they (i.e., jihad and funeral prayer) become good for the meaning of disbelief of the disbeliever and the Islam of the deceased, and that is a meaning separate from jihad and prayer. (al-Sanbali) 'Resembling' etc.: due to the presence of the deceased who is like a stone before the worshippers. (al-Qamar) For fulfilling the right of the Muslim etc.: Know firstly that funeral prayer comprises two matters: praise of Allah, which is good in itself, and supplication for the deceased, which is good due to the right of the Muslim. So calling the funeral prayer good for other than itself is with consideration of part of its meaning. Thus said the greatest scholars (i.e., our master Abd al-Salam). And secondly, it is restricted to Islam because if the deceased were not a Muslim, prayer over him would be ugly and prohibited by His statement: 'And do not ever pray over any of them who dies' (al-Tawbah:84) (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the matn's classification of 'husn' (goodness/beauty) in commanded acts. The commentator (likely from the Noor al-Anwar tradition) explains that the author of the matn attempted to provide a comprehensive definition but made several 'tolerances' (musamahat). The three categories are: (1) husn li-ma'na fi nafsihi (good for a meaning in itself) – acts like faith and prayer; (2) mulhaq bihi (attached to the intrinsic) – acts like zakat, fasting, and hajj, which are good in themselves but also serve other ends; (3) husn li-ghayrihi (good for another's sake) – acts like wudu and jihad, which are not good in themselves but become good because they enable or cause something else (e.g., prayer or raising Allah's word). The commentator notes that the author restricted the first two categories because they have both self-good and other-good, but the third category remains purely other-good and thus was not further restricted. The discussion then examines the examples: wudu is a commanded act whose 'other' (prayer) is not achieved solely by its performance; rather, prayer requires additional acts (like intention and the actual prayer). Jihad is the opposite: its 'other' (raising Allah's word) is achieved directly by the act itself. The commentator also addresses subtle points: wudu is not an intended act of worship (qurbah maqsudah) because it is merely a means; it does not require intention for its validity as purification, though intention can make it a worship. He criticizes the example of wudu, suggesting that 'hastening to Friday prayer' (sa'y) would be a clearer example of good-for-another. For jihad, the discussion involves reconciling its apparent harm (torture, destruction) with its being good: it is not good in itself but becomes good for the sake of raising Allah's word. This aligns with the Hanafi usul principle that actions are evaluated by their ends. The passage also touches on funeral prayer, which is good due to fulfilling the right of a Muslim, though its form resembles idolatry. Overall, this is a dense commentary that refines the categorization and examples, reflecting the Hanafi method of rigorous conceptual analysis.

Key Terms
حسن لمعنى في نفسه (ḥusn li-maʿnā fī nafsihi): Goodness inherent in the act itself, independent of external factors (e.g., tasdiq, prayer).
ملحق به (mulḥaq bihi): Goodness attached to an intrinsic good; acts that are good in themselves but also serve a purpose beyond themselves (e.g., zakat, fasting, hajj).
حسن لغيره (ḥusn li-ghayrihi): Goodness derived from the act's being a means to another good end (e.g., wudu for prayer, jihad for raising Allah's word).
مسامحة (musāmaḥah): A tolerance or leniency in conceptual classification; here, three such tolerances in the matn's formulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an example of 'ḥusn li-ghayrihi' according to the passage?
A) Wudu (wuḍūʾ)
B) Jihad (jihād)
C) Funeral prayer (ṣalāt al-janāzah)
D) Zakat (zakāh)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between intrinsic and instrumental goodness is essential for understanding the rationale behind many commanded acts and for precise legal reasoning.

Divisions of Legal Ugliness: Inseparable vs Separable External Ugliness
Definition-expansion of qabeeh li ghayrihi with three fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qabeeh li ghayrihi lazim and mujawir, and apply each category to determine the fiqh ruling (fasid vs makruh, binding upon commencement, necessity of qada).

Core Idea
External ugliness can be inseparable (corrupts the act) or separable (renders it disliked but still binding).

This passage from Noor al-Anwar sharpens a critical usul distinction: not all acts prohibited due to an external factor are equally void. Whether the external ugliness is part of the act's definition (lazim) or merely a neighbor (mujawir) determines if the act becomes fasid (corrupt) or remains valid but makruh (disliked). The author illustrates this with three fiqh examples—fasting on the Day of Nahr, prayer at disliked times, and sale during the Friday call—training you to see how definitional boundaries control legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal concept
The passage defines 'qabeeh li ghayrihi' (ugly due to another) and splits it into two subcategories: lazim (inseparable) and mujawir (separable). The distinction hinges on whether the external factor (e.g., time) is part of the act's definition.
This is a definition-expansion probe: the author takes a known term and refines it.
2
Analyze the first example: fasting on Day of Nahr
Time (the day) is part of fasting's definition (dawn to dusk). The external ugliness (turning away from hospitality) attaches inseparably, so the fast is fasid and not binding upon commencement; no qada is required.
Contrast with a vowed fast: the vow's own merit overrides the day's ugliness, so the obligation persists via qada.
3
Analyze the second example: prayer at disliked times
Time is NOT part of prayer's definition (prayer is defined by actions, not time slot). The external ugliness is separable (mujawir), so the prayer is makruh but binding; if invalidated, qada is required.
The same category (qabeeh li ghayrihi) yields a different ruling because time is external to the definition.
4
Analyze the third example: sale during Friday call
Sale's definition does not include time. The ugliness (forsaking Friday prayer) is separable: it accompanies the sale only when the seller also misses prayer. The sale itself is valid and confers ownership after possession, like a usurper's sale.
This shows the mujawir subcategory: the prohibition attaches situationally, not to the act's essence.
5
Extract the usul principle
The ruling on an externally ugly act depends on whether the external factor is part of the act's definition (lazim → fasid, not binding, no qada) or merely associated (mujawir → makruh, binding, qada if invalidated).
This principle guides a faqih when encountering new cases: first check definitional inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
بيان أقسام القبح
وإنما يحرم لأجل أن يوم النحر يومُ ضيافة الله تعالى، وفي الصوم إعراض عنها، وهذا المعنى لازم بمنزلة الوصف لهذا الصوم؛ لأن الوقت داخل في تعريف الصوم، ووصف الجزء وصف الكل، فصار فاسدًا، و لم يلزم بالشروع بخلاف النذر فإنه في نفسه طاعة، ولا فساد في التسمية، وإنما الفساد في الفعل، فيجب قضاؤه، وبخلاف الصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة فإنها وإن كانت من هذا القسم أيضًا لكن الوقت
ليس داخلاً في تعريفها ولا معيارا لها، فلم تكن فاسدة بل مكروهة تلزم بالشروع
ويجب القضاء بالإفساد.
والبيع وقت النداء مثال لما قبح لغيره مجاوراً، فإن البيع في ذاته أمر مشروع مفيد للملك، وإنما يحرم وقت النداء؛ لأن فيه ترك السعي إلى الجمعة الواجب بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاسَعَوْا إِلَى ذكر الله وَذَرُوا الْبَيْعَ﴾، وهذا المعنى مما يجاور البيع في بعض الأحيان فيما إذا باع وترك السعي، وينفك عنه في بعض الأحيان فيما إذا سعى إلى الجمعة وباع في الطريق بأن يكون البائع والمشتري راكبين في سفينة تذهب إلى الجامع، وفيما إذا لم يبع ولم يسع إلى الجمعة، بل اشتغل بلهو آخر فهذا البيع كبيع الغاصب يفيد الملك بعد القبض، ومثله
Section on the Specific: Explanation of the divisions of ugliness. It is prohibited only because the Day of Nahr is the day of Allah's hospitality, and fasting involves turning away from it, and this meaning is attached as a description to this fast; because time is included in the definition of fasting, and the description of the part is a description of the whole, so it becomes corrupt, and it is not binding upon commencement. Unlike a vow, for it is in itself an act of obedience, and there is no corruption in the naming, rather corruption is in the act, so it must be made up. And unlike prayer in disliked times, for although it is also of this category, time is not included in its definition nor is it a standard for it, so it is not corrupt but disliked, and binding upon commencement, and making up is obligatory if it is invalidated. And sale during the call is an example of what is ugly due to something external as a neighbor. For sale in itself is a lawful act beneficial for ownership; it is only prohibited during the call because it involves abandoning the obligatory going to Friday prayer by Allah's saying: 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave off sale.' This meaning sometimes accompanies sale (when one sells and abandons going) and sometimes separates from it (when one goes to Friday and sells on the way, e.g., both parties are riding a boat going to the mosque, or when one neither sells nor goes to Friday but engages in other idle pursuits). So this sale is like the sale of a usurper: it confers ownership after possession. And similar to it...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from the section on al-Khas (the Specific) in Noor al-Anwar. It explains the division of acts that are legally ugly (qabeeh) into those ugly in themselves and those ugly due to something external. The author illustrates the category 'ugly due to an external, inseparable quality' (qabeeh li ghayrihi laziman) using the example of fasting on the Day of Nahr. Since the day itself is the time for hospitality and fasting means turning away from that, and because time is part of the definition of fasting (i.e., fasting is abstention from dawn to sunset), the external ugliness of the day becomes a description of the fast itself, rendering it corrupt (fasid). Therefore, if one begins such a fast, it is not binding to complete it; rather one must break it, and no make-up (qada) is required. In contrast, a vowed fast (nadhr) to fast on that day: the vow itself is a good act, and the corruption is only in the actual performance, so the vow is valid and must be fulfilled by making up the day. Another comparison is prayer at disliked times (makruh). Though these prayers are also externally ugly (due to time), time is not a component or standard of prayer's definition; it is merely a container. Thus the prayer is not corrupt but only disliked (makruh), and it is binding once started; if invalidated, it must be made up. Finally, the sale during the call to Friday prayer is an example of 'ugly due to an external, separable neighbor' (qabeeh li ghayrihi mujawiran). Sale itself is lawful; the prohibition comes from the command to leave it for Friday prayer. This ugliness sometimes accompanies the sale (when one sells and misses prayer) and sometimes does not (e.g., selling on the way to prayer without missing it). Such a sale is valid and conveys ownership after possession, similar to the sale of a usurper's property (which is valid but ownership transfers only after possession with the owner's permission).

Key Terms
القبح (al-qubh): Legal ugliness or repugnance that prevents an act from being valid or desirable.
القبيح لغيره (al-qabeeh li ghayrihi): An act that is legally ugly not in its essence but due to an external factor, such as time or circumstance.
لازم (lazim): Inseparable; the external factor is part of the act's definition, making the act fasid.
مجاور (mujawir): Separable; the external factor is not part of the act's definition, so the act remains valid but may be makruh or prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who begins a supererogatory prayer in a disliked time and then invalidates it?
A) The prayer is fasid and no qada is required.
B) The prayer is makruh but binding; qada is required.
C) The prayer is valid and binding only if it was a vow.
D) The prayer is invalid and must be made up only if the time was part of its definition.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between lazim and mujawir within qabeeh li ghayrihi determines whether an externally prohibited act is fasid (not binding, no qada) or makruh (binding, qada required).
