Isti'ara and Majaz: Terminology and Conditions in Hanafi Usul
Classifying majaz into isti'ara and majaz mursal with their relationships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi usul definition of isti'ara, distinguish it from the balagha classification, and apply the conditions of specificity (khussus) and fame (shuhra) to identify valid figurative usages.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, isti'ara and majaz are synonymous; all figurative language is isti'ara.

This lesson clarifies a crucial terminological point in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the equivalence of 'isti'ara' and 'majaz'. While balagha scholars distinguish between isti'ara (metaphor based on resemblance) and majaz mursal (other relationships), the usul tradition treats all figurative language under the single term 'isti'ara'. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts that employ figurative expressions. The passage also lays out the conditions for valid isti'ara: the shared meaning must be specific and well-known for the borrowed-from.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terminological systems
The passage explicitly contrasts the usul and balagha definitions of isti'ara. In usul, isti'ara is synonymous with majaz; in balagha, isti'ara is a subtype. This is a key distinction.
This step ensures you grasp the foundational terminological difference.
2
Examine the relationship between isti'ara and majaz
The author affirms that in usul, isti'ara and majaz are used interchangeably. All figurative language falls under isti'ara, regardless of the type of relationship.
This is the core claim of the passage.
3
List the two main types of figurative connections
The passage mentions connection in form (surah) or meaning (ma'na). The meaning-based connection is what balagha calls tashbih (simile), while form-based connections cover other relationships like causality and whole-part.
In usul, both are considered isti'ara, but the type of connection determines the classification in balagha.
4
Apply the conditions of khussus and shuhra
A valid isti'ara (meaning-based) requires that the shared meaning be both specific to the borrowed-from (khass) and well-known for it (mashhur). For example, 'lion' for a brave man works because courage is specific to and well-known for lions.
These conditions ensure the metaphor is effective and not arbitrary.
5
Note the twenty-five relationships of majaz mursal
The passage enumerates various relationships (e.g., cause-effect, whole-part) that constitute figurative usage without tashbih. In usul, these are still considered isti'ara, but recognizing them is important for textual analysis.
This list is by induction and covers the common patterns of figurative extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
بيان علاقات المجاز
ثم لما فرغ المصنف له عن التفريعات شرع في بيان علاقات المجاز، فقال: وطريق الاستعارة الاتصال بين الشيئين صورة أو معنى والاستعارة في عرف الأصوليين يرادف المجاز، وعند أهل البيان قسم من المجاز، فإن المجاز عندهم إن كانت فيه علاقة التشبيه يسمى استعارة بأقسامها وإن كانت فيه علاقة غير التشبيه من علاقات الخمس والعشرين مثل السببية والمسببية، والحال والمحل التشبيه يسمى شرع في بيان علاقات هذا رد لما يتوهم من أن المصنف من ههنا يبين أحوال الاستعارة، وهو قسم من المجاز، ولما كان بصدد بيان المجاز لكونه قسيما للحقيقة، فكان عليه أن يبين أحوال المجاز مطلقًا أي قسم كان من أقسام المجاز، فإنه ذكر في علم البيان أن المجاز ينقسم إلى مرسل ومستعارة، فإن كانت العلاقة وجه استعارة وإلا مرسلاً، فأشار بهذا الكلام إلى رده بأن هذا الفرق بينهما على اصطلاح البيان، وأما على اصطلاح الأصول الاستعارة والمجاز مترادفان (السنبلي) بين الشيئين: أي المعنى الحقيقي والمعنى المجازي. (القمر) من صورة أو معنى الترديد على سبيل منع الخلو، فيجوز أن يكون الاتصال صورة ومعنى معا. (القمر) البيان: هو علم من علوم البلاغة. (القمر) يسمى استعارة كاستعمال الأسد في الرجل الشجاع لمشابهته إياه في الشجاعة. (القمر) بأقسامها وهي أربعة الكناية وهي تشبيه شيء بشيءٍ في النفس، وترك جميع أركانه أي المشبه والمشبه به ووجه التشبيه وحرف التشبيه سوى المشبه والتخيلية وهي إثبات لازم المشبه به المتروك للمشبه والتصريحية وهو ذكر المشبه به وإرادة المشبه والترشيحية وهو إثبات ملائم المشبه به للمشبه. (القمر) علاقات الخمس والعشرين إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب كإطلاق الغيث على النبات، عكسه كإطلاق الخمر على العنب، إطلاق اسم الكل على الجزء كالأصابع على الأنامل، عكسه كإطلاق الرقبة على الذات إطلاق اسم الملزوم على اللازم كالنطق للدلالة عكسه كشدّ الإزار للاعتزال من النساء إطلاق اسم المقيد على المطلق كالشقر الذي هو شفة الإبل للشفة المطلقة، عكسه كاليوم ليوم لقيامة، إطلاق اسم الخاص الخاص على العام، عكسه ومثالهما ظاهر، حذف المضاف وإقامة المضاف إليه مقامه نحو واسأل القرية أهلها، حذف المضاف إليه المجاورة كالميزاب للماء تسمية الشيء باعتبار ما يؤول إليه كإطلاق الفاضل على الطالب، تسمية الشيء باعتبار ما كان كإطلاق اليتيم على البالغ إطلاق اسم المحل على الحال كالكوز للماء، عكسه نحو: ففي رحمة الله أي الجنة، فإنها محل الرحمة، إطلاق اسم آلة الشيء عليه كاللسان للذكر، إطلاق أحد البدلين على الآخر كالدم للدية، إطلاق الشيء المعرف على واحد منكر إطلاق أحد الضدين على الآخر كالبصير للأعمى، الزيادة نحو: ليسَ كَمِثْله شَيْءٍ (الشوری: ۱۱)، الحذف كما يقال: لَكُمْ أَنْ تَضَلُّوا (النساء: ١٧٦) أي لئلا تضلوا، إطلاق النكرة في الإثبات للعموم نحو: علمت نَفْس (التكوير : ١٤) أي كل نفس، هذه مقامات المجاز المرسل، فصارت العلاقات بضم هذه مع علاقة الاستعارة وهو التشبيه خمسة وعشرين وهذا بالاستقراء. (القمر) متصلا أي بلا اعتبار اشتراك في معنى ثالث. (القمر) أن يكونا أي المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي. (القمر) في معنى واحد ولما كان هذا المعنى أمرا كليا والكليات لا تحس فسمي هذا الاتصال بالمعنوي. (القمر) خاص: المراد بالخصوص أن هذا المعنى لازم للمستعار منه وليس ذاتيا له وله خصوصية معه بحسب الغالب فلا ينافيه وجوده في غيره كالشجاعة للأسد، وإنما اعتبر كون ذلك المعنى خاصا بالمستعار منه؛ لأنه لو جازت الاستعارة بكل معنى لم يبق للكلام حسن وطراوة كذا قيل القمر) مشهور به ليس المراد بالشهرة أن يكون المستعار منه أشهر بذلك المعنى من المستعار له بل المراد أشهريته بذلك المعنى بالنسبة إلى غيره من أوصافه، فالمستعار له والمستعار منه إذا استويا في ذلك المعنى يصح الاستعارة كما في استعارة الهبة الصادقة، وبالعكس، فإنهما مستويان في الشهرة في كون لكل منهما تمليكا بغير عوض. (القمر) خاص مشهور إلخ إذ لو لم يكن خاصا أو لم يكن مشهورًا لما صحت الاستعارة حتى لم يجز تسمية شخص أسدا باعتبار معنى الحيوانية لعدم اختصاصها، ولا تسمية الأبخر والمحموم أسداً؛ لعدم شهرة الأسد بهذين الوصفين، وإن كان من لوازمه. (السنبلي)
Exposition of the sections of the third division. Exposition of the relationships of metaphor. Then when the author of the matn finished with the ramifications, he began the exposition of the relationships of metaphor, saying: The way of isti'ara is the connection between two things in form or meaning. Isti'ara in the terminology of usul scholars is synonymous with majaz, while in the terminology of balagha scholars it is a type of majaz. For majaz according to them: if it contains a relationship of tashbih (simile), it is called isti'ara with its divisions; and if it contains a relationship other than tashbih from the twenty-five relationships, such as causality, effect, condition, and place [then it is majaz mursal]. He began the exposition of the relationships of this [majaz]. This is a response to what might be imagined: that the author from here onward explains the conditions of isti'ara, which is a type of majaz. And since he intended to explain majaz because it is the counterpart of haqiqah, it was incumbent upon him to explain the conditions of majaz absolutely, whatever type of majaz it may be. For it is mentioned in the science of balagha that majaz is divided into mursal and isti'ara. If the relationship is a mode of simile, then it is isti'ara; otherwise it is mursal. He indicated by this statement that he rejects this [distinction] because this difference between them is according to the terminology of balagha, but according to the terminology of usul, isti'ara and majaz are synonymous. (al-Sunbuli) Between the two things: i.e., the literal meaning and the figurative meaning. (al-Qamar) In form or meaning: the disjunction is by way of preventing emptiness, so it is possible that the connection is both form and meaning together. (al-Qamar) Al-Bayan: it is a science among the sciences of balagha. (al-Qamar) It is called isti'ara, like using 'lion' for a courageous man due to his resemblance to it in courage. (al-Qamar) Its divisions: they are four: kinayah (allusion), which is likening something to something else in the mind, leaving out all its pillars except the object of likeness; takhyiliyyah (imaginative), which is affirming a necessary attribute of the omitted object of likeness to the subject; tasrihiyyah (explicit), which is mentioning the object of likeness while intending the subject; and tarshihiyyah (reinforced), which is affirming a suitable attribute of the object of likeness to the subject. (al-Qamar) The twenty-five relationships: Using the name of the cause for the effect, like using 'rain' for vegetation; its opposite, like using 'wine' for grapes; using the name of the whole for the part, like 'fingers' for fingertips; its opposite, like using 'neck' for the entire person; using the name of the necessary for the contingent, like 'speech' for indication; its opposite, like 'tightening the loincloth' for abstaining from women; using the name of the restricted for the unrestricted, like 'al-shaqr' which is the camel's lip, for the lip in absolute; its opposite, like 'day' for the Day of Judgment; using the name of the specific for the general, and its opposite, their examples are clear; deletion of the mudaf (genitive construct) and putting the mudaf ilayh in its place, like 'Ask the village' (meaning its people); deletion by adjacency, like 'al-mizab' (waterspout) for water; naming a thing by what it will become, like calling a student 'learned'; naming a thing by what it was, like calling an adult 'orphan'; using the name of the place for the thing contained, like 'pitcher' for water; its opposite, like 'in the mercy of Allah' meaning Paradise, for it is the place of mercy; using the name of the instrument of a thing for that thing, like 'tongue' for speech; using one of two alternatives for the other, like 'blood' for blood-money; using a definite thing for an indefinite one; using one of two opposites for the other, like 'seeing' for blind; addition, like 'There is nothing like His likeness' (Q. 42:11); deletion, like 'For you that you go astray?' (Q. 4:176) meaning 'so that you do not go astray'; using an indefinite noun in affirmative for generality, like 'Every soul will know' (Q. 81:14) meaning every soul. These are the categories of majaz mursal. So the relationships, by adding these to the relationship of isti'ara which is tashbih (simile), become twenty-five, and this is by induction. (al-Qamar) Connected: i.e., without consideration of sharing in a third meaning. (al-Qamar) That they are: i.e., the literal meaning and the figurative meaning. (al-Qamar) In one meaning: And since this meaning is a universal and universals are not perceptible, this connection is called 'meaning-based' (ma'nawi). (al-Qamar) Specific: what is meant by 'specific' is that this meaning is necessary for the borrowed-from (musta'ar minhu) and not intrinsic to it, and it has a particular association with it in most cases, so its existence in other things does not negate it, like courage for the lion. And it is considered that this meaning is specific to the borrowed-from because if isti'ara were permissible with any meaning, speech would have no beauty or freshness. This is what is said. (al-Qamar) Well-known: what is meant by 'well-known' is not that the borrowed-from is more famous for that meaning than the borrowed-for, but rather its fame for that meaning relative to its other attributes. Thus if the borrowed-for and the borrowed-from are equal in that meaning, isti'ara is valid, as in the isti'ara of 'sincere gift' and its opposite, for they are equally famous in that each involves transfer of ownership without compensation. (al-Qamar) Specific and well-known, etc. For if it were not specific or not well-known, the isti'ara would not be valid. Thus it is not permissible to call a person a lion based on the meaning of animality due to lack of specificity, nor to call a person with halitosis or fever a lion due to the lion's lack of fame for these two attributes, even if they are among its necessary concomitants. (al-Sunbuli)
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the relationships (علاقات) of metaphor (مجاز) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator explains that the author of the matn, having completed the exposition of ramifications, begins to discuss the relationships of majaz. He clarifies a crucial point: in the terminology of usul scholars, 'isti'ara' (metaphor proper) is synonymous with 'majaz' (figurative language), whereas in the science of balagha (rhetoric), isti'ara is a subcategory of majaz. This distinction is important because the author uses the term 'isti'ara' in the usul sense, meaning any figurative usage, and then divides majaz based on the type of relation between the literal and figurative meanings. The connection between the two meanings can be either formal (صورة) or meaning-based (معنى). Formal connection refers to relationships like causality, whole-part, etc., which belong to majaz mursal. Meaning-based connection refers to a shared attribute, which is isti'ara (again, synonymous with majaz in usul). The commentator then lists the twenty-five relationships of majaz mursal, as commonly enumerated in balagha, but with the understanding that in usul, all these are forms of isti'ara as well. He also discusses the conditions for isti'ara: the shared meaning must be specific (خاص) to the object of metaphor (e.g., courage is specific to lions) and well-known (مشهور) for that object (e.g., lions are famously courageous). Without these conditions, the metaphor fails. The passage is detailed and shows the integration of balagha concepts into usul al-fiqh with appropriate terminological adjustments.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majaz): Figurative language, the use of a word in a sense other than its original meaning, synonymous with isti'ara in Hanafi usul.
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): In Hanafi usul, synonymous with majaz; in balagha, a specific type of majaz based on the relationship of tashbih.
علاقة (ʿalāqah): The relationship or connection between the literal meaning and the figurative meaning that justifies the transfer of the word from one to the other.
المجاز المرسل (al-majāz al-mursal): Figurative usage based on relationships other than tashbih, such as causality, whole-part, etc. In balagha, this is a category distinct from isti'ara.
التشبيه (al-tashbīh): Simile; a relationship of resemblance between the literal and figurative meanings, which in balagha characterizes isti'ara as opposed to majaz mursal.
خاص (khāṣṣ): Specific; a necessary condition for isti'ara according to this text: the shared meaning must be peculiar to the borrowed-from in common usage.
مشهور (mashhūr): Well-known; a condition for isti'ara: the borrowed-from must be well-known for that meaning more than its other attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul tradition as presented in this passage, how are the terms 'isti'ara' and 'majaz' related?
A) They are completely distinct; isti'ara is a type of majaz.
B) They are synonymous; both refer to all figurative language.
C) Majaz is broader, including both isti'ara and majaz mursal.
D) Isti'ara is only used for metaphors based on tashbih.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, all figurative language is isti'ara; valid meaning-based isti'ara requires the shared meaning to be both specific to and well-known for the borrowed-from.

Types of Majaz: Suri and Ma'nawi Connections
Explaining suri and ma'nawi relations in majaz and isti'ara

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given metaphor is suri or ma'nawi and apply the conditions of isti'ara (khas and mashhur) to determine its validity.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, metaphors rely on formal contiguity (suri) or a shared specific attribute (ma'nawi); for isti'ara, the attribute must be specific and well-known.

This passage from a commentary on usul al-fiqh lays out the Hanafi classification of metaphors (majaz). It distinguishes between majaz mursal, based on formal connections like cause-effect, and isti'ara, based on a shared attribute. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting the Qur'an and Sunnah when metaphors are used.

Guided Path
1
Classify the type of majaz
Determine whether the connection between real and metaphorical meaning is suri (formal contiguity) or ma'nawi (shared attribute). Suri includes cause, effect, condition, state, etc.; ma'nawi requires a specific well-known attribute.
The passage uses 'lion' for a brave man as ma'nawi and 'sky' for rain as suri.
2
For suri, identify the specific relation
Name the exact relation: cause, effect, condition, part-whole, etc. For example, 'sky' for rain is cause-effect (the sky causes rain) or place-effect (clouds contain rain).
The text lists al-sabab, al-'illah, al-shart, al-hal, and their opposites.
3
For ma'nawi, check the conditions of isti'ara
Ensure the shared attribute is specific (khas) to the borrowed-from and well-known (mashhur) in it. If not, the metaphor fails.
Courage is specific to lions and well-known; animality is not specific, and bad breath is not famous.
4
Apply the conditions to examples
Test given metaphors from the text or Quran. For instance, 'Every soul' (81:14) is suri (indefinite for generality); 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) is suri (increase).
Use the lists in the passage to match relations.
5
Note the application in shari'ah rulings
Recognize that both types of majaz appear in legal texts (shar'iyat). Understanding the type prevents misinterpretation and ensures correct legal implications.
Causal relations in law (e.g., marriage as 'illah for ownership) mirror suri connections.

بيان علاقات المجاز
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث واللازم والملزوم وغيرها يسمى مجازًا مرسلاً ، والمصنف له عبر عن علاقات المجاز المرسل كلها بقوله: صورة وعن علاقة الاستعارة المسماة بالتشبيه بقوله: معنى، فكأنه قال: وطريق المجاز وجود العلاقة بين المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي بعلاقات المجاز المرسل أو بعلاقة الاستعارة، والأول هو الصوري، والثاني هو المعنوي، وأراد بالصوري أن تكون صورة المعنى المجازي متصلاً بصورة المعنى الحقيقي بنوع مجاورة بأن يكون سببًا له أو علة أو شرطًا أو حالاً أو عكسها، وبالمعنوي أن يكونا متشاركين في معنى واحد خاص مشهور به في العرف كما في تسمية الشجاع أسداً و المطر سماء نشر على غير ترتيب اللف، فإن الأول مثال للاتصال المعنوي؛ إذ الرجل الشجاع والهيكل المعلوم كلاهما متشاركان في معنى لازم
= كالدم للدية، إطلاق الشيء المعرف على واحد منكر إطلاق أحد الضدين على الآخر كالبصير للأعمى، الزيادة نحو: ليسَ كَمِثْله شَيْءٍ (الشوری: ۱۱)، الحذف كما يقال: لَكُمْ أَنْ تَضَلُّوا (النساء: ١٧٦) أي لئلا تضلوا، إطلاق النكرة في الإثبات للعموم نحو: علمت نَفْس (التكوير : ١٤) أي كل نفس، هذه مقامات المجاز المرسل، فصارت العلاقات بضم هذه مع علاقة الاستعارة وهو التشبيه خمسة وعشرين وهذا بالاستقراء. (القمر)
متصلا أي بلا اعتبار اشتراك في معنى ثالث. (القمر) أن يكونا أي المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي. (القمر) في معنى واحد ولما كان هذا المعنى أمرا كليا والكليات لا تحس فسمي هذا الاتصال بالمعنوي. (القمر) خاص: المراد بالخصوص أن هذا المعنى لازم للمستعار منه وليس ذاتيا له وله خصوصية معه بحسب الغالب فلا ينافيه وجوده في غيره كالشجاعة للأسد، وإنما اعتبر كون ذلك المعنى خاصا بالمستعار منه؛ لأنه لو جازت الاستعارة بكل معنى لم يبق للكلام حسن وطراوة كذا قيل القمر) مشهور به ليس المراد بالشهرة أن يكون المستعار منه أشهر بذلك المعنى من المستعار له بل المراد أشهريته بذلك المعنى بالنسبة إلى غيره من أوصافه، فالمستعار له والمستعار منه إذا استويا في ذلك المعنى يصح الاستعارة كما في استعارة الهبة الصادقة، وبالعكس، فإنهما مستويان في الشهرة في كون لكل منهما تمليكا بغير عوض. (القمر)
خاص مشهور إلخ إذ لو لم يكن خاصا أو لم يكن مشهورًا لما صحت الاستعارة حتى لم يجز تسمية شخص أسدا باعتبار معنى الحيوانية لعدم اختصاصها، ولا تسمية الأبخر والمحموم أسداً؛ لعدم شهرة الأسد بهذين الوصفين، وإن كان من لوازمه. (السنبلي)

بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
٢٩٤
يسمى
بیان علاقات المجاز
أسدا
مشهور مختص بالهيكل المعلوم، وهو الشجاعة أعني الجرأة، فلا الرجل باعتبار الحيوانية لعدم الاختصاص ولا الأبخر لعدم الشهرة، والثاني مثال للاتصال
الصوري، فإن صورة المطر يتصل بصورة السّماء يعني السحاب، فإن العرف يسمى كل ما علاك وأظلك سماءً، والمطر ينزل من السحاب، فيكون متصلا به.
ثم بين أن هذين القسمين كما وجدا في الحسيات والمحاورات كذلك وجدا في الأحكام
للمجاز
الشرعية، فقال: وفي الشرعيات الاتصال من حيث السببية والتعليل نظير الصورة يعني أن العلاقة بين الشيئين من حيث كون الأول سببًا للثاني، أو مسببًا عنه، أو كون الأول علة للثاني
أعني الجرأة إنما فسر الشجاعة بالجرأة؛ لأن الشجاعة مختصة بالإنسان والجرأة أعم من الشجاعة تشمل الإنسان وغيره كذا قيل وما في مسير" "الدائر" من أن العام مطلق الجرأة، وهو ليس بمراد ههنا بقرينة اقتضاء ،المقام، بل المراد جرأة الشجاعة وهو ليس بعام فمما لا أفهمه. (القمر) لعدم الاختصاص فإن الحيوانية ليست مختصة بالأسد. (القمر) ولا الأبخر إلخ أي لا يسمى الرجل الأبخر أسداً لعدم الشهرة، فإن الأسد يشتهر بالبخر (القمر) يتصل إلخ إن أريد بالسماء السحاب كما يشعر به قول الشارح فيما سيأتي يعني السحاب، فاتصال المطر بالسماء اتصال الحال بالمحل، فإن أهل العرف يزعمون أن السحاب محل المطر، وإن أريد بالسماء الفلك فاتصال المطر به اتصال المسبب بالسبب، فإن الأوضاع الفلكية سبب لحدوث المطر كذا قيل (القمر) فإن العرف إلا دليل على أن المراد بالسماء السحاب. (القمر) يسمى إلخ ومنه قيل لسقف البيت سماء. (القمر) كذلك وجدا إلخ لأن بناء المجاز على وجود الاتصال يوجد في الحسيات يوجد في الأحكام الشرعية أي الألفاظ الدالة على معان يترتب
كما صورة أو معنى، وهو عليها فوائد شرعية معتبرة عند الشارع . (القمر) الاتصال: أي بين المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي. (القمر)
من حيث السببية إلخ العلة في الشرع ما يكون موضوعًا لحكم مطلوب حتى لو لم يتصور الحكم لا يكون مشروعًا، فيضاف إليه وجود الحكم ووجوبه كالنكاح، فإنه موضوع لإفادة ملك المتعة ولا يجوز بينه وبين الحكم أمر يضاف إليه الحكم والسبب ما لا يكون مشروعًا كذلك، بل قد يكون مفضيًا إلى الحكم، ويكون بينه وبين الحكم أمر يضاف إليه الحكم، فلا يضاف إليه وجود ولا وجوب كالشراء، فإنه سبب لملك المتعة؛ لأنه فيما لا يتصور فيه ملك المتعة كشراء المحرمات كالأخت الرضاعية مثلاً. (القمر) يتصور
Statement of the relations of majaz. Statement of the sections of the third division: the necessary, the necessitated, and others are called majaz mursal. The author expressed all the relations of majaz mursal by 'form' and expressed the relation of isti'ara, called tashbih, by 'meaning', as if he said: the path of majaz is the existence of a relation between the real meaning and the metaphorical meaning either by the relations of majaz mursal or by the relation of isti'ara. The first is suri (formal) and the second is ma'nawi (meaning-based). He meant by suri that the form of the metaphorical meaning is connected to the form of the real meaning by a kind of contiguity, such as being a cause, a condition, a state, or their opposites. By ma'nawi he meant that they share a single specific meaning well-known customarily, as when calling a courageous man 'lion' and rain 'sky' published without alphabetical order. The first is an example of ma'nawi connection, for the courageous man and the known figure both share a necessary meaning. = e.g., blood for blood-money, applying the definite to an indefinite, applying one of two opposites to the other like blind to the seeing, increase like 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11), deletion like 'For you that you stray' (4:176) i.e. lest you stray, applying an indefinite in affirmation for generality like 'Every soul knew' (81:14) i.e. every soul. These are the positions of majaz mursal. The relations become twenty-five by adding these to the relation of isti'ara which is tashbih. This is by induction. (al-Qamar) 'Connected' meaning without considering sharing in a third meaning. (al-Qamar) 'Both be' i.e. the real and metaphorical meanings. (al-Qamar) 'In one meaning' and since this meaning is a universal and universals are not perceived, this connection is called ma'nawi. (al-Qamar) 'Specific' meaning that this meaning is necessary for the borrowed-from and not essential to it, and it has a specificity with it generally, so its existence in other things does not negate it, like courage for the lion. It was considered that this meaning is specific to the borrowed-from because if isti'ara were allowed with any meaning, speech would lose beauty and freshness. Thus it is said (al-Qamar) 'Well-known' meaning not that the borrowed-from is more famous with that meaning than the borrowed-to, but rather its fame with that meaning relative to its other descriptions. If the borrowed-to and borrowed-from are equal in that meaning, isti'ara is valid, as in the isti'ara of 'true gift' and vice versa, for both are equal in fame in that each is a transfer without compensation. (al-Qamar) 'Specific well-known' etc. For if it were not specific or not famous, isti'ara would not be valid. Thus it is not permissible to call a person 'lion' considering the meaning of animality due to lack of specificity, nor to call one with bad breath and fever 'lion' due to lack of fame of the lion with these two descriptions, even if they are among its necessities. (al-Sunbuli) Statement of the sections of the third division called statement of the relations of majaz 'lion' famous and specific to the known figure, which is courage meaning bravery. So neither the man considering animality due to lack of specificity nor the one with bad breath due to lack of fame. The second is an example of suri connection, for the form of rain connects to the form of the sky meaning cloud. For custom calls whatever covers you above and gives shade 'sky', and rain falls from clouds, so it is connected to it. Then he explained that these two sections, just as they exist in sensible things and daily speech, also exist in the rulings of shari'ah majaz. He said: 'And in shari'ah matters the connection from the aspect of causality and illah is analogous to form.' Meaning that the relation between two things from the aspect of the first being a cause for the second, or an effect from it, or the first being an illah for the second. I mean bravery; he interpreted courage as bravery because courage is specific to humans and bravery is more general including humans and others. Thus it is said. And what is in the circular reasoning that the general is absolute bravery, and that is not intended here by the context of the occasion, but rather bravery of courage is intended, and it is not general, I do not understand. (al-Qamar) 'Due to lack of specificity' for animality is not specific to the lion. (al-Qamar) 'Nor the one with bad breath etc.' i.e. he does not call the man with bad breath a lion due to lack of fame, for the lion is famous for halitosis. (al-Qamar) 'Connects etc.' If by sky is meant cloud, as suggested by the commentator's later statement 'meaning cloud', then the connection of rain to the sky is a connection of state to place, for the people of custom claim that cloud is the place of rain. If by sky is meant the celestial sphere, then the connection of rain to it is a connection of effect to cause, for celestial configurations are cause for the occurrence of rain. Thus it is said (al-Qamar) 'For custom except that it is evidence that what is meant by sky is cloud.' (al-Qamar) 'It is called etc.' And from this the ceiling of the house is called sky. (al-Qamar) 'Likewise they exist etc.' Because the basis of majaz is the existence of connection, it exists in sensible things and also in shari'ah rulings, i.e. words indicating meanings upon which are based shari'ah benefits considered by the Lawgiver. (al-Qamar) 'Connection' i.e. between real and metaphorical meaning. (al-Qamar) 'From the aspect of causality etc.' The illah in shari'ah is what is made a subject for a sought-after ruling, so that if the ruling were not imagined, it would not be legislated. Thus existence and obligation of the ruling are added to it, like marriage, for it is subject to providing the ownership of enjoyment, and nothing is allowed between it and the ruling that is added to the ruling. The cause (sabab) is not legislated likewise, but it may lead to the ruling, and there exists between it and the ruling something that is added to the ruling. Thus existence or obligation is not added to it, like purchase, for it is a cause for ownership of enjoyment, because in something where ownership of enjoyment is not imagined, like purchasing prohibited items such as a foster sister, for example. (al-Qamar) 'Imagine' end.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on usul al-fiqh, explaining the types of connections in majaz (metaphor). It distinguishes two main types: suri (formal) connection based on physical contiguity or cause-effect, and ma'nawi (meaning-based) connection based on a shared specific attribute. The author outlines the relations of majaz mursal (metonymy) under suri, giving examples from the Quran, and then discusses the relation of isti'ara (metaphor proper) under ma'nawi. For isti'ara, the attribute must be specific (khas) and well-known (mashhur) in the borrowed-from. The commentary by al-Qamar and al-Sunbuli clarifies these conditions: specificity ensures the metaphor is not overly general, and fame ensures it is recognizable. It also notes that both types apply in legal contexts (shar'iyat) where causal relations mirror suri connections, and shared meanings mirror ma'nawi. The passage ends with a distinction between 'illa and sabab in fiqh, showing how causal relations in majaz align with legal causes.

Key Terms
مجاز مرسل (majaz mursal): A metaphorical expression where the connection between the real and metaphorical meaning is based on a physical or causal relationship (suri), not a shared abstract quality.
استعارة (isti'ara): A metaphor where the connection is based on a shared specific attribute (ma'nawi) that is well-known in the borrowed-from.
صوري (suri): Formal connection: the metaphorical meaning relates to the real meaning through contiguity (cause, effect, condition, etc.).
معنوي (ma'nawi): Meaning-based connection: both meanings share a specific conceptual attribute, forming the basis of isti'ara.
خاص مشهور (khas mashhur): The attribute must be specific (not universal) and well-known (famous) in the borrowed-from for isti'ara to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, for isti'ara to be valid, which two conditions must the shared attribute satisfy?
A) It must be universal and known to all.
B) It must be specific (khas) and well-known (mashhur) in the borrowed-from.
C) It must be generic and frequently used.
D) It must be essential and intrinsic to the borrowed-from.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, metaphors are classified as suri (majaz mursal) or ma'nawi (isti'ara); for isti'ara, the shared attribute must be specific (khas) and well-known (mashhur) to the borrowed-from.

Distinguishing 'Cause' and 'Reason' in Hanafi Usul
Commentary on metaphorical relationships and the distinction between cause and reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'illa (cause) and sabab (reason) in Hanafi usul, and apply this distinction to evaluate whether a metaphorical transfer between legal concepts is valid.

Core Idea
A cause necessitates the ruling directly; a reason leads through an intermediary—this distinction governs metaphorical transfers in legal language.

This lesson unpacks a foundational distinction in Hanafi usul: the difference between a cause ('illa) that directly necessitates a ruling, and a reason (sabab) that works through an intermediary. This distinction is not merely theoretical—it determines when a metaphorical use of legal terms is permissible. The commentary you'll read explains this through concrete fiqh examples, such as how purchase causes ownership of the corpus but is only a reason for ownership of usufruct. By mastering this distinction, you will better understand how Hanafi scholars reason about legal analogies and metaphorical extensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conceptual connection
The commentary distinguishes between a formal connection (like effect to cause) and a conceptual connection (like sharing a common goal). First, locate these two types in the passage.
The formal connection is 'pictorial' (صوری), while the conceptual connection is 'meaning-based' (معنوی).
2
Define 'illa and sabab per Hanafi usul
From the text: 'العلة ما يوجب الحكم بنفسه من غير واسطة شيء، والسبب ما يفضي إلى الحكم بواسطة علة تقع بينهما.' Write your own definitions in one sentence each.
Remember: 'illa is direct; sabab involves an intermediary.
3
Apply the definitions to the examples
Apply the definitions to the examples given: purchase (شراء) and ownership of corpus (ملك الرقبة), and purchase as reason for ownership of usufruct (ملك المتعة). Explain why each is 'illa or sabab.
Purchase is 'illa for corpus ownership because no intermediary; it is sabab for usufruct ownership because corpus ownership mediates.
4
Determine when metaphor is permitted
The text states that the relationship permits (يجوز) but does not obligate metaphor. For a metaphor to be valid, the shared meaning must be considered without focusing on differing consequences. Identify the condition for metaphorical transfer.
The condition is that the core meaning (e.g., 'reinforcement of debt') is shared, even if consequences differ.
5
Recognize the practical consequence
The distinction affects whether a term can be used metaphorically. For example, can the word 'sale' be used to refer to 'cause of ownership'? Only if the relationship is direct ('illa) or indirect (sabab) with proper connection.
This distinction underlies later debates, such as using divorce terms for manumission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان علاقات المجاز
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث أو معلولاً له، نظير الاتصال الصوري من الحسيات، فإن المسبب يتصل بالسبب ويجاوره صورة، وكذا المعلول يتصل بالعلة ويجاورها كالملك يتصل بالشراء، وملك المتعة يتصل بملك الرقبة. والاتصال في معنى المشروع كيف شرع نظير المعنى أي العلاقة في المعنى الذي شرع المشروع لأجله حال كونه بأية كيفية شرع نظير الاتصال المعنوي في المحسوسات كالاتصال بين الكفالة والحوالة في كونهما توثيقا للدين، وبين الصدقة والهبة في كونهما تمليكا بغير عوض وأمثاله.
نظير الاتصال إلخ إذ العلاقة ليست هي المشاركة في وصف (القمر) يتصل بالشراء إلخ: فإن الملك معلول والشراء علة . (القمر) يتصل بملك الرقبة فإن ملك الرقبة سبب لملك المتعة. (القمر) والاتصال: أي الاتصال عقد مشروع بعقد مشروع (القمر) في المعنى الذي شرع إلخ: فيه إيماء إلى أن قول المصنف معنى المشروع بإضافة التخصيص أي المعنى الذي له خصوصية بالمشروع، وشرع المشروع لأجله، والحاصل أنه يتأمل في المعنى الذي شرع المشروع لأجله، فإن وقف عليه ووجد ذلك المعنى في مشروع آخر جاز استعارة كل منهما للآخر. (القمر) بأية كيفية شرع أي بلا ملاحظة لوازمه؛ لأنه لو نظر إلى لوازمه لا يصح الاستعارة للمباينة بين الكفالة والحوالة، فإن الأصل في كل واحد ظاهر وهو ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في الأول، ونقل الدين من ذمة إلى ذمة في الثاني معناه التوثق للدين كما قال الشارح مع تباين لوازمهما؛ لأن لازم الكفالة مرجوع إلى المكفول عنه، ولازم الحوالة عدم الرجوع قبل التوى.
بين الكفالة إلخ لأن معنى الحوالة نقل الدين من ذمة إلى ذمة أخرى ومعنى الوكالة: نقل ولاية التصرف، فهما الصدقة متشابهان في المعنى وكذا الميراث والوصية بينهما اتصال معنوي، فيجوز استعارة أحدهما للآخر. (السنبلي) في كونهما توثيقا إلخ يعني أن الكفالة والحوالة تشتركان في كونهما توثيقا للدين، فيصح الاستعارة من الطرفين، فالكفالة بشرط براءة الأصيل حوالة والحوالة بشرط عدم براءة الأصيل كفالة. (القمر) توثيقا إلخ : فقلنا : الكفالة بشرط براءة الأصيل حوالة والحوالة بشرط مطالبة الأصيل كفالة. (السنبلي) إلخ فجوزنا استعارة الهبة للصدقة فيما إذا وهب للفقير شيئا، واستعارة لفظ الصدقة للهبة فيما إذا تصدق على الغني. (السنبلي) تمليكاً إلخ: يعني أن الصدقة والهبة تشتركان في أن كلا منهما تمليك بغير عوض، فيستعار لفظ الهبة للصدقة فيما إذا وهب لفقيرين فهذه صدقة حتى لا تبطل بالشيوع، ويستعار لفظ الصدقة للهبة فيما إذا تصدق على غنيين، فهذه هبة، فتبطل بالشيوع. (القمر) وأمثاله كالميراث والوصية، فإن كل واحد منهما يثبت الملك بطريق الخلافة بعد الفراغ عن حاجة الميت، فيجوز استعارة أحدهما للآخر. (السنبلي)
ثم بعد ذلك ترك المصنف الله تفصيل الاتصال المعنوي، وذكر بعض أنواع الاتصال الصوري ليبتني عليه الفرق بين العلة والسبب فقال: والأول على نوعين: أي الاتصال من حيث السببية، والتعليل يتنوع على نوعين؛ لأن السببية نوع آخر، والتعليل نوع آخر، ولما كان علاقة التعليل أشرف من السبية قدمها حيث قال: أحدهما: اتصال الحكم بالعلة كاتصال الملك بالشراء، وإنه يوجب الاستعارة من الطرفين، فيجوز أن تذكر العلة ويراد الحكم، وأن يذكر الحكم وتراد العلة؛ لأن الحكم يحتاج إلى العلة من حيث الثبوت، والعلة محتاجة إلى الحكم من حيث الشرعية؛ إذ لم تشرع العلة إلا للحكم، فجاء الافتقار من الطرفين، والأصل في الاستعارة: أن يذكر المفتقر إليه ويراد المفتقر، فتصح الاستعارة من الجانبين.
ليبتني عليه إلخ يعنى أنه إنما خص الاتصال الشرعي الصوري بالذكر دون الاتصال المعنوي الشرعي؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى بيان الفرق بين اتصال الحكم بالعلة واتصال المسبب بالسبب، وقد يبتني على المسألة الخلافية، وهي استعارة ألفاظ الطلاق للعتق كما ستعرف. (القمر) الاتصال: أي الاتصال الصوري الشرعي بين المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي. (القمر) أشرف إلخ لإضافة الحكم إلى العلة وجودًا وعدما دون السبب. (القمر) بالعلة إلخ والفرق بين السبب والعلة أن العلة ما يوجب الحكم بنفسه أي من غير واسطة شيء، والسبب ما يفضي إلى الحكم بواسطة علة تقع بينهما كالبيع يوجب ملك الرقبة من غير واسطة شيء، فكان علة له، ويوجب ملك المتعة في الإماء بواسطة ملك الرقبة، فكان سببًا له والسبب المحض عندهم ما يكون مفضيًا إلى الحكم من غير أن يضاف إليه العلة المتخللة بينهما كدلالة السارق على مال إنسان يسرقه؛ لأن علة تلف المسروق وهي السرقة لا يضاف إلى الدلالة والمراد بالسبب ههنا غير العلة أعم من أن يكون سببًا محضا، أو سببا بمعنى العلة. (السنبلي) كاتصال الملك إلخ فإن الملك حكم للشراء، والشراء علته وهو موضوع لترتب الملك عليه. (القمر) فيجوز إلخ إيماء إلى أن المراد بقول المصنف: يوجب التجويز والتصحيح لا الإيجاب، فإن العلاقة لا تكون موجبة للاستعارة بل تجوزها. (القمر) إلى العلة أي إلى علة ما على سبيل البدلية. (القمر) إذ لم تشرع: أي لم تقصد العلة شرعًا لذاتها، بل إنما شرعت لحكمها. (القمر) العلة حتى لا تكون مشروعة في محل لا يتصور شرع الحكم فيه نحو: بيع الحر ونكاح المحارم. (المحشي)
Statement of the relationships of metaphor. Statement of the divisions of the third division: either as an effect of it, analogous to the formal connection in physical things, for the effect is connected to the cause and adjoins it in form, and likewise the caused is connected to the cause and adjoins it, like ownership is connected to purchase, and ownership of usufruct is connected to ownership of the corpus. And the connection in the meaning of the legislated [act] in whatever manner it was legislated, analogous to the meaning—i.e., the relationship in the meaning for which the legislated act was legislated, while it is in whatever quality it was legislated—similar to the conceptual connection in physical things, like the connection between suretyship and transfer of debt in both being a reinforcement for the debt, and between charity and gift in both being ownership without compensation, and their likes. 'Analogous to the connection, etc.' because the relationship is not the sharing in a description (al-Qamar). It is connected to purchase, etc.: for ownership is the effect and purchase is the cause. (al-Qamar) It is connected to ownership of the corpus, for ownership of the corpus is a cause for ownership of usufruct. (al-Qamar) 'And the connection': i.e., the connection of a legislated contract with a legislated contract. (al-Qamar) 'In the meaning for which it was legislated, etc.' There is an indication that the author's statement 'meaning of the legislated' with the genitive of specification means the meaning that has specificity to the legislated act, and for which the legislated act was legislated. The result is that one examines the meaning for which the legislated act was legislated; if one stops at it and finds that meaning in another legislated [act], it is permissible to use each as a metaphor for the other. (al-Qamar) 'In whatever quality it was legislated': i.e., without observing its necessary consequences; because if one looked at its necessary consequences, the metaphor would not be valid due to the difference between suretyship and transfer of debt. For the essence of each is apparent: it is joining a liability to a liability in the first, and transferring the debt from one liability to another in the second, while their meaning is the reinforcement of the debt, as the commentator said, despite the difference in their consequences; because the consequence of suretyship is recourse to the principal debtor, and the consequence of transfer of debt is no recourse before insolvency. 'Between suretyship, etc.' Because the meaning of transfer of debt is transferring the debt from one liability to another, and the meaning of agency is transferring the authority to act, so they are similar in meaning, and likewise inheritance and bequest have a conceptual connection between them, so it is permissible to use each as a metaphor for the other. (al-Sunbulī) 'In both being a reinforcement, etc.' Means that suretyship and transfer of debt share in being a reinforcement for the debt, so metaphor is valid from both sides. So suretyship with the condition of the original debtor being free is transfer of debt, and transfer of debt with the condition of the original debtor not being free is suretyship. (al-Qamar) 'Reinforcement, etc.': So we said: Suretyship with the condition of the original debtor being free is transfer of debt, and transfer of debt with the condition of suing the original debtor is suretyship. (al-Sunbulī) 'etc.' So we allowed using gift as a metaphor for charity when one gives something to a poor person, and using the word charity as a metaphor for gift when one gives charity to a rich person. (al-Sunbulī) 'Ownership without compensation, etc.': Means that charity and gift share in that each is ownership without compensation, so the word gift is used as a metaphor for charity when one gives to two poor people—this is charity so that it is not invalidated by common ownership—and the word charity is used as a metaphor for gift when one gives charity to two rich people—this is a gift, so it is invalidated by common ownership. (al-Qamar) 'And their likes': like inheritance and bequest, for each of them establishes ownership by way of succession after fulfilling the deceased's needs, so it is permissible to use each as a metaphor for the other. (al-Sunbulī) Then after that, the author (may Allah have mercy on him) left the detailing of the conceptual connection and mentioned some types of the formal connection to build upon it the difference between the cause and the reason. He said: 'And the first is of two types': i.e., the connection from the perspective of causality and causing is divided into two types; because causality is one type, and causing is another type. And since the relationship of causing is nobler than that of causality, he gave precedence to it when he said: 'One of them: the connection of the ruling to the cause, like the connection of ownership to purchase, and it necessitates metaphor from both sides. So it is permissible that the cause be mentioned and the ruling intended, and that the ruling be mentioned and the cause intended; because the ruling is in need of the cause in terms of existence, and the cause is in need of the ruling in terms of legislation; since the cause was only legislated for the ruling, so need arises from both sides. The principle in metaphor is that the one in need is mentioned and the one in need is intended, so metaphor is valid from both sides.' 'To build upon it, etc.': Means that he only specified the formal legislative connection over the conceptual legislative connection because he needed to explain the difference between the connection of the ruling to the cause and the connection of the effect to the reason, and it may build upon a controversial issue, which is the use of divorce terms as metaphors for manumission, as you will know. (al-Qamar) 'The connection': i.e., the formal legislative connection between the real and metaphorical meaning. (al-Qamar) 'Nobler, etc.': For the attribution of the ruling to the cause both in existence and absence without the reason. (al-Qamar) 'To the cause, etc.': And the difference between the reason and the cause is that the cause is that which necessitates the ruling by itself, i.e., without any intermediary, and the reason is that which leads to the ruling through an intermediary cause between them—like sale necessitates ownership of the corpus without any intermediary, so it is a cause for it, and it necessitates ownership of usufruct in slave-women through the intermediary of ownership of the corpus, so it is a reason for it. And the pure reason according to them is that which leads to the ruling without the intervening cause being attributed to it, like a thief pointing to a person's property that he steals; because the cause of the loss of the stolen property—which is theft—is not attributed to the pointing. And what is meant by reason here is other than the cause, whether it is a pure reason or a reason in the sense of cause. (al-Sunbulī) 'Like the connection of ownership, etc.': For ownership is a ruling of purchase, and purchase is its cause, and it is placed for ownership to follow from it. (al-Qamar) 'So it is permissible, etc.': An indication that what the author means by 'necessitates' is permissibility and validation, not obligation; for the relationship does not necessitate metaphor but permits it. (al-Qamar) 'To the cause': i.e., to a certain cause by way of substitution. (al-Qamar) 'Since it was not legislated': i.e., the cause was not intended legislatively for itself, but rather was only legislated for its ruling. (al-Qamar) 'The cause so that it is not legislated in a place where the legislation of the ruling is not conceivable, like selling a free person and marrying close relatives. (al-Muhashshī)
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of metaphorical relationships (علاقات المجاز) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It explains the third division, which concerns the connection between the real and metaphorical meanings. The author distinguishes between two types of conceptual connection: the connection of the ruling to its cause (اتصال الحكم بالعلة) and the connection of the effect to its reason (اتصال المسبب بالسبب). The text emphasizes that the relationship is not mere sharing of a description but a meaningful link. Examples from fiqh are given: ownership (الملك) is connected to purchase (الشراء) as effect to cause; ownership of usufruct (ملك المتعة) is connected to ownership of the corpus (ملك الرقبة) as effect to reason. Similarly, suretyship (الكفالة) and transfer of debt (الحوالة) share the common goal of reinforcing a debt, while charity (الصدقة) and gift (الهبة) both involve ownership without compensation. The commentary notes that these conceptual connections permit metaphorical usage (استعارة) from either side, provided the conditions are met. The text then details the difference between cause (علة) and reason (سبب): a cause necessitates the ruling directly without intermediary, while a reason leads to the ruling through an intermediary cause. For example, sale is a cause for ownership of the corpus, but a reason for ownership of usufruct because it goes through corpus ownership. The author stresses that the relationship does not obligate metaphor but only permits it, and that the metaphor is valid only when the common meaning is considered without focusing on differing consequences. This discussion lays the groundwork for later controversies, such as using divorce terms for manumission.

Key Terms
العلة (al-‘illa): The cause that necessitates the ruling by itself without any intermediary
السبب (al-sabab): The reason that leads to the ruling through an intermediary cause
المجاز (al-majāz): Metaphorical usage based on a connection between real and metaphorical meanings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a cause ('illa) and a reason (sabab) according to the passage?
A) A cause is always physical; a reason is always legal.
B) A cause necessitates the ruling directly; a reason leads through an intermediary.
C) A cause is only for acts of worship; a reason is for transactions.
D) A cause requires explicit textual proof; a reason can be inferred.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the distinction between 'illa (direct cause) and sabab (indirect reason) is crucial for determining the validity of metaphorical transfers between legal concepts.

The Two-Way Metaphorical Connection Between Cause and Ruling
Explains the second type of formal connection in majaz and its fiqh branch

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'illa (cause) and sabab (reason) in Hanafi usul and apply the principle of metaphorical borrowing (isti'ara) to oaths of emancipation, identifying when the intended meaning is accepted religiously but not judicially.

Core Idea
Cause and ruling are mutually dependent, so each can stand for the other in metaphorical language.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key usul principle: the formal connection between a legal cause ('illa) and its ruling (hukm) is so strong that each can be used metaphorically for the other. This is not just a linguistic curiosity; it has practical consequences for how oaths are interpreted. The Hanafi school allows this borrowing, but with a crucial distinction between religious acceptance (diyaanatan) and judicial enforcement (qadaa'an). We will explore the definitions, the proof, and the application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of formal connection
The passage states that the formal legal connection is of two types: connection of ruling with cause (ta'lil) and connection of effect with reason (sabab). Focus on the first as it is more noble.
The author prioritizes causation over causality because the ruling is directly tied to the cause.
2
Define 'illa and sabab precisely
'Illa directly necessitates the ruling without any intermediary. Sabab leads to the ruling via an intervening cause. Example: sale causes ownership directly (illa) but the purchase of a slave girl causes ownership of enjoyment only through ownership of the slave (sabab).
The pure reason (al-sabab al-mahd) is when the intervening cause is not attributed to the reason.
3
Understand mutual dependency
The ruling needs the cause for its existence; the cause needs the ruling for its legislation. Hence, each is dependent on the other, allowing metaphorical borrowing (isti'ara): the needed thing is mentioned, the needing thing is intended.
This mutual need is the foundation for permissibility, not obligation.
4
Apply to oaths of emancipation
If someone says 'If I buy a slave, he is free' but intends ownership, or vice versa, the oath is valid religiously (diyanatan) because the metaphorical relation is sound. The mufti will give a fatwa according to the intention.
The result differs: purchase does not require simultaneous ownership of all parts, while ownership does.
5
Distinguish diyanatan from qada'an
Judicially, the qadi does not accept the intended meaning because the apparent wording governs court decisions. The slave is not freed in court if the condition according to the real meaning is not met.
This distinction is crucial for understanding fatwa vs. qada in Hanafi fiqh.

يتنوع على نوعين؛ لأن السببية نوع آخر، والتعليل نوع آخر، ولما كان علاقة التعليل أشرف من السببية قدمها حيث قال: أحدهما: اتصال الحكم بالعلة كاتصال الملك بالشراء، وإنه يوجب الاستعارة من الطرفين، فيجوز أن الاستعارة: أن يذكر المفتقر إليه ويراد المفتقر، فتصح الاستعارة من الجانبين. ليبتني عليه إلخ يعنى أنه إنما خص الاتصال الشرعي الصوري بالذكر دون الاتصال المعنوي الشرعي؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى بيان الفرق بين اتصال العلة وجودًا وعدما دون السبب. بالعلة إلخ والفرق بين السبب والعلة أن العلة ما يوجب الحكم بنفسه أي من غير واسطة شيء، والسبب ما يفضي إلى الحكم بواسطة علة تقع بينهما كالبيع يوجب ملك الرقبة من غير واسطة شيء، فكان علة له، ويوجب ملك المتعة في الإماء بواسطة ملك الرقبة، فكان سببًا له والسبب المحض عندهم ما يكون مفضيًا إلى الحكم من غير أن يضاف إليه العلة المتخللة بينهما كدلالة السارق على مال إنسان يسرقه؛ لأن علة تلف المسروق وهي السرقة لا يضاف إلى الدلالة والمراد بالسبب ههنا غير العلة أعم من أن يكون سببًا محضا، أو سببا بمعنى العلة. كاتصال الملك إلخ فإن الملك حكم للشراء، والشراء علته وهو موضوع لترتب الملك عليه. فيجوز إلخ إيماء إلى أن المراد بقول المصنف: يوجب التجويز والتصحيح لا الإيجاب، فإن العلاقة لا تكون موجبة للاستعارة بل تجوزها. إلى العلة أي إلى علة ما على سبيل البدلية. إذ لم تشرع: أي لم تقصد العلة شرعًا لذاتها، بل إنما شرعت لحكمها. العلة حتى لا تكون مشروعة في محل لا يتصور شرع الحكم فيه نحو: بيع الحر ونكاح المحارم. حتى إذا قال: "إن اشتريت عبدًا فهو حر"، ونوى به الملك، أو قال: "إن ملكتُ عبدًا فهو حر"، ونوى به الشراء، يصدق فيهما ديانةً تفريع لاستعارة العلة للحكم وعكسه، فإن الشراء علة والملك معلول، والأصل في الشراء أن لا يشترط اجتماع الكل في الملك، والأصل في الملك أن يشترط الاجتماع عرفًا، فإن اشترى نصف عبد وباعه، ثم اشترى النصف الآخر يعتق هذا النصف في صورة الشراء لا في صورة الملك باعتبار المعنى الحقيقي، فإن قال أردتُ بأحدهما الآخر يصدق في الصورتين ديانة؛ لصحة الاستعارة، لتحقق الشرط لعدم تحقق الشرط فيعتق نصف العبد الباقي في صورة ما نوى الشراء بالملك ولم يعتق في صورة ما نوى الملك بالشراء، ولكن القاضي لا يصدقه ديانة: أي فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لا قضاء. اجتماع الكل في الملك: أي في زمان واحد، فإن من اشترى الشيء متفرقًا أو مجتمعًا يقال له: إنه اشتراه، أن يشترط إلخ فإنه لا يقال عرفا لمن ملك شيئًا ثم باعه ثم ملك شيئًا آخر ثم باعه ثم ملك شيئًا آخر: إنه مالك هذه الأشياء الثلاثة، بل يقال: إنه مشتريها. يعتق هذا إلخ لتحقق الشرط، لأنه صار مشتريًا للعبد بتمامه، وإن كان الشراء متفرقًا. في صورة الشراء: أي فيما إذا قال: "إن اشتريت عبدًا فهو حر، ثم اعلم أن هذا إذا كان الشراء صحيحًا، وأما إذا كان الشراء فاسدًا فلا يعتق وإن اشترى العبد جملة؛ لأن شرط الحنث قد تم قبل أن يقبضه، ولا ملك له في الشراء الفاسد قبل القبض، فينحل اليمين ولم يقع الجزاء لعدم المحل كذا في "التحقيق". لا في صورة الملك: أي لا يعتق هذا النصف الثاني في صورتها إذا قال: "إن ملكت عبدًا فهو حر"، لأن الملك يقتضي الاجتماع، وهو ما صار مالكًا لتمام العبد بالاجتماع؛ لأنه مشتريه متفرقًا، فما تحقق الشرط فلا يعتق. الملك: لأن الملك يوجب الاجتماع ولم يوجد. بأحدهما الآخر: أي بالشراء الملك وبالملك الشراء. يصدق: إذا استفتى القائل عن جواب هذه الحادثة المفتي يفتي على وفق نيته. ما نوى الشراء إلخ: أي قال: "إن ملكت" إلخ، ونوى "إن اشتريت" إلخ. بالملك: لأن الشراء لم يوجب الاجتماع. ما نوى إلخ أي قال: "إن اشتريت" إلخ، ونوى به "إن ملكت" إلخ. لا يصدقه أي إذا خاصم إليه العبد.
Statement of the divisions of the third division. Then after that, the author [م] left out the detailed explanation of the spiritual connection, and mentioned some types of the formal connection so that the difference between cause and reason could be built upon it. He said: And the first is of two types: meaning the connection in terms of causality and causation is divided into two types because causality is another type and causation is another type. Since the relation of causation is nobler than causality, he gave precedence to it, saying: One of them is the connection of the ruling with the cause, like the connection of ownership with purchase. And indeed it necessitates borrowing (metaphor) from both sides, so it is permissible to mention the cause and intend the ruling, and to mention the ruling and intend the cause. Because the ruling needs the cause in terms of existence, and the cause needs the ruling in terms of legality, for the cause was not legislated except for the ruling. Thus the need is from both sides. And the principle in metaphor is that the needed thing is mentioned and the needing thing is intended, so metaphor is valid from both sides. 'That it may be built upon it etc.' means that he only specified the formal legal connection for mention, not the legal spiritual connection, because there is need to explain the difference between the connection of the ruling with the cause and the connection of the effect with the reason, and it may be built upon the contentious issue, which is the metaphor of the words of divorce for emancipation, as you will come to know. (Al-Qamar) Connection: i.e., the formal legal connection between the real and metaphorical meanings. (Al-Qamar) 'More noble etc.' because of the addition of the ruling to the cause in existence and non-existence, unlike the reason. (Al-Qamar) 'With the cause etc.' And the difference between reason and cause is that the cause is that which necessitates the ruling by itself, i.e., without any intermediary; while the reason is that which leads to the ruling by means of a cause that occurs between them. For example, sale necessitates ownership of the slave without any intermediary, so it is a cause for it; and it necessitates ownership of enjoyment in slave girls by means of ownership of the slave, so it is a reason for it. The pure reason in their view is that which leads to the ruling without the intervening cause being attributed to it, like the informer of a thief directing to a person's property to steal it, for the cause of the loss of the stolen property, which is theft, is not attributed to the informing. And what is meant by reason here is other than the cause, more general, whether it is a pure reason or a reason in the sense of cause. (Al-Sunbulī) 'Like the connection of ownership etc.' for indeed ownership is a ruling of purchase, and purchase is its cause, and it is laid down for the effect of ownership upon it. (Al-Qamar) 'Permissible etc.' indicates that what the author means by 'necessitates' is permission and validation, not obligation, for the relation does not necessitate metaphor but makes it permissible. (Al-Qamar) 'To the cause' i.e., to any cause by way of substitution. (Al-Qamar) 'Since it was not legislated' i.e., the cause was not intended legally for itself, but rather it was legislated only for its ruling. (Al-Qamar) The cause so that it is not legislated in a place where the ruling cannot be conceived legally, like the sale of a free person or marriage of mahrams. (Al-Muḥashshī) So that if he says, 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' intending thereby ownership, or he says, 'If I own a slave, he is free,' intending thereby purchase, it is accepted in both cases as a matter of din (religious duty), a branch of the metaphor of the cause for the ruling and its opposite. For purchase is a cause and ownership is an effect. The principle regarding purchase is that it does not require the gathering of all parts in ownership at one time; while the principle regarding ownership is that it does require gathering customarily. If he buys half a slave and sells it, then buys the other half, this half is emancipated in the case of purchase, not in the case of ownership, according to the real meaning. If he says, 'I intended one by the other,' it is accepted in both cases as a matter of din due to the validity of the metaphor. (Due to the fulfillment of the condition, due to the non-fulfillment of the condition) So the remaining half of the slave is emancipated in the case where he intended purchase by ownership, and not emancipated in the case where he intended ownership by purchase. However, the qadi does not accept it from him. 'Diyānah' (religiously): i.e., between him and Allah, not judicially. (Al-Qamar) 'Gathering of all parts in ownership': i.e., at one time. For one who buys something piecemeal or together is said to have bought it. (Al-Qamar) 'Does require etc.' For customarily, one who owns something, then sells it, then owns something else, then sells it, then owns something else, is not said to be the owner of these three things; rather he is said to be their buyer. (Al-Qamar) 'Is emancipated this etc.' because the condition is fulfilled, for he became a buyer of the entire slave, even if the purchase was piecemeal. (Al-Qamar) 'In the case of purchase': i.e., when he says, 'If I buy a slave, he is free.' Then know that this applies if the purchase is valid; but if the purchase is invalid, he is not emancipated even if he bought the slave in one go, because the condition of the oath was completed before he took possession, and he has no ownership in an invalid purchase before possession, so the oath is annulled and the penalty does not occur due to lack of subject. Similarly in 'al-Tahqīq'. (Al-Qamar) 'Not in the case of ownership': i.e., this second half is not emancipated in the case of ownership when he says, 'If I own a slave, he is free,' because ownership requires gathering, and he did not become owner of the entire slave by gathering because he bought it piecemeal, so the condition is not fulfilled and thus he is not emancipated. (Al-Qamar) 'Ownership': because ownership necessitates gathering and it did not exist. (Al-Muḥashshī) 'One by the other': i.e., by purchase [he meant] ownership and by ownership [he meant] purchase. (Al-Qamar) 'Is accepted': if the speaker asks the mufti for an answer to this incident, the mufti gives a fatwa according to his intention. (Al-Qamar) 'What he intended by purchase etc.': i.e., he said, 'If I own...' and intended 'If I buy...' (Al-Qamar) 'By ownership': because purchase did not necessitate gathering. (Al-Muḥashshī) 'What he intended etc.': i.e., he said, 'If I buy...' and intended thereby 'If I own...' (Al-Qamar) 'He does not accept it from him': i.e., if the slave brings a case against him [in court].
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the third division of the types of formal legal connections (al-ittisal al-suri al-shar'i) that allow metaphor (ist'ara) between real and metaphorical meanings. The author, al-Qamar, explains that the connection between a ruling (hukm) and its cause ('illa) is a two-way street: each is dependent on the other, so borrowing a word from one to refer to the other is permissible. The cause is what directly brings about the ruling (e.g., purchase directly brings ownership), while the reason (sabab) leads to the ruling indirectly via a cause. The fiqh branch concerns oaths of emancipation: if a person says, 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' but intends ownership, or vice versa, the oath is valid religiously because the metaphorical relation holds. However, in court, the qadi does not accept the intended meaning because the legal effect differs: purchase does not require simultaneous ownership of all parts, while ownership does. Thus, if someone buys half a slave piecemeal, the condition of purchase is fulfilled gradually, but ownership of the entire slave is not achieved until all parts are owned at once. The mufti will follow the person's intention in giving a fatwa, but the qadi will not enforce it because the apparent wording controls judicial matters.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illa): That which necessitates the ruling by itself without any intermediary.
السبب (as-sabab): That which leads to the ruling by means of a cause that occurs between them.
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphorical borrowing of a word from its real meaning to another meaning due to a connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between 'illa (cause) and sabab (reason) according to the Hanafi usul as explained in this passage?
A) 'illa is broader than sabab
B) 'illa directly necessitates the ruling, while sabab leads to it via an intermediary
C) Sabab is the same as 'illa
D) 'illa is used only for contracts

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that cause and ruling can be used metaphorically for each other is valid religiously but not enforced judicially, due to the difference in legal consequences.

Third Division of Majaz: Interchanging Cause and Effect in Oaths
Explaining the third division of majaz with example of oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul rule of interchanging cause and effect in oaths and distinguish its application religiously versus judicially.

Core Idea
In oaths, cause and effect can be interchanged metaphorically, but the judge does not accept claims of metaphorical intent.

This passage explains the third division of majaz in Hanafi usul: the borrowing of cause for effect and vice versa. The author uses an example of an oath concerning buying or owning a slave to illustrate how the rule operates. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct fatwa and judicial practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base principle
Recognize that the third division of majaz allows interchanging cause (sabab) and effect (musabbab). In oaths, the word for purchase (cause) can be used to mean ownership (effect) and vice versa, provided the intention is sincere.
This is a form of isti'ara (metaphorical borrowing) recognized in Hanafi usul.
2
Analyze the example
Consider the two oath formulas: 'If I buy a slave, he is free' and 'If I own a slave, he is free'. The literal meaning of 'buy' requires purchase, while 'own' requires possession. Through majaz, one can intend the other meaning.
Purchase is the cause, ownership is the effect; in metaphor they can be exchanged.
3
Apply to partial purchase scenario
If someone buys half a slave, sells it, then buys the other half, in the purchase-based oath the second half is freed (since purchase is fulfilled incrementally), but in the ownership-based oath it is not (ownership requires simultaneous full possession).
This demonstrates that the literal meaning is considered when determining legal consequences.
4
Distinguish religious vs. judicial acceptance
The person's claim of metaphorical intent is accepted religiously (between him and Allah) but not judicially (in court) because the judge suspects a lie to avoid the oath. This is a key practical outcome.
Religious acceptance (dayanatan) means the person is not sinful; judicial rejection (qadan) means the legal ruling follows the literal meaning.
5
Note the exception for specified slaves
If the slave is specified (e.g., 'this slave'), the rule changes: both purchase and ownership are treated equally because the description of 'buying' or 'owning' is meaningless for a present, specified object.
The passage ends by noting this exception to prevent misunderstanding.

(القمر) في صورة الشراء: أي فيما إذا قال: "إن اشتريت عبدًا فهو حر، ثم اعلم أن هذا إذا كان الشراء صحيحًا، وأما إذا كان الشراء فاسدًا فلا يعتق وإن اشترى العبد جملة؛ لأن شرط الحنث قد تم قبل أن يقبضه، ولا ملك له في الشراء الفاسد قبل القبض، فينحل اليمين ولم يقع الجزاء لعدم المحل كذا في "التحقيق". (القمر) الملك: لأن الملك يوجب الاجتماع ولم يوجد.(المحشي) بأحدهما الآخر: أي بالشراء الملك وبالملك الشراء. (القمر) يصدق: إذا استفتى القائل عن جواب هذه الحادثة المفتي يفتي على وفق نيته. (القمر) ما نوى الشراء إلخ : أي قال: "إن ملكت إلخ، ونوى إن اشتريت إلخ.(القمر) بالملك: لأن الشراء لم يوجب الاجتماع. (المحشي ما نوى إلخ أي قال: "إن اشتريت" إلخ، ونوى به إن ملكت إلخ. (القمر) لا يصدقه أي إذا خاصم إليه العبد.(القمر) في هذا الأخير؛ لأنه نوى تخفيفًا عليه، فيصير متهما في هذه النية هكذا قالوا. واعترض عليه بأن في الصورة الأولى أيضًا تخفيفا عليه؛ لأن الملك كان أعم من أن يكون بالشراء، أو الهبة، أو بالوصية، أو بالإرث والشراء يختص بسبب معين منها، فينبغي أن لا يصدق قضاء في الأول أيضا، ولكن هذا لا يرد على المصنف؛ لأنه لم يتعرض لذكر القضاء، وهذا أي هذا الاعتراض كله إذا قال عبدا منكرا، أما إذا قيل: "هذا العبد فالملك والشراء سواء في أنه لا يشترط الاجتماع فيه؛ لأن التفرق والاجتماع وصف، والوصف في الحاضر لغو وفي الغائب معتبر.
Statement of the divisions of the third division: until when he says: 'If I buy a slave, he is free', and he intends by it ownership, or he says: 'If I own a slave, he is free', and he intends by it purchase, he is believed in both cases religiously. A branch for borrowing the cause for the ruling and its opposite. For purchase is the cause and ownership is the effect. And the principle in purchase is that it does not require gathering all in ownership. And the principle in ownership is that gathering is required customarily. If he buys half a slave and sells it, then buys the other half, this half is freed in the case of purchase, not in the case of ownership, considering the literal meaning. If he says: 'I meant one by the other', he is believed in both cases religiously due to the validity of borrowing. Due to the fulfillment of the condition, due to the non-fulfillment of the condition, so half of the remaining slave is freed in the case where he intends purchase by ownership, and it is not freed in the case where he intends ownership by purchase. But the judge does not believe him religiously: i.e., between him and Allah, not judicially. ... This is all if he said 'a slave' indefinitely. But if it is said: 'This slave', then ownership and purchase are equal in that gathering is not required in it, because separation and gathering are descriptions, and description in the present is meaningless, and in the absent is considered.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the third division of majaz (metaphor), specifically the borrowing of cause for effect and vice versa in oaths. The author gives an example: if someone says 'If I buy a slave, he is free' but intends ownership, or says 'If I own a slave, he is free' but intends purchase, he is believed religiously (between him and Allah) not judicially. The reason is that purchase is the cause and ownership is the effect; in metaphor, they can be interchanged. The rule is that purchase does not require the slave to be owned all at once, while ownership does. Thus, if someone buys a slave in parts, the second half is freed in the case where the oath was expressed as purchase (since purchase is fulfilled by buying even piecemeal), but not in the case where the oath was expressed as ownership (since ownership requires possession of the whole at once). The judge does not accept a claim of metaphorical intention in court because it could be a ruse to avoid the oath. This applies only when the slave is indefinite; if the slave is specified (as 'this slave'), then there is no issue because the description is meaningless in the present.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical use of a word in a sense other than its original meaning due to a relation.
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): A type of majaz where a word is borrowed from its original meaning to another based on similarity or relation, here cause for effect.
ديانة (dayanatan): Religiously; matters between the individual and Allah, based on sincerity.
قضاء (qadan): Judicially; matters decided by a judge based on apparent evidence, not inner intentions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the judge not accept the metaphorical intention in the case where the person says 'If I own a slave, he is free' but intends purchase?
A) Because metaphorical intention is invalid in Hanafi usul.
B) Because the person is suspected of lying to lighten the oath.
C) Because ownership and purchase are not related.
D) Because the slave is specified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, interchanging cause and effect in oaths is valid religiously but not judicially, maintaining a balance between sincerity and legal certainty.

The Second Type of Majaz: Sabab and Musabbab
Defining the second type of majaz relation and the term sabab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term sabab in Hanafi usul and distinguish it from 'illa and shart.

Core Idea
Sabab is a path to the ruling, not the direct cause; obligation and existence are attributed to the 'illa, not the sabab.

Majaz in usul al-fiqh is not just a linguistic metaphor; it is a real connection between terms and their legal effects. Among the types of majaz is the relation of sabab (cause) to musabbab (caused). The Hanafi scholars carefully distinguish sabab from 'illa (effective cause) and shart (condition). This passage explains that sabab is merely a path that leads to the ruling, while the ruling's obligation and existence are traced back to the 'illa, not to the sabab itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of sabab
Find the technical definition: sabab is 'that which is a path to the ruling and neither obligation nor existence is attributed to it.' Note the two negative attributions.
The wordings 'obligation' and 'existence' are deliberate; the first excludes 'illa, the second excludes shart.
2
Examine the example given
The example is the husband's statement 'You are free' (anta hurra). In Hanafi fiqh, this utterance is a sabab for divorce. Trace: the utterance is a path, but the actual effective cause ('illa) is the husband's intent or the removal of ownership of the neck (zawal milk al-raqaba).
The 'illa is the intermediary between the sabab and the ruling.
3
Distinguish sabab from 'illa
The text says: 'by the word obligation he avoids the effective cause' — meaning obligation is attributed to 'illa, not sabab. The sabab does not directly cause the obligation; it merely leads to it via the 'illa.
Example: The entry of time (sabab) leads to prayer obligation, but the 'illa is the prescribed time itself.
4
Distinguish sabab from shart
The text says: 'by the word existence he avoids the condition' — a condition (shart) enables existence but does not cause it. Sabab, unlike shart, is a path that actually leads to the ruling through an intermediary.
Example: Wudu is a shart for prayer, not a sabab; it enables permission but does not cause obligation.
5
Apply the definition to a new case
Consider the sale contract (al-bay'). In Hanafi usul, the offer and acceptance (sigha) are a sabab for ownership transfer. The 'illa is mutual consent; the shart is capacity. Verify where obligation and existence attach.
Practice: Identify which element is sabab, which is 'illa, and which is shart in a given transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الثاني اتصال السبب بالمسبب المراد بالسبب ما لا يكون علة أضيف إليها الحكم، وفي الاصطلاح ما يكون طريقا إلى الحكم ولا يضاف إليه وجوب ولا وجود، ما يكون طريقا إلخ كقوله: "أنت حرة" فإنه سبب للحكم، وطريق مفض إليه وهو زوال ملك المتعة، وليس بمضاف إليه، بل هو مضاف إلى علته وهو زوال ملك الرقبة وهذه العلة واسطة بين السبب والحكم. إليه: العائد يرجع إلى يرجع إلى "ما" وكذا ضمير "فيه" و "بينه". وجوب ولا وجود أي وجوب الحكم ولا وجوده قيل: بلفظ الوجوب احترز عن العلة، وبلفظ الوجود احترز عن الشرط.
And the second type: the connection of the cause to the caused. What is meant by cause is that which is not a cause to which the ruling is attributed, and in terminology, it is that which is a path to the ruling and neither obligation nor existence is attributed to it. It is a path, etc., like his saying: 'You are free' for it is a cause for the ruling, and a path leading to it, which is the removal of the ownership of enjoyment, and it is not attributed to it, but rather it is attributed to its effective cause which is the removal of ownership of the neck, and this effective cause is an intermediary between the cause and the ruling. ... Obligation and existence: i.e., the obligation of the ruling and its existence. It is said: by the word 'obligation' he avoids the effective cause, and by the word 'existence' he avoids the condition.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of majaz (metaphor) relation: the connection of the cause (sabab) to the caused (musabbab). Here, 'sabab' is not the effective cause ('illa) to which the ruling is directly attributed. In usul terminology, sabab is a path that leads to the ruling, but the ruling's obligation and existence are not attributed to it. An example is the husband's statement 'You are free' to his wife; this is a sabab for the ruling of talaq (divorce), but the actual effective cause is the husband's desire or the removal of ownership. The sabab (the utterance) is just a path. The commentary clarifies that by saying 'obligation' the author excludes the effective cause (which directly causes obligation), and by 'existence' he excludes the condition (which enables existence but is not the cause itself).

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): That which is a path to a ruling but to which the ruling's obligation or existence is not attributed.
المسبب (al-musabbab): The caused, i.e., the ruling or effect that results from the sabab through an intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is an example of sabab?
A) The husband's utterance 'You are free' in divorce.
B) The death of a person causing inheritance.
C) The entry of time for prayer making it obligatory.
D) The breaking of wudu by urination.

Key Takeaway
Sabab is a path to the ruling, distinct from 'illa and shart; the ruling's obligation is attributed to the 'illa, not the sabab.
