Preferring Haqiqah over Majaz in Oaths: The Hanafi Dispute
Explaining where literal and metaphorical meanings are abandoned for the original meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when haqiqah is still used and majaz is common, Abu Hanifa prioritizes haqiqah in oaths, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between his position and that of the two companions.

Core Idea
In oaths, if the literal meaning is still used, it prevails over a more common metaphorical meaning.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar addresses a nuanced situation in Hanafi usul: when a word has both a literal meaning (haqiqah) that is still in use and a metaphorical meaning (majaz) that is more common, which one governs an oath? Abu Hanifa holds that the literal remains primary, while his two companions give priority to the metaphorical. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly determining when an oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Recognize the scenario: the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word is still in use, but the metaphorical meaning (majaz) is more common (mutāraf). The rule for oaths in such a situation is disputed.
This is different from the case where haqiqah is abandoned entirely (hajr).
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa rules that the haqiqah prevails; the oath is broken only by the literal act. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the majaz prevails—or both meanings apply, depending on the narration.
The two companions have two narrations: one giving exclusive priority to majaz, the other giving both.
3
Examine the example: wheat
The example: 'I will not eat from this wheat.' The haqiqah is eating the grain itself (roasted, boiled, etc.), still in use. The majaz is eating bread, more common. Abu Hanifa: oath broken only by eating grain. Two companions: broken by eating bread (majaz) or either (both).
Also note the rule for 'min' (from): whether partitive or initiatory, it points to the grain itself.
4
Examine the example: Euphrates
Similarly, 'I will not drink from this Euphrates.' Haqiqah: drinking directly from the river by cupping (kar'). Majaz: drinking from a vessel filled with its water. Abu Hanifa: only kar' breaks oath. Two companions: vessel or both.
If one drinks from a branch of the Euphrates, the oath is not broken because the name 'Euphrates' no longer applies.
5
Apply to other cases
The principle extends to other oaths, and even to Quran recitation: the haqiqah of 'reading' is any recitation; majaz is a substantial amount. This leads to differing views on the minimum recitation in prayer.
The principle is that the original meaning is adhered to as long as it is feasible without hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان المواضع التي تترك فيها الحقيقة والمجاز ويصار إلى الأصل وإن كان مهجورًا شرعاً. وإذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة والمجاز متعارفًا فهي أولى عند أبي حنيفة رحله خلافاً لهما يعني ما ذكرنا سابقاً كان في الحقيقة المهجورة، وإن لم تكن مهجورة بل كانت مستعملة في العادة، ولكن كان المجاز متعارفًا غالب الاستعمال من الحقيقة، أو غالبًا في الفهم من اللفظ، فحينئذ الحقيقة أولى عند أبي حنيفة حلبه وعندهما المجاز فقط أولى في رواية، وعموم المجاز في رواية. كما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذه الحنطة أو لا يشرب من هذا الفرات.
ويصار إلخ تفريع على قوله: صار مقصودًا إلخ أي يصار إلى الأصل أي الحقيقة وإن كان الأصل مهجوراً شرعًا، ونظيره: ما إذا قال رجل: والله لأسرقن الليلة ينعقد اليمين وإن كانت السرقة حراما؛ لأن السرقة مقصودة باليمين فلا يلغو الكلام. (القمر)
فيصار إلى الأصل إلخ كمن حلف ليشر بن الخمر ينعقد اليمين وإن كان حرامًا شرعًا لصيرورة الشراب مقصود اليمين، فيحنث إن لم يشرب، قلت والأصل في إيراد النكرة والمعرفة أن اليمين إذا انعقدت على موصوف يتقيد بصفته معرفاً كان أو منكراً إن صلح الوصف أن يكون داعياً إلى اليمين كما إذا حلف لا يأكل رطبا أو هذا الرطب، فأكله بعد ما صار تمراً لا يحنث؛ لأن الرطوبة مضرة وإن لم يصلح كما إذا حلف لا يأكل من لحم هذا الحمل يحنث إذا أكل من لحمه كبشا؛ لأن الحمل أنفع منه فلم يصلح أن يتقيد اليمين به، وعلى هذا كان ينبغي يتقيد اليمين ههنا بوصف الصبا معرفاً كان الصبي أو منكراً؛ لأن وصف الصبا داع إلى الحلف لسفاهة الصبيان لكن ترك التقييد به لكون هجران الصبي حرامًا شرعًا إلا إذا أورد الحالف صبياً منكراً، فيعتبر التقييد به وإن كان حراما لكونه مقصوداً بالحلف؛ لأنه هو المعرف للمحلوف عليه كما في الحلف بشرب الخمر فتدبر. (السنبلي) ما ذكرنا من أن المصير إلى المجاز. (القمر) متعارفاً : اعلم أنه لم يذكر محمد تفسير المتعارف، فاختلف المشايخ في تفسيره فقال مشايخ بلخ: المراد من التعارف التعامل، وقال مشايخ العراق: المراد به التبادر والتفاهم، فأشار الشارح له إلى هذا الاختلاف بقوله : غالب إلخ. (القمر)
الحقيقة أولى لأن العمل بالأصل ممكن بلا مشقة، فلا يعدل إلى الحلف عند وجود الأصل. (القمر)
هذه من إلخ: سواء كان "من" للابتداء أو للتبعيض أما على الأول فظاهر؛ لأنه يقتضي أن يكون ابتداء الأكل الحنطة، وذلك إنما يكون بأكل عين الحنطة وكذا على الثاني: لأنه يقتضي أكل بعض الحنطة، وبعض الشيء ما يكون متصلاً به اتصال قرار وذلك إنما يكون بأكل عين الحنطة. (السنبلي)
فإن حقيقة الأول أن يأكل من عين الحنطة وهو مستعملة؛ لأنها تغلى وتقلى وتؤكل قضماً، ولكن المجاز وهو الخبز غالب الاستعمال في العادة، فعنده إنما يحنث إذا أكل من عين الحنطة، وعندهما يحنث إذا أكل من الخبز أو منهما بأن يراد باطنها، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحنث بالسويق أيضاً، ولكن لما كان جنساً آخر في العرف لم يعتبر. وحقيقة الثاني أن يشرب من الفرات بطريق الكرع وهي مستعملة كما هو عادة أهل البوادي، ولكن المجاز غالب الاستعمال وهو أن يشرب من غرف أو إناء يتخذ فيه الماء منها، فعنده يحنث بالكرع فقط، وعندهما بالإناء والغرف أو بهما وبالكرع جميعاً، ولو شرب من نهر منشعب من الفرات لا يحنث؛ لأنه انقطع اسم الفرات عنه، بخلاف ما إذا قيل: "من ماء الفرات"، الأول : أي :قوله يأكل من هذه الحنطة. (القمر) غالب الاستعمال وغالب في الفهم أيضاً، فإنه إذا قيل أهل بلد كذا يأكلون الحنطة يفهم منه أن طعامهم من أجزاء الحنطة لا من أجزاء الشعير. (القمر) يحنث إذا أكل إلخ فإنهما أخذا الحنطة مجازاً بمعنى الخبز. (القمر) وعندهما إلخ : قال ابن الملك شارح "المنار": وعلى هذا اختلف أبو حنيفة وصاحباه في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْرَأُوا مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ (المزمل: 20)، فإن له حقيقة ما يطلق عليه اسم القراءة، ومجازاً متعارفاً وهو ما يسمى قراءة عرفاً، فجوز أبو حنيفة بالله القراءة في الصلاة بآية قصيرة وجوزاها بآية طويلة. (السنبلي) بأن يراد أي على سبيل عموم المجاز. (القمر) وعلى هذا أي على عموم المجاز ينبغي أن يحنث بالسويق أيضاً أي عندهما؛ لأن السويق الحنطة من أجزاء باطنها وهذا اعتراض (القمر) جنساً :آخر أي من الخبز غير جنس الدقيق؛ ولهذا جوزا بيع الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً كذا قال ابن الملك. (القمر) الثاني: أي قوله: يشرب من هذا الفرات. (القمر) أن يشرب إلخ : فإن "من" ابتدائية، فالمعنى: لا يشرب مبتدئاً من هذا الفرات. (القمر) أن يشرب سواء كان "من" للابتداء أو للتبعيض كما قلنا في المسألة الأولى في حاشيتنا السؤال والجواب. (المحشي) الكرع هو أن يتناول الماء بفيه من موضع الماء. (القمر) غالب الاستعمال وغالب في الفهم أيضاً، فإنه إذا قيل: بنو فلان يشربون من هذا الفرات يفهم منه أنهم يشربون من ماء منسوب إليه. (القمر) بالإناء والغرف هذا على أخذ المجاز . (القمر) أو بهما وبالكرع هذا على أخذ عموم المجاز. (القمر)
Explanation of the places where the literal and metaphorical meanings are abandoned and one resorts to the original even if it is legally abandoned. And if the literal meaning is used and the metaphorical is common, then the literal is preferable according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions. What we mentioned previously was in the case of the abandoned literal. But if it is not abandoned but rather used customarily, but the metaphorical is common—more frequent in usage than the literal, or more frequent in understanding from the wording—then the literal is preferable according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two companions only the metaphorical is preferable in one narration, and the generality of the metaphorical in another narration. As when one swears not to eat from this wheat or not to drink from this Euphrates.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary discussing the third division of haqiqah and majaz: when the haqiqah is still in use but the majaz is more common (mutāraf). The rule is that Abu Hanifa prefers the haqiqah, while his two companions prefer the majaz in one narration, or give equal weight to both in another. The example given is an oath: if someone swears 'I will not eat from this wheat' or 'I will not drink from this Euphrates.' The commentators clarify that the literal meaning of eating wheat is eating the grain itself (roasted or boiled), while the metaphorical meaning is eating bread. Similarly, drinking from the Euphrates literally means drinking by cupping water directly from the river (commonly done in rural areas), while metaphorically it means drinking from a vessel filled from it. According to Abu Hanifa, the oath is violated only by the literal act; according to the two companions, the metaphorical act also breaks the oath. The passage also includes a discussion of the general principle regarding oaths: an oath can be valid even if the object is sinful (e.g., swearing to steal) because the act is the intended object of the oath. The commentators note that when the description in the oath is a motivation for the oath, it restricts the scope; but if the act is legally prohibited (like avoiding a child), the restriction may be waived unless the child is described in a way that emphasizes the prohibition. The passage ends with the Euphrates example, noting that if one drinks from a branch of the Euphrates, the oath is not broken because the name 'Euphrates' no longer applies. The commentary also applies this principle to Quran recitation: the literal is any recitation, the metaphorical is a substantial recitation, leading to differing views on the minimum required in prayer.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as established by original usage.
مجاز (majaz): The metaphorical or transferred meaning that is commonly understood and used.
متعارف (mutāraf): Commonly known and used; there is disagreement among scholars whether it refers to actual usage (shaykhs of Balkh) or immediate understanding (shaykhs of Iraq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears 'I will not eat from this wheat', under Abu Hanifa's position, what action would break the oath?
A) Eating bread made from that wheat
B) Eating the wheat grain itself (e.g., roasted)
C) Eating any food containing wheat
D) Both eating the grain and eating bread

Key Takeaway
In oaths, Abu Hanifa prioritizes the literal meaning (haqiqah) over the more common metaphorical (majaz) when the literal is still used, giving a different result than his two companions.

Abandoning Haqiqah and Majaz: Master's Acknowledgment of Paternity
Explaining when haqiqah and majaz are abandoned in a master's statement of paternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that impossibility of literal meaning does not necessarily block metaphorical interpretation, and distinguish between iqrar and insha' in a master's statement of paternity.

Core Idea
Impossibility of literal meaning does not block metaphor; the Sahibayn's condition is rebutted by the 'Zayd is a lion' analogy.

This passage explores a disputed usul rule: when a master says 'this is my son' to an older slave, both haqiqah (sonship) and majaz (freedom) seem impossible according to the Sahibayn. Abu Hanifa argues that the metaphorical meaning is still valid because the impossibility is not in the intended meaning. The passage also distinguishes whether the statement is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of prior freedom or a creation (insha') of freedom now, with practical consequences for the slave's mother's status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core case and the dispute
The case is a master saying 'this is my son' to a slave older than him. The dispute: Abu Hanifa holds it frees the slave (metaphor for freedom); Sahibayn hold it is invalid (laghw).
The age discrepancy makes literal sonship impossible.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's argument and the condition for majaz
Sahibayn argue that for a metaphor (majaz) to be valid, the literal meaning must be possible. Since sonship is impossible, the metaphor for freedom cannot stand, rendering the speech invalid.
This is their usul principle: possibility of haqiqah is a condition for majaz.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's rebuttal using the 'Zayd is a lion' example
Abu Hanifa counters that the condition is not absolute. The statement 'Zayd is a lion' is valid despite literal impossibility, because it is either a literal statement with deleted particle of similitude or a metaphor where impossibility is not intended. Thus, the master's statement can be a valid metaphor for freedom.
The text further distinguishes between cases where impossibility is intended (like 'Zayd is a lion') and where it is incidental (like 'I saw a lion shooting').
4
Distinguish iqrar vs insha' and the effect of coercion
If the statement is iqrar (acknowledgment of prior freedom), the slave is freed judicially and his mother becomes umm walad. If it is insha' (declaring freedom now), the slave is freed both judicially and religiously, but the mother does not become umm walad. Coercion invalidates iqrar but not insha', confirming the statement is iqrar.
The text sides with iqrar as 'more correct'.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
In practice, a mufti would rule that the slave is freed and the mother becomes umm walad if the statement is iqrar. If the statement was made under coercion, it is ineffective because iqrar is void under coercion.
The passage also mentions the possibility of metamorphosis (maskh) but dismisses it as far-fetched.

بيان أقسام القسيم الثالث
بيان المواضع التي تترك فيها الحقيقة والمجاز من حين ملكه؛ لأن الابن يكون حراً على الأب دائمًا، وعندهما: لما كانت الخلفية في الحكم وكان إمكان المعنى الحقيقي شرطًا لصحة المجاز لغا هذا الكلام؛ لأن البنوة من الأصغر سنا لا يمكن حتى يحمل على المجاز الذي هو العتق لا يقال: فينبغي أن يكون قوله: زيد أسد لغواً؛ لعدم إمكان الحقيقة؛ لأنا لا نسلم أنه مجاز بل حقيقة بحذف حرف التشبيه أي زيد كالأسد وأما قوله رأيت أسدًا يرمي؛ فإنه وإن كان مجازاً لكن المقصود بالحقيقة خبر من رجل الرؤية لا كونه أسدًا، حتى يلزم المحال قصدًا، وقيل: يمكن كونه أسدًا بالمسخ وهو بعيد.

Statement of the divisions of the third division. Statement of the places where literal and metaphorical meanings are abandoned from the moment of his ownership; because the son is always free with respect to the father. According to the two (companions): since the substitution (khalafiyya) is in the ruling, and the possibility of the literal meaning is a condition for the validity of the metaphor, this speech is invalid; because sonship from the younger in age is impossible, so it cannot be taken to the metaphor which is freeing. It cannot be said: then his statement 'Zayd is a lion' should be invalid due to impossibility of the literal meaning; because we do not accept that it is a metaphor, but rather it is literal by deletion of the particle of similitude, i.e., Zayd is like a lion. As for his statement 'I saw a lion shooting', although it is a metaphor, the intended meaning in truth is the report of seeing a man, not that he is a lion, such that impossibility would be intended. And it was said: it is possible for him to be a lion through metamorphosis (maskh), but that is far-fetched. From the moment of his ownership etc. So on this view, he is freed judicially. As for religiously (diyana), if the freeing actually occurred from him, then he is freed, otherwise not. Then the mother of this slave becomes an umm walad for him. This if this statement is an acknowledgment of freedom from the time of ownership as was said. But if it is not an acknowledgment but rather a creation of freeing in the manner of 'you are free' from the time of ownership as was said, then he is freed judicially and religiously, but his mother does not become an umm walad. The first (that it is an acknowledgment) is more correct. For if a man is coerced into saying 'this is my son' to his slave, it does not make him free; this is stated by Muhammad in [the chapter of] coercion. And coercion only prevents the validity of acknowledgment of freeing, not creation; thus it is known that it is an acknowledgment. (al-Sunbuli) al-Khalafiyya: i.e., the substitute of metaphor for literal meaning. (al-Qamar) This speech is invalid, i.e., his statement to the older slave 'this is my son'. (al-Qamar) which is freedom, contrary to when he says to the younger (in age) 'this is my son' while his lineage is known from another, it is permissible that he is created from his water through fornication or doubtful intercourse, and his lineage has become known from another. (al-Sunbuli) Then it should etc. The gist is that the statement of the two companions is contrary to the people of Arabic language; for according to their statement, 'Zayd is a lion' would be invalid due to impossibility of literal meaning, while they (the Arabists) say it is valid. (al-Qamar) this. 'For we do not accept etc.' is connected to the negation in his statement 'it cannot be said'. (al-Qamar) Not that he is a lion etc. I say: The intended meaning of the speech is seeing a lion that performs the act of shooting, not that the person is a lion, and that is possible; because there is no impossibility in the act of shooting being performed by him through training or otherwise, but it does not occur customarily; therefore they turned to metaphor. This is from some commentaries. And the author of al-Tawdih said in explaining the difference between something like 'Zayd is a lion' and 'I saw a lion shooting': the first includes a claim of something impossible intentionally, so it requires the estimation of a particle of similitude to exit from impossibility (i.e., correctness), unlike 'I saw a lion shooting', because although it includes affirming lion-ness for Zayd, it does not occur intentionally; rather the intention is only to affirm seeing, so it does not require estimating similitude for correction. (al-Sunbuli) 'Until the impossible is required' therein: speech that includes the impossible is false, whether the impossible is intended or not; therefore one must interpret that speech for its correction, so it was said. (al-Qamar) 'It is possible etc.' And such possibility suffices for resorting to metaphor. (al-Qamar) 'And it is far-fetched': perhaps the aspect of its being far-fetched is that there is no metamorphosis in this nation, and if metamorphosis were considered, then 'this is my son' pointing to the older one would not be invalid according to the two companions because it could be possible that he is his son through metamorphosis. Reflect. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a usul ruling discussing when both literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings are abandoned. The specific case is a master saying 'this is my son' to an older slave. Since the literal meaning (sonship) is impossible due to age, the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) holds that the metaphorical meaning (freedom) is adopted, and the slave is freed. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that both meanings are impossible because the metaphorical meaning (freedom) requires the possibility of the literal meaning as a condition, so the statement is invalid (laghw). The text defends Abu Hanifa by distinguishing this case from the metaphor in 'Zayd is a lion', which is actually a literal statement with a deleted particle of similitude. It also discusses whether the master's statement is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of prior freedom or a creation (insha') of freedom now. If it is an acknowledgment, the slave's mother becomes an umm walad; if creation, she does not. Coercion invalidates acknowledgment but not creation, confirming it is acknowledgment. The commentary elaborates on the distinction between different types of metaphor and addresses the rarity of metamorphosis.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqīqah): Literal meaning of a word, as originally assigned.
المجاز (al-majāz): Metaphorical meaning that is transferred from the literal meaning.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Acknowledgment of a pre-existing right or fact, such as prior freedom.
الإنشاء (al-inshā’): Creation of a legal effect directly through the utterance, e.g., declaring freeing now.
الخلفية (al-khalafiyyah): The substitution of metaphor for literal meaning in the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical difference between the master's statement being an iqrar versus an insha'?
A) Iqrar frees the slave, insha' does not.
B) Iqrar makes the mother umm walad, insha' does not.
C) Iqrar is not affected by coercion, insha' is.
D) Both have the same effect on the mother.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, impossibility of the literal meaning does not preclude resort to metaphorical meaning; the master's statement 'this is my son' to an older slave is an acknowledgment of freedom, freeing the slave and making his mother an umm walad.

When Both Haqiqah and Majaz Are Impossible: The Husband's Statement
Usul rule: impossibility of both haqiqah and majaz leads to laghw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition when both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible and derive that the speech is legally void (laghw), using the husband's statement as an example.

Core Idea
When a word's literal meaning is impossible and its metaphorical meaning is also impossible, the speech becomes legally void (laghw).

This passage examines a key usul principle: speech must be capable of conveying a possible meaning. When neither the literal nor the metaphorical sense of an utterance can apply in the given context, the statement has no legal effect. The classical example is a man telling his wife 'You are my daughter' when she is known to be unrelated and of similar age—neither literal daughterhood nor metaphorical divorce is legally possible, rendering the statement void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage states that when both literal and metaphorical meanings of a word are impossible in a given context, the speech becomes invalid (laghw) by necessity.
This principle is applied to the example of a husband telling his wife 'You are my daughter'.
2
Analyze the literal meaning impossibility
The literal meaning of 'daughter' is impossible because the wife has a known lineage and is of similar age or older, so she cannot be his biological daughter.
3
Analyze the metaphorical meaning impossibility
If 'daughter' were a metaphor for divorce, it would require a valid marriage to be in place. But daughterhood implies eternal prohibition, which invalidates any marriage, so divorce cannot occur. Thus the metaphorical meaning is also impossible.
4
Consider the exception
The text notes that if the husband insists on the statement, the judge may separate them, but the statement itself remains void.
This exception does not change the ruling that the speech is laghw.
5
Address the counterargument
A possible objection is that 'daughter' could be a metaphor for absolute prohibition (including divorce). The passage rebuts this by showing that such a metaphor would be mockery, similar to the Quranic verse 'give them tidings of a painful punishment' meaning 'warn them', but mockery is not valid legal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان تعذر الحقيقة والمجاز معاً
وقد تتعذر الحقيقة والمجاز معًا إذا كان الحكم ممتنعا يعني قد يتعذر المعنى الحقيقي والمعنى المجازي معًا إذا كان كلا الحكمين ممتنعا، فيلغو الكلام حينئذ بالضرورة. كما في قوله لامرأته: هذه بنتي وهي معروفة النسب وتولد لمثله أو أكبر سنا منه حتى لا تقع الحرمة بذلك أبدًا؛ فإنه إذا كانت المرأة معروفة النسب استحال أن تكون بنته وإن كانت أصغر سنًا منه، وكذا إذا كانت أكبر سنا منه فإنه استحال أن تكون بنته أبدا، فتعذر المعنى الحقيقي ظاهر، وأما تعذر المعنى المجازي، فلأنه لو كان مجازا لكان أنت طالق وهو باطل؛ لأن الطلاق يقتضي سابقية صحة النكاح والبنتية تقتضي أن تكون محرمة أبدا، فلا يقع بينه وبينها نكاح ولا طلاق فإذا لم يكن مجازا عنه فلا تقع قوله أنت طالق الحرمة بذلك القول أبدا، فيلغو الكلام إلا أنهم قالوا: إذا أصرّ على ذلك يفرق القاضي
الزوج
من قوله:
وقد تتعذر إلخ: أي يمتنع العمل بالحقيقة والمجاز ، وليس المراد بالتعذر ههنا مقابل المهجور. (القمر) إذا كان الحكم إلخ : أي يكون مفادًا للفظ ممتنعا في محل استعمل فيه اللفظ وإن كان ممكنا في محل آخر. (القمر)
الغير،
فيلغو إلخ لأن الكلام موضوع لإفادة المعنى، فإذا تعذر معناه الحقيقي والمجازي صار لغوا ضرورة. (القمر) وتولد لمثله أي حال كون زوجته بسن تولد مثلها لمثل هذا القائل (القمر) حتى لا تقع إلخ: وأما إذا قال لزوجته: أنت علي مثل أمي ونوى به الطلاق، فيقع الطلاق لا ؛ لأنه استعارة بل لأنه تشبيه في الحرمة. (القمر) أبدا إلخ سواء أصر على ذلك أو لا، أو لا، وسواء صدقته امرأته أو لا. (السنبلي ظاهر : فإن ثبوت النسب من وكبر السن مانع من أن يثبت النسب شرعًا من القائل. (القمر) لو كان مجازا لكان إلخ وجه الملازمة: أن التحريم الذي في وسع القائل ليس إلا التحريم بالطلاق، وأما التحريم المؤبد فليس في وسعه.(القمر) تقتضي أن تكون إلخ فتستدعى البنتية عدم صحة النكاح، فبين الطلاق والبنتية منافاة ولا استعارة مع التنافي، إلا تهكما واستهزاء كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَبَشِّرْهُمْ بِعَذَابٍ أَلِيمٍ (آل عمران:۲۱) أي أنذرهم، وفيه أن البنتية تستلزم الحرمة المؤبدة كما قلتم فتستلزم الحرمة المطلقة لاستلزام المقيد، فجاز أن تكون مجازا عن مطلق الحرمة، فيقع به الطلاق؛ لوجود مطلق الحرمة في الطلاق.
Statement of the impossibility of both literal and metaphorical meaning together. And it may happen that both literal and metaphorical meanings become impossible when the ruling is impossible, meaning that both the literal meaning and the metaphorical meaning may become impossible when both rulings are impossible. Then the speech necessarily becomes invalid. As in his statement to his wife: 'This is my daughter' while she is known in lineage and is of similar age or older such that prohibition never occurs by that. For if the woman is known in lineage, it is impossible for her to be his daughter even if she is younger in age, and likewise if she is older, it is impossible for her to be his daughter ever. So the impossibility of the literal meaning is apparent. As for the impossibility of the metaphorical meaning, because if it were a metaphor, it would be 'you are divorced', which is invalid; because divorce presupposes prior validity of marriage, while daughterhood requires that she be eternally prohibited. So between them there is neither marriage nor divorce. So if it is not a metaphor for that, then the statement 'you are divorced' does not occur, and prohibition never occurs by that statement. Thus the speech is invalid, except that they said: if he insists on that, the judge separates the husband from his statement. 'And it may become impossible etc.': i.e., acting upon the literal and metaphorical meanings is prevented, but the meaning here of 'impossibility' is not the opposite of 'abandoned' (mahjur). (al-Qamar) 'When the ruling etc.': i.e., the import of the word becomes impossible in the place where the word is used, even if it is possible in another place. (al-Qamar) 'the other' (al-ghayr). So it becomes invalid etc., because speech is intended to convey meaning; so when its literal and metaphorical meanings become impossible, it becomes necessarily invalid. (al-Qamar) 'And is born for his like': i.e., the condition that his wife is of an age that one like her is born to someone like this speaker. (al-Qamar) 'So that prohibition never occurs etc.': And if he says to his wife 'You are like my mother to me' and intends divorce, divorce occurs, not because it is a metaphor, but because it is a similitude in prohibition. (al-Qamar) 'Ever etc.': whether he insists on that or not, and whether his wife believes him or not. (al-Sunbali) 'Apparent: for the establishment of lineage and old age prevent the lineage from being established legally from the speaker. (al-Qamar) 'If it were a metaphor, it would be etc.': the aspect of the implication is that the prohibition that is within the speaker's capacity is only prohibition by divorce; as for eternal prohibition, it is not within his capacity. (al-Qamar) 'Requires that she be etc.': so daughterhood entails the invalidity of marriage, so between divorce and daughterhood there is contradiction, and no metaphor with contradiction, except as mockery and jest, as in the Almighty's statement "But give them tidings of a painful punishment" (Quran 3:21) meaning warn them. And it includes that daughterhood entails eternal prohibition as you said, so it entails absolute prohibition because the conditioned entails the condition; so it is permissible for it to be a metaphor for absolute prohibition, so divorce occurs through it because absolute prohibition exists in divorce.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a usul ruling explaining when both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible together. The example is a man telling his wife 'you are my daughter'. If she is known to be from another family and of similar age or older, literal meaning (biological daughter) is impossible. The metaphorical meaning (divorce) is also impossible because divorce requires a valid marriage, but if she were his daughter, marriage would be eternally prohibited and thus invalid. Therefore, the statement is void (laghw) with no legal effect. However, the text notes an exception: if the man insists on that statement, the judge may separate them. The commentary clarifies that the impossibility here is specific to this context (the ruling is impossible in this situation, though possible elsewhere). It also distinguishes this case from saying 'you are like my mother' which can be a valid metaphor for divorce because resemblance in prohibition is possible. The discussion includes a counterargument that daughterhood might be a metaphor for absolute prohibition (including divorce), but this is rejected because it would lead to mockery, unlike the Quranic usage of 'give tidings of punishment' meaning 'warn'. Overall, the passage demonstrates the principle that speech must convey a possible meaning, and when neither literal nor metaphorical sense is possible, it is legally meaningless.

Key Terms
تعذر (ta‘adhdhur): Impossibility of applying the meaning (literal or metaphorical) due to the ruling being impossible in that context.
لغو (laghw): Speech that is legally invalid and without effect because it conveys no possible meaning.
البنتية (al-bintiyyah): The legal relationship of daughterhood, which entails eternal prohibition of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the metaphorical meaning of 'you are my daughter' (as divorce) impossible?
A) Because divorce requires a valid marriage, but daughterhood implies eternal prohibition of marriage.
B) Because the wife might be younger, making the metaphor offensive.
C) Because the word 'daughter' has no metaphorical use in Arabic.
D) Because the husband cannot divorce his daughter.

Key Takeaway
If both the literal and metaphorical meanings of an utterance are impossible in context, the speech is legally void (laghw).

Indicators for Preferring Majaz over Haqiqah
Explains five qara'in for preferring majaz over haqiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five indicators for preferring majaz over haqiqah as stated by the author, and evaluate the commentator's critique that they reduce to custom ('adah).

Core Idea
Haqiqah is abandoned only when a qarina like custom makes it impossible; the Hanafi school prioritizes literal meaning unless custom dictates otherwise.

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary examines the types of indicators (qara'in) that necessitate abandoning the literal meaning (haqiqah) in favor of a metaphorical meaning (majaz). The author of the original matn lists five such indicators, but the commentator critically analyzes this classification, arguing that most of them are actually subsumed under custom ('adah). Understanding this debate is key to applying the Hanafi principle that haqiqah is the default unless a clear qarina—typically derived from custom—overrides it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Qara'in
List the five indicators for leaving haqiqah as given by the author: custom, the word itself, context of speech, speaker's state, and place of speech. Note that the commentator says these are according to the author's claim.
The five are: 1) دلالة العادة, 2) دلالة اللفظ في نفسه, 3) دلالة سياق النظم, 4) دلالة حال المتكلم, 5) دلالة محل الكلام.
2
Analyze the Commentator's Critique
Understand why al-Qamar argues that indicators 2-5 are actually included under custom. For example, the word itself (like 'fish seller' not being called 'meat seller') relies on conventional usage. Similarly, context and speaker's state depend on customary understanding.
The commentator states: 'because his statement: indication of the word itself is included in indication of custom... and likewise his statement: indication of the context of the phrasing is also included.'
3
Examine the Alternative Fourfold Classification
Consider the classification from other books that reduces qara'in to four: intellect, senses, general custom, and specific custom. Examples from the Quran illustrate how intellect prevents literal interpretation of commands.
The four are: عقل, حس, عرف عام, عرف خاص.
4
Grasp the Concept of Mushakkak
Understand that 'indication of the word itself' involves words with gradation (mushakkak), where strength or deficiency in the meaning affects its application. This is determined by the word's derivation, not context.
Mushakkak causes doubt for the listener because individuals vary in the intensity of the attribute.
5
Apply the Principle: When is Haqiqah Left?
Apply the rule that haqiqah is left only when a clear qarina (primarily custom) makes literal meaning impossible. For example, a vow of prayer is interpreted as ritual prayer due to Islamic custom, but for someone unaware of the custom, the linguistic meaning (supplication) applies.
The passage states: 'if the vow-maker is not a person of custom in the legal meaning... his vow should be referred to language.'

(القمر) على ما زعمه إلخ إنما قال الشارح ذلك أي خمسة على ما زعمه؛ لأن قوله: دلالة اللفظ في نفسه داخل في دلالة العادة كما أشار الشارح إليه بقوله : ولأن بائع السمك لا يسمى في العرف بائع اللحم، وكذا قوله: بدلالة سياق النظم أيضا داخل في دلالة العادة كما أوماً الشارح أيضًا؛ لأن هذا الكلام إنما يقع عند إرادة عجز المخاطب، والمراد بالعادة في قول المصنف: العرف سواء كان عاما أو خاصا كما بينه الشارح، فلم يكن الأقسام خمسة في الواقع. وقال في بعض الكتب : اعلم أن القرينة المانعة عن إرادة الحقيقة على أربعة أقسام: العقل والحس، والعرف العام والعرف الخاص، الأول كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاسْتَفْزِزْ مَنِ اسْتَطَعْتَ إلخ (الإسراء: ٦٤) فههنا العقل مانع عن حقيقة الأمر، فإن الله حكيم لا يأمر بالغي، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرُ﴾ (الكهف: (۲۹) إلخ، والثاني كقول القائل: "لا يأكل من هذه النخلة، ولا يشرب من هذه البئر"، أما الثالث فكقوله : "لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان، ونحو يمين الفور وغيره وأما الرابع كما في النذر بالصلاة والحج، والتوكيل بالخصومة وبعد معرفة ذلك ظهر لك ما في عبارة المتن من الخلل والزلل، ولعل الشارح أراد ذلك بقوله : على ما زعمه والله اعلم (السنبلي) تترك أي بلا نية من المتكلم. (القمر) بدلالة العادة أي العادة في استعمال الألفاظ وفهم المعنى منها ، ثم اعلم أنه إنما تركت الحقيقة بدلالة العادة؛ لأن الكلام موضوع للإفهام، فإذا كان مستعملاً لشيء عرفا، ونقل عن معناه اللغوي، فهذه العادة أي عادة الاستعمال رجحت إرادته فيترك معناه الحقيقي. ثم اعلم أن ترك الحقيقة بدلالة العادة مقيد بما إذا لم يكن الحقيقة مستعملة؛ إذ لو كانت الحقيقة مستعملة كانت أولى عند الإمام من المجاز المتعارف على ما مر . (القمر) كالنذر إلخ فإنه محمول على ما هو معتاد في الشرع، فلو كان الناذر غير صاحب عادة بالمعنى الشرعي وغير عالم به، بل كان من أهل الحرب، فينبغي أن يتصرف نذره إلى اللغة كذا قيل. (القمر)

فإن قال أحد: "لله على أن أصلى" تجب عليه الصلاة لا الدعاء، وكذا الحج لغة القصد مطلقًا ثم نقل في الشرع إلى المناسك المعهودة في مكة، فلو قال: "الله على أن أحج" تجب عليه العبادة المعهودة، وفي حكمها سائر الألفاظ المنقولة شرعًا أو عرفًا عامًا وخاصا، وكذا قوله: "لا" يضع قدمه في "دار فلان" على ما مر .
وبدلالة اللفظ في نفسه أي باعتبار مأخذ اشتقاقه ومادة حرفه لا باعتبار إطلاقه بأن كان اللفظ مثلاً موضوعًا لمعنى فيه قوة، فيخرج ما وجد فيه ذلك المعنى ناقصا، أو لمعنى فيه نقصان وضعف، فيخرج ما وجد فيه ذلك المعنى زائدا ويسمى هذا مشككا، وعبر عنه صاحب التوضيح : بكون بعض الأفراد فيه زائدا أو ناقصاً.
إلى الأركان المعلومة من القيام والقراءة وغيرهما. (القمر) تجب عليه الصلاة إلخ فإن عادة أهل الإسلام أن ينذروا العبادة المعهودة من الطواف والسعي وغيرهما لا الدعاء. (القمر) تجب عليه العبادة المعهودة إلخ فإن عادة أهل الإسلام أن ينذروا العبادة المعهودة لا القصد. لا يضع إلخ: فالمعنى الحقيقي وهو وضع القدم حافيًا ترك، والمتعارف معتادًا هو المعنى المجازي وهو الدخول. (القمر) في نفسه أي لا بالنظر إلى السياق والسباق والعادة. (القمر)
أو لمعنى إلخ معطوف على قوله : لمعنى إلخ. (القمر)

Statement of the divisions of the third division. Statement of the indicators for acting with majaz and leaving haqiqah. Then the author proceeded after that to explain the indicators for acting with majaz and leaving haqiqah, and they are five according to his claim. Statement of the indicators for acting with majaz and leaving haqiqah: He said: haqiqah is left by the indication of custom ('adah) like a vow for prayer and Hajj, for prayer in language is supplication. They are five: indication of custom, indication of the word itself, indication of the context of the phrasing, indication of the speaker's state, and indication of the place of speech. (al-Qamar) 'According to his claim' contains an allusion that there is discussion about the five as the commentator will say. (al-Qamar) 'According to his claim' etc.: the commentator only said that, i.e., five according to his claim, because his statement: indication of the word itself is included in indication of custom as the commentator pointed to with his statement: because a fish seller is not called in custom a meat seller. And likewise his statement: indication of the context of the phrasing is also included in indication of custom as the commentator also indicated; because this speech only occurs when intending inability of the addressee, and what is meant by custom in the author's statement is 'urf, whether general or specific, as the commentator explained. So the divisions were not five in reality. And it is said in some books: Know that the qarina preventing intending haqiqah is of four types: intellect, sense, general custom, and specific custom. The first is like His statement: 'And disturb whomever you can' (Quran 17:64) — here intellect prevents the reality of the command, for Allah is wise and does not command immorality, and likewise His statement: 'So whoever wills let him believe and whoever wills let him disbelieve' (Quran 18:29). The second is like someone's saying: 'Do not eat from this palm tree and do not drink from this well.' As for the third, it is like his statement: 'Do not put his foot in so-and-so's house' and similar to an immediate oath and others. As for the fourth, it is like in a vow for prayer and Hajj, and authorization for litigation. After knowing that, what is in the text of the matn of defect and error appears to you, and perhaps the commentator intended that by his statement: 'according to his claim' — and Allah knows best. (al-Sunbuli) 'Left' i.e., without intention from the speaker. (al-Qamar) 'By indication of custom' i.e., custom in using words and understanding meaning from them. Then know that haqiqah is only left by indication of custom because speech is intended for understanding. So if something is used customarily and transferred from its linguistic meaning, then this custom — i.e., the custom of usage — gives weight to its intended meaning, so its real meaning is left. Then know that leaving haqiqah by indication of custom is conditioned upon haqiqah not being used; for if haqiqah were used, it would have priority according to Imam Abu Hanifa over conventional majaz as has passed. (al-Qamar) 'Like a vow' etc.: it is carried upon what is customary in the Law. So if the vow-maker is not a person of custom in the legal meaning and not aware of it, but rather was from the people of war, then his vow should be referred to language — thus it is said. (al-Qamar) Statement of the divisions of the third division as in His statement: 'O you who believe, send blessings upon him' (Quran 33:56) and his statement: 'And when he is fasting, let him pray.' (al-Qamar) 'Let him pray' i.e., let him supplicate. Then it was transferred to the known pillars and the customary worship, and its first meaning was abandoned, i.e., supplication. So if someone says: 'For Allah upon me that I pray' — prayer is obligatory upon him, not supplication. And likewise Hajj in language is absolute intending, then it was transferred in the Law to the customary rites in Mecca. So if he says: 'For Allah upon me that I perform Hajj' — the customary worship is obligatory upon him. And in its ruling are all other words transferred by Law or general or specific custom. And likewise his statement: 'Do not put his foot in so-and-so's house' as has passed. 'And by indication of the word itself' i.e., considering the origin of its derivation and the material of its letters, not considering its unrestricted application — such that the word is, for example, placed for a meaning in which there is strength, so that which is found in that meaning deficient is excluded; or for a meaning in which there is deficiency and weakness, so that which is found in that meaning excessive is excluded; and this is called mushakkak. And the author of al-Tawdih expressed it as 'some individuals being excessive or deficient.' (al-Qamar) 'To the known pillars' i.e., of standing, recitation, and others. (al-Qamar) 'Prayer is obligatory upon him' etc.: because the custom of the people of Islam is that they vow the customary worship of tawaf, sa'i, and others, not supplication. (al-Qamar) 'The customary worship is obligatory upon him' etc.: because the custom of the people of Islam is that they vow the customary worship, not mere intending. 'Do not put' etc.: the real meaning, i.e., placing the foot barefoot, is abandoned, and the conventionally customary meaning is the metaphorical one, i.e., entering. (al-Qamar) 'In itself' i.e., not considering the context and sequence and custom. (al-Qamar) 'Or for a meaning' etc. is conjoined to his statement 'for a meaning' etc. (al-Qamar) 'And this is called mushakkak' because of the variation of individuals by excess and deficiency. (al-Qamar) 'Mushakkak' etc.: it is only called that because it casts doubt on the listener as to whether it is shared due to the difference of its individuals. (al-Sunbuli) 'Excessive or deficient' — some individuals in strength are at a level as if they are not individuals of it, and some individuals in weakness are at a level as if they are not individuals of it. (al-Qamar) Muslim narrated in his Sahih, no. 1431, chapter on the command to respond to the inviter to a meal, from Abu Hurairah who said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'When one of you is invited, let him respond; if he is fasting, let him pray; and if he is not fasting, let him eat.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) on a matn that discusses the indicators (qara'in) for when the real (haqiqah) meaning of a word is abandoned in favor of a metaphorical (majaz) meaning. The author of the matn claims there are five types of such indicators: (1) custom ('adah), (2) the word itself (dalalah al-lafz fi nafsihi), (3) context of speech (siyaq al-nazm), (4) speaker's state (hal al-mutakallim), and (5) place of speech (mahall al-kalam). The commentator (al-Qamar) criticizes this classification, arguing that types 2-5 are actually subsumed under custom ('adah) because they are all based on conventional usage. He cites a view from some books that reduces the indicators to four: intellect, senses, general custom, and specific custom, with examples from Quran and everyday speech. The key point is that haqiqah is left only when a clear qarina — such as custom — makes it impossible to intend the real meaning. For example, when someone vows to pray (salah), the shari'ah meaning of salah (the ritual prayer) is intended, not its linguistic meaning (supplication). Similarly, when one says 'don't put your foot in so-and-so's house,' the custom is to interpret it as 'don't enter,' not the literal act. The passage also introduces the concept of mushakkak (gradated terms), where a word applies to different individuals with varying degrees of strength or deficiency, and this is determined by the word's derivation and root letters, not context.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The original, literal meaning of a word as defined by its linguistic origin.
مجاز (majaz): The metaphorical or transferred meaning of a word, used when the literal meaning is impossible due to a qarina.
قرينة (qarina): An indicator that prevents intending the literal meaning and necessitates the metaphorical meaning.
عادة (‘adah): Custom or convention in speech usage, either general ('urf 'amm) or specific ('urf khass).
مشكك (mushakkak): A word that has different grades of application to its individuals, causing doubt about whether it applies equally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on haqiqah when the custom of usage is established?
A) Haqiqah is always preferred, regardless of custom.
B) Haqiqah is left and the metaphorical meaning is taken, provided the haqiqah is not itself in use.
C) Custom only applies if there is a specific legal text.
D) Haqiqah and majaz are equally valid, and the interpreter may choose.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah is the default meaning, but a clear qarina—most notably custom—can override it, provided the literal meaning is not actively in use.

Indications for Figurative Meaning in Hanafi Usul
Defining and exemplifying qara'in for figurative meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five categories of qara'in (indications) for figurative meaning in Hanafi usul and apply them to determine whether literal or figurative meaning is intended in a given text.

Core Idea
A qarina is required to shift from haqiqah to majaz; without it, the literal meaning holds.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar systematically outlines the indications (qara'in) that guide the interpreter from a word's literal meaning to its figurative meaning. Understanding these categories is crucial for correct istinbat (derivation) of rulings, especially when dealing with oaths and legal language. We will walk through the examples and classifications as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the default: haqiqah
Every word is presumed to be used in its original literal meaning (haqiqah) unless there is an indication (qarina) to the contrary. This is the starting point for interpretation.
2
Identify transferred terms (manqul)
Words like 'salah' and 'hajj' were transferred from their linguistic meaning to a Shariah-specific meaning. In oaths or vows, the Shariah meaning applies because of the qarina of customary usage among Muslims.
This is a type of qarina based on custom ('urf).
3
Understand mushtakkik words
Some words, like 'lahm' (meat), are derived from a root that implies strength or a quality. Their application varies: individuals that lack that quality are excluded from the word's scope. This creates ambiguity (tashkik) and requires careful analysis.
The derivation (ishtiqaq) is a key tool in Hanafi usul.
4
Classify the five types of qara'in
From the passage, extract the five categories: external to speaker and speech, attribute of speaker, word external to speech, word identical to speech, and word part of speech. Each provides a different kind of clue that figurative meaning is intended.
Memorize these categories with examples from the text.

بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث فالأول كما إذا حلف لا يأكل لحما ، فلا يتناول لحم السمك، وقوله : "كل مملوك لي حر لا يتناول المكاتب"، فإن اللحم لا يتناول السمك؛ إذ هو مشتق من الالتحام وهو الشدة، ولا شدة بدون الدم والسمك لا دم فيه؛ لأن الدموي لا يسكن الماء ولا
يعيش فيه، فلا يتناول هذا الحلف لحم السمك، وإن كان أطلق عليه في القرآن في قوله
اللحم
:تعالى: ﴿لِتَأْكُلُوا مِنْهُ لَحْماً طَريا و به تمسك مالك لله في أنه يحنث بأكل لحم السمك،
(النحل: ١٤) قوله تعالى
ونحن نقول : لا يحنث به لأجل مأخذ اللفظ، ولأن بائعه لا يسمى في العرف بائع اللحم،
فالأول: أي ما إذا كان اللفظ موضوعًا لمعنى فيه قوة. (القمر) فلا يتناول لحم السمك: هذا إذا لم ينو شيئًا، وأما إذا نوى تناول لحم السمك، فيتناوله كذا قيل. (القمر) إذ هو مشتق إلخ: يعني أن اللحم مأخوذ من الالتحام يقال التحم العرب أي اشتد فسمي اللحم بهذا الاسم؛ لما فيه من الشدة، ولا شدة بدون الدم الذي هو أقوى الأخلاط في الحيوان والسمك لا دم فيه وما يسيل عنه عند الشق، فذلك ليس بدم إنما هو ماء أحمر ويطلق عليه الدم مجازا؛ لأن الدموي إلخ. (القمر) في قوله تعالى إلخ لتَأْكُلُوا مِنْهُ أي من البحر ولَحْماً طَرِيَّا (النحل: ١٤) هو السمك ووصفه بالطراوة؛ لأنه أرطب اللحوم، فيسرع إليه الفساد فيسارع إلى أكله كذا قال البيضاوي (القمر) يحنث إلخ فإن مطلق اسم اللحم يتناوله. (القمر) لأجل مأخذ اللفظ إلخ والتفصيل لقرائن المجاز: أن القرينة للمجاز إما خارجة عن المتكلم والكلام أي لا يكون صفة للمتكلم، ولا من جنس الكلام كدلالة الحال نحو يمين الفور وكدلالة العرف والعادة، ودلالة الحس، أو يكون صفة للمتكلم نحو: ﴿وَاسْتَفْزِزْ مَنِ اسْتَطَعْتَ (الإسراء: ٦٤) فإن الله لا يأمر بالمعصية، أو يكون لفظا خارجا عن الكلام الذي فيه المجاز كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ﴾ (الكهف: ٢٩)، فإن حقيقة هذا الكلام للتخيير لكن قوله تعالى بعد ذلك: ﴿إِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَاراً (الكهف:٢٩)، قرينة مانعة ذلك، أو يكون لفظًا هو عين هذا الكلام نحو الأعمال بالنيات"، فإن القرينة مجموع هذا الكلام ثبوت النية لجميع الأعمال، أو يكون لفظا هو جزء هذا الكلام، وهو على قسمين: الأول أن يكون بعض الأفراد أولى بأن يكون ناقصا كما في قوله "كل" مملوك لي حر"، فإنه لا يتناول المكاتب؛ لأنه ناقص في المملوكية، أو يكون زائدا كما في قوله لا يأكل الفاكهة"، فإنه لا يتناول العنب؛ لأنه زائد على معنى التفكه، والثاني: أن
عن
لا يكون بعض الأفراد أولى كما في قوله: "لا يضع قدمه في دار "فلان" هكذا في "التوضيح". (السنبلي)
يعني
إسناد
Explanation of indications for using figurative meaning and leaving literal meaning. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: like His statement, 'O you who have believed, bless him' and his statement, 'And when he is fasting' (al-Ahzab:56). So let him pray, i.e., supplicate, then it was transferred to the known pillars and the established worship, and abandoned its first meaning, i.e., supplication. So if someone says: 'I vow to Allah that I will pray' (using salat), the obligation is the prayer (salat), not supplication. Similarly, Hajj linguistically is absolute intention, then it was transferred in the Shariah to the established rites in Mecca. So if he says: 'I vow to Allah that I will perform Hajj', the established worship is obligatory. And in its ruling are all other words transferred by Shariah or common usage, general and specific. And similarly his statement: 'He will not place his foot in the house of so-and-so', as previously mentioned. And by the indication of the word in itself, i.e., considering the origin of its derivation and the matter of its letters, not considering its application, such that the word is, for example, coined for a meaning that has strength, so that which is found in that meaning deficient is excluded; or for a meaning that has deficiency and weakness, so that which is found in that meaning excessive is excluded. This is called mushtakkik (equivocal). And the author of al-Tawdih expressed it as some individuals in it being excessive or deficient. (al-Qamar) 'to the known pillars of standing and recitation etc.' (al-Qamar) 'the prayer is obligatory upon him etc.' For the custom of the people of Islam is that they vow the established worship of tawaf and sa'i etc., not supplication. (al-Qamar) 'the established worship is obligatory upon him etc.' For the custom of the people of Islam is that they vow the established worship, not intention. 'He will not place etc.': So the literal meaning, which is placing the foot barefoot, is abandoned, and what is conventionally accustomed is the figurative meaning, which is entering. (al-Qamar) 'in itself i.e., not by consideration of context and precedence and custom.' (al-Qamar) 'or for a meaning etc.' conjunct to his statement 'for a meaning etc.' (al-Qamar) 'and this is called mushtakkik: due to variation of individuals by excess and deficiency.' (al-Qamar) 'mushtakkik etc.: It is only called so because it casts doubt on the hearer as to whether it is homonymous due to the difference of its individuals.' (al-Sanbali) 'excessive or deficient: So some individuals are in strength to a degree as if they are not an individual of it, and some individuals are in weakness to a degree as if they are not an individual of it.' (al-Qamar) Muslim narrated in his Sahih, number 1431, chapter on the command to answer the caller to an invitation, from Abu Huraira who said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'When any of you is invited, let him respond. If he is fasting, let him pray (i.e., supplicate), and if he is not fasting, let him eat.' 315 Explanation of indications for using figurative meaning and leaving literal meaning. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: first, like if he swears that he will not eat meat, then it does not include fish meat. And his statement: 'Every slave I own is free' does not include the mukatab. For meat does not include fish, because it is derived from al-tiham which is strength, and there is no strength without blood, and fish has no blood, because the blooded creature does not dwell in water and does not live in it. So this oath does not include fish meat, even though it is called meat in the Quran in His statement: 'that you may eat from it fresh meat' (al-Nahl:14). And Malik held by it that he breaks his oath by eating fish meat. And we say: He does not break his oath by it because of the derivation of the word, and because its seller is not called in custom a seller of meat. So the first: i.e., when the word is coined for a meaning that has strength. (al-Qamar) 'does not include fish meat: This is if he did not intend anything. But if he intended to eat fish meat, it includes it, as it is said.' (al-Qamar) 'because it is derived etc.: meaning that meat is taken from al-tiham. It is said 'the Arabs coalesced' i.e., they became strong, so meat was named this name because of the strength in it. And there is no strength without blood, which is the strongest of humors in animals, and fish has no blood. What flows from it when cut is not blood but red water, and it is called blood metaphorically, because the blooded creature etc.' (al-Qamar) 'in His statement etc. 'that you may eat from it fresh meat' (al-Nahl:14): it is fish, and He described it as fresh because it is the moistest of meats, so it quickly goes bad, so (people) hasten to eat it. Thus said al-Baydawi. (al-Qamar) 'he breaks his oath etc.: For the absolute name of meat includes it.' (al-Qamar) 'because of the derivation of the word etc. And the detail of indications for figurative meaning is: the indication (qarina) for figurative meaning is either external to the speaker and the speech, i.e., it is not a description of the speaker nor of the type of speech, like indication of state such as an immediate oath, and like indication of custom and habit, and indication of sense; or it is a description of the speaker, like: 'and incite whomever you can' (al-Isra:64), for Allah does not command sin; or it is a word external to the speech containing the figurative meaning, like His statement: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' (al-Kahf:29), for the literal meaning of this speech is choice, but His statement after that: 'Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire' (al-Kahf:29) is an indication preventing that; or it is a word that is exactly this speech, like 'Actions are by intentions', for the indication is the totality of this speech establishing intention for all actions; or it is a word that is part of this speech, and it has two divisions: first, that some individuals are more likely to be deficient, like in his statement 'Every slave I own is free', it does not include the mukatab because he is deficient in slavery; or excessive, like in his statement 'He does not eat fruit', it does not include grapes because they are excessive to the meaning of fruity; second, that some individuals are not more likely, like in his statement 'He will not place his foot in the house of so-and-so'. Thus in al-Tawdih. (al-Sanbali) 'meaning attribution'?
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar, a Hanafi usul text, explains the indications (qara'in) that lead us to interpret a word figuratively rather than literally. It begins by stating the topic: explaining the indications for using figurative meaning (majaz) and leaving the literal meaning (haqiqah). The author then discusses the third division of the subject, providing examples from the Quran and hadith. For instance, the word 'salah' originally meant supplication, but in Islamic law it was transferred to the specific ritual prayer. So if someone vows to perform 'salah', the ritual prayer is obligatory, not just supplication. Similarly, 'hajj' linguistically means 'intention' but in Shariah refers to the pilgrimage rites. The same applies to other terms transferred by Shariah or custom. Then the passage introduces the concept of 'mushtakkik' (equivocal words) where the strength or weakness of the meaning varies among individuals. For example, the word 'meat' (lahm) is derived from 'al-tiham' (strength), requiring blood, so fish is not included in an oath to avoid meat because fish has no blood. However, Imam Malik holds that such an oath is broken by eating fish because the Quran calls fish 'fresh meat'. The Hanafi response is that the derivation and custom ('urf) exclude fish; sellers of fish are not called 'meat sellers'. The passage then systematically categorizes the indications (qara'in) for figurative meaning. Qara'in can be external to the speaker and speech (like context, custom, or sensory evidence), attributes of the speaker (like when Allah addresses the Prophet in a way that cannot be literal), words external to the speech (like contextual verses that clarify), words that are identical to the speech (like 'actions are by intentions'), or words that are part of the speech. The latter is further divided: either some individuals are more prone to deficiency (like a mukatab slave not being fully owned) or excess (like grapes being beyond common 'fruit'), or no individual is more likely (like the expression 'placing a foot' meaning entering). This detailed analysis shows the Hanafi method of combining linguistic derivation, custom, and context to determine legal meaning.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as coined in the language.
مجاز (majaz): The figurative, transferred meaning of a word, used when a qarina prevents the literal meaning.
قرينة (qarina): An indication or contextual clue that shifts interpretation from literal to figurative.
مشكك (mushtakkik): An equivocal word where individuals of the meaning vary in strength or weakness, causing doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position discussed, if someone swears 'I will not eat meat', and then eats fish, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because fish is called meat in the Quran.
B) No, because the word 'meat' is derived from strength that requires blood, which fish lacks.
C) Yes, because all oaths are interpreted literally.
D) No, because the swearer did not intend fish.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a clear qarina is essential to shift from literal to figurative interpretation; without it, the literal meaning remains operative.

Contextual Indicators (Qara'in) for Preferring Metaphor over Literal Meaning
Explaining the contextual indicators that shift meaning from literal to metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the five types of contextual indicators (qara'in) that shift a word from literal to metaphorical meaning, and apply them to derive Hanafi rulings from textual examples.

Core Idea
Contextual indicators—external, verbal, or part of speech—determine when a word is used metaphorically, overriding its literal sense.

In Hanafi usul, distinguishing literal (haqiqah) from metaphorical (majaz) meaning is crucial for deriving rulings from texts. This passage from Noor al-Anwar provides a systematic classification of the indicators (qara'in) that signal metaphorical usage. By analyzing examples from oaths and slaves, you will learn how custom, linguistic derivation, and textual context determine the intended meaning. Mastery of these categories is essential for accurate istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main classification
The passage divides qara'in into five types: external to speaker and speech, a description of the speaker, a word external to the speech, the same speech itself, or a part of the speech. Note the examples for each.
Focus on the broad categories before diving into subtypes.
2
Analyze the first part-of-speech subtype
The passage explains that some individuals are deficient in the meaning (e.g., mukatab under 'mamluk') while others are additional (e.g., grapes under 'fakihah'). Understand the reasoning: deficiency/excess relative to the core meaning.
This subtype is further divided into two: deficient individuals and additional individuals.
3
Trace the dispute with Malik
The Hanafi position on 'lahm' (meat) excludes fish, while Malik includes it based on Quranic usage. Observe how the Hanafis prioritize linguistic derivation and custom over a single scriptural occurrence.
This dispute illustrates the practical importance of qara'in.
4
Examine the pickpocket example
The tarrar is included under sariq despite taking from the awake, because the additional element does not change the original meaning. This illustrates the concept of 'dalalat al-nass' (indication of the text).
This shows how qara'in can also confirm inclusion rather than exclusion.
5
Apply the classification to a new scenario
Given a hypothetical text, determine which type of qarīnah applies and whether the intended meaning is literal or metaphorical.
Practice with the examples in the practice tasks.

بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث فالأول كما إذا حلف لا يأكل لحما ، فلا يتناول لحم السمك، وقوله : "كل مملوك لي حر لا يتناول المكاتب"، فإن اللحم لا يتناول السمك؛ إذ هو مشتق من الالتحام وهو الشدة، ولا شدة بدون الدم والسمك لا دم فيه؛ لأن الدموي لا يسكن الماء ولا يعيش فيه، فلا يتناول هذا الحلف لحم السمك، وإن كان أطلق عليه في القرآن في قوله :تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي سَخَّرَ الْبَحْرَ لِتَأْكُلُوا مِنْهُ لَحْمًا طَرِيًّا﴾ (النحل: ١٤). به تمسك مالك لله في أنه يحنث بأكل لحم السمك، ونحن نقول : لا يحنث به لأجل مأخذ اللفظ، ولأن بائعه لا يسمى في العرف بائع اللحم، فالأول: أي ما إذا كان اللفظ موضوعًا لمعنى فيه قوة. (القمر) فلا يتناول لحم السمك: هذا إذا لم ينو شيئًا، وأما إذا نوى تناول لحم السمك، فيتناوله كذا قيل. (القمر) إذ هو مشتق إلخ: يعني أن اللحم مأخوذ من الالتحام يقال التحم العرب أي اشتد فسمي اللحم بهذا الاسم؛ لما فيه من الشدة، ولا شدة بدون الدم الذي هو أقوى الأخلاط في الحيوان والسمك لا دم فيه وما يسيل عنه عند الشق، فذلك ليس بدم إنما هو ماء أحمر ويطلق عليه الدم مجازا؛ لأن الدموي إلخ. (القمر) في قوله تعالى إلخ لتَأْكُلُوا مِنْهُ أي من البحر ولَحْمًا طَرِيًّا (النحل: ١٤) هو السمك ووصفه بالطراوة؛ لأنه أرطب اللحوم، فيسرع إليه الفساد فيسارع إلى أكله كذا قال البيضاوي (القمر) يحنث إلخ فإن مطلق اسم اللحم يتناوله. (القمر) لأجل مأخذ اللفظ إلخ والتفصيل لقرائن المجاز: أن القرينة للمجاز إما خارجة عن المتكلم والكلام أي لا تكون صفة للمتكلم، ولا من جنس الكلام كدلالة الحال نحو يمين الفور وكدلالة العرف والعادة، ودلالة الحس، أو تكون صفة للمتكلم نحو: {وَاسْتَفْزِزْ مَنِ اسْتَطَعْتَ} (الإسراء: ٦٤) فإن الله لا يأمر بالمعصية، أو يكون لفظا خارجا عن الكلام الذي فيه المجاز كقوله تعالى: {فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ} (الكهف: ٢٩)، فإن حقيقة هذا الكلام للتخيير لكن قوله تعالى بعد ذلك: {إِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَارًا} (الكهف:٢٩) قرينة مانعة ذلك، أو يكون لفظًا هو عين هذا الكلام نحو «الأعمال بالنيات»، فإن القرينة مجموع هذا الكلام ثبوت النية لجميع الأعمال، أو يكون لفظا هو جزء هذا الكلام، وهو على قسمين: الأول أن يكون بعض الأفراد أولى بأن يكون ناقصا كما في قوله "كل" مملوك لي حر"، فإنه لا يتناول المكاتب؛ لأنه ناقص في المملوكية، أو يكون زائدا كما في قوله لا يأكل الفاكهة"، فإنه لا يتناول العنب؛ لأنه زائد على معنى التفكه، والثاني: أن لا يكون بعض الأفراد أولى كما في قوله: "لا يضع قدمه في دار "فلان" هكذا في "التوضيح". (السنبلي) يعني إسناد

بيان قرائن العمل بالمجاز وترك الحقيقة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث ولفظ مملوك في قوله: "كل" مملوك لي "حر" لا يتناول المكاتب؛ لأنه ما كان مملوكًا كاملاً من جميع الوجوه يدا ورقبة فيتناول المدبر وأم الولد ولا يتناول المكاتب؛ لأنه مملوك رقبة حريدًا، فكان ناقصا في معنى المملوكية. والثاني ما ذكره بقوله: وعكسه الحلف بأكل الفاكهة أي عكس المذكور من المثالين ما إذا حلف لا يأكل الفاكهة، فلا يتناول العنب؛ لأن الفاكهة اسم لما يتفكه به ويتلذذ حال كونه أي يتنعم به زائدا على ما يقع به قوام البدن، فهو موضوع للنقصان والعنب والرطب والرمان فيها كمال ليس في الفاكهة وهو أن يكون به قوام البدن ويكفي بها في بعض الأمصار للغذاء، فلا يدخل في الناقص، وأما إدخال الطرار في السارق وإن كان فيه كمال أيضًا من السارق؛ فلأن ذلك الكمال والزيادة ليس بمغير لمعنى الأصل بل مكمل له من قبيل دلالة النص: أي السرقة معنى الأصل
المكاتب هو من عقد من مولاه بأنه يؤدي إلى المولى هذا القدر من المال ثم يعتق. (القمر) فيتناول المدبر وأم الولد : فإنهما مملو كان يدا ورقبةً، والمدير: من قال له المولى إذا مت فأنت حر، وأم الولد أمة استولد منها المولى وحكمهما : أنهما يعتقان بعد موت المولى (القمر)
لأنه أي لأن المكاتب مملوك رقبة، فإن المكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه درهم كذا جاء في "الجامع" ولذا إذا عجز عن بدل الكتابة يعود إلى الرق. (القمر) حر يدا أي ليس بمملوك يدًا ليتحقق مقصودًا لكتابة وهو أداء البدل، فيملك المكاتب البيع والشراء وأمثالهما. (القمر) فكان ناقصا إلخ وفيه أنه لو كان الملك في المكاتب ناقصا وفي المدير وأم الولد كاملاً، فلا يتأدى الكفارة به، ويتأدى بهما مع أنه ليس كذلك. وأجيب بأن مدار الكفارة على الرق، والرق فيهما ناقص، فإن ما ثبت فيهما من جهة العتق لا يرتفع بوجه والرق فيه كامل؛ لأنه عبد كما كان إذا عجز؛ فلذا لا يتأدى الكفارة بهما ويتعدى به (القمر) والثاني أي ما إذا كان اللفظ موضوعًا لمعنى فيه ضعف. (القمر) وأما إدخال الطرار إلخ جواب إشكال تقريره أنه يلزم على ما ذكرتم عدم دخول الطرار في السارق؛ إذ في الطرار زيادة ليست في السرقة فإنه يأخذ عن اليقظان. (القمر)
من قبيل دلالة النص قد مر البحث فيه فتذكر . (القمر)
Explanation of the indicators for preferring metaphorical meaning over literal meaning. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: The first is like when one swears not to eat meat, and it does not apply to fish meat, and his saying: 'Every slave of mine is free' does not apply to the mukatab. For meat does not apply to fish, because it is derived from 'iltiham' which is intensity, and there is no intensity without blood, and fish has no blood, because the blood-dwelling creature does not inhabit water nor live in it. So this oath does not apply to fish meat, even though it is called meat in the Quran in His statement: '...to eat from it fresh meat' (Quran 16:14). Imam Malik adhered to this, so he rules that he violates the oath by eating fish meat. And we say: He does not violate it due to the origin of the word, and because the seller of fish is not called in custom a seller of meat. The first: meaning when the word is coined for a meaning that has strength. (Al-Qamar) So it does not apply to fish meat: this is if he did not intend anything, but if he intended to apply it to fish meat, then it applies, as was said. (Al-Qamar) 'Because it is derived from...' etc.: meaning that 'lahm' is taken from 'iltiham'; they say the Arabs became intense, i.e. they became strong, so meat was named by this name because of the intensity in it, and there is no intensity without blood which is the strongest humor in the animal, and fish has no blood, and what flows from it when split is not blood but red water, and it is called blood metaphorically, because the blood[-dweller] etc. (Al-Qamar) In His saying, the Exalted, etc.: '...to eat from it', i.e. from the sea, 'fresh meat' (Quran 16:14) is fish, and He described it with freshness because it is the moistest of meats, so it quickly spoils, thus one hastens to eat it, so said al-Baydawi. (Al-Qamar) 'He violates the oath' etc.: because the absolute name of meat applies to it. (Al-Qamar) 'Due to the origin of the word' etc. And the detailed explanation of the indicators for metaphor: The indicator for metaphor is either external to the speaker and the speech, i.e., it is not a description of the speaker nor of the same type as the speech, such as the indication of the state—like the oath of urgency—and the indication of custom and habit, and the indication of the senses; or it is a description of the speaker, like {And incite whomever you can} (Quran 17:64), for God does not command disobedience; or it is a word external to the speech containing the metaphor, like His saying: {So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve} (Quran 18:29), for the literal meaning of this speech is permission, but His saying thereafter: {Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire} (Quran 18:29) is a preventing indicator; or it is a word that is the same as this speech, like 'Actions are by intentions', for the indicator is the entire statement: the establishment of intention for all actions; or it is a word that is a part of this speech, and it is two types: first, that some individuals are more likely to be deficient, as in his saying 'Every slave of mine is free', for it does not apply to the mukatab because he is deficient in being a slave; or it is additional, as in his saying 'He does not eat fruit', for it does not apply to grapes because they are additional to the meaning of enjoying fruit; and the second: that some individuals are not more likely, as in his saying 'He does not place his foot in the house of so-and-so', thus in 'al-Tawdih'. (al-Sanbali) meaning attribution.

Explanation of the indicators for preferring metaphorical meaning over literal meaning. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: The word 'mamluk' in his saying: 'Every slave of mine is free' does not apply to the mukatab because he was not completely owned in all respects, in hand and in title, so it applies to the mudabbar and umm al-walad, and does not apply to the mukatab because he is owned in title but free in hand, so he is deficient in the meaning of being owned. The second is what he mentioned by his saying: And its opposite is the oath of eating fruit, i.e., the opposite of the two mentioned examples: if he swears not to eat fruit, it does not apply to grapes, because fruit is a name for what one enjoys and delights in while it is—meaning one takes pleasure in it—in addition to what supports the body, so it is coined for deficiency, and grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates contain perfection not in fruit, namely that they support the body and suffice for nourishment in some regions, so they do not fall under the deficient. As for including the pickpocket under the thief, even though there is also perfection in the pickpocket over the thief, it is because that perfection and increase do not change the meaning of the original but rather complete it, like the indication of the text: i.e., theft is the original meaning.

The mukatab is one who contracts with his master that he will pay him a certain amount of money and then be freed. (Al-Qamar) So it applies to the mudabbar and umm al-walad: for they are owned in hand and in title. The mudabbar is one to whom the master says: 'When I die, you are free.' Umm al-walad is a female slave whom the master impregnated. Their ruling is that they become free after the master's death. (Al-Qamar) 'Because he' i.e., because the mukatab is owned in title, for the mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham remains upon him, as stated in 'al-Jami''. Hence, if he fails to pay the contract price, he returns to slavery. (Al-Qamar) 'Free in hand' i.e., he is not owned in hand, so that the purpose of the contract—paying the price—is realized; thus the mukatab has the right to buy, sell, and the like. (Al-Qamar) 'So he is deficient' etc. It contains that if ownership in the mukatab is deficient and in the mudabbar and umm al-walad is complete, then expiation cannot be performed with them, but it can with these, even though it is not so. And it was answered that the basis of expiation is slavery, and slavery in them is deficient, for what is established in them by way of freedom is not removed at all, and slavery in him is complete because he is a slave as he was if he becomes unable; therefore expiation cannot be performed with them and can be performed with him. (Al-Qamar) The second: i.e., when the word is coined for a meaning that has weakness. (Al-Qamar) 'As for including the pickpocket' etc. is a response to an objection that would require, based on what you stated, the non-inclusion of the pickpocket under the thief, since the pickpocket has an additional element not in theft, as he takes from the awake. (Al-Qamar) It is from the category of the indication of the text; the discussion has already passed, so remember it. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the usul al-fiqh concept of qara'in (contextual indicators) that determine when a word is used metaphorically (majaz) rather than in its literal sense (haqiqah). The commentary opens by stating the topic: 'Explanation of the indicators for preferring metaphorical meaning over literal meaning.' It then elaborates on the third division of the matn's classification. The first example given is an oath not to eat meat (lahm). The literal meaning of lahm is not applied to fish because lahm is derived from 'iltiham' meaning intensity, which is tied to blood; fish lack blood, so the word is not used for fish in the literal sense, even though the Quran calls fish 'lahman tariyyan' (fresh meat). The Hanafi position is that such an oath does not cover fish (the swearer does not break his oath by eating fish) because the derivation of the word and common custom ('urf) do not consider fish as meat. Imam Malik, however, would consider it a breach due to the Quranic usage. The passage then provides the detailed categorization of qara'in: they can be external to the speaker and speech (like context, custom, sense perception), or a description of the speaker (like God's command which cannot be taken literally for sin), or a word external to the metaphorical speech (as in Quran 18:29 where the threat of fire prevents taking the permission literally), or the very same words (like the hadith 'actions are by intentions' which establishes intention for all actions), or a part of the speech. The part-of-speech indicator is further divided: some individuals are deficient in the meaning (like the mukatab in the phrase 'every slave of mine is free') or additional (like grapes in the oath not to eat fruit), and the second sub-division where no individual is more likely (like the example of not placing foot in a house). The commentary also explains the application to the mukatab: he is owned in title but free in hand, so he is deficient in the meaning of mamluk (owned). The mudabbar and umm al-walad are fully owned in hand and title, so they are included. The passage ends with a discussion on the pickpocket (tarrar) being included under thief (sariq) despite the additional element of taking from the awake, because the increase does not change the original meaning but completes it, analogous to the indication of the text (dalalat al-nass). This is a standard Hanafi usul reasoning showing how qara'in operate to determine the intended meaning.

Key Terms
قرينة (qarīnah): A contextual indicator that shows the intended meaning is metaphorical rather than literal.
مجاز (majāz): A word used in a meaning other than its original, literal coining, due to a contextual indicator.
لحم (laḥm): Meat, derived from 'iltiham' meaning intensity, implying the presence of blood.
مملوك (mamlūk): A slave who is owned completely in hand and title ('yadan wa raqabatan').
فاكهة (fākiha): Fruit that is enjoyed as a luxury beyond staple food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if someone swears not to eat 'lahm' (meat) and then eats fish, does he violate his oath?
A) Yes, because the Quran calls fish 'lahman tariyyan'.
B) No, because 'lahm' is derived from 'iltiham' which requires blood, and fish has no blood.
C) Yes, because customarily fish is considered meat.
D) No, unless he intended to include fish.

Key Takeaway
The five types of qara'in—external, speaker-descriptive, external word, same speech, part-of-speech—determine when a word is used metaphorically, with Hanafi usul prioritizing linguistic origin and custom over isolated scriptural occurrences.
