The Waw Does Not Indicate Sequence: Usul Applied to Marriage Cases
Expanding on the usul principle: waw does not imply sequence, using examples from slave marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the conjunction 'waw' does not imply sequence to derive changes in rulings based on context, specifically in marriage cases involving slave women and emancipation.

Core Idea
The waw creates no order; sequence is derived from legal context and effects, not from the conjunction itself.

The conjunction 'waw' (and) is ubiquitous in legal texts, but does it imply a chronological sequence? The Hanafi school holds that it does not—any appearance of order comes from the surrounding context and the inherent logic of the rulings. This lesson unpacks this principle through a detailed case study from our text: a master who marries two slave women to a man via an intervening party (fudūlī), then emancipates them. The variation in the emancipation wording (single word, connected speech, separated speech) directly affects the validity of the marriages, demonstrating that the 'waw' itself is neutral. We will walk through the core principle, the supporting da līl, and the practical consequences in analogous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core usul principle
State clearly: The conjunction waw does not inherently indicate sequence or arrangement. Any perceived order comes from the linguistic context or the nature of the legal rulings involved.
This is a fundamental Hanafi rule in usul al-fiqh.
2
Analyze the base scenario: two slave women in one marriage contract
Examine the case: a master marries two slave women to a man (via a fudūlī) without the husband’s permission. The marriages are suspended (mawqūf) pending ratification. When the master emancipates them, the order of emancipation (if any) and the wording affect the outcome.
The suspension (tawaqquf) is key—the marriages are not fully valid until authorization.
3
Apply the waw principle to the emancipation sentence
If the master says 'I emancipate this and this' (with waw), both are emancipated simultaneously. The waw does not make one first. However, if he uses separate statements or sequential language (e.g., 'then'), order emerges from context.
The text shows that when the master says 'this and this' in one breath, the emancipation is simultaneous.
4
Trace the legal effect: prohibition of combining free and slave co-wives
When the first slave becomes free, her marriage becomes effective immediately. At that moment, the second slave is still a slave, and having both as co-wives would violate the prohibition 'Do not marry a slave woman over a free woman' (da līl from Ibn Abi Shayba). Hence the second marriage becomes void.
The prohibition is explicit and indisputable (qat'ī al-dalālah).
5
Extend to analogous cases: two sisters or multiple masters
The same logic applies to parallel scenarios: marrying two sisters in separate contracts, or when two different masters emancipate their slave wives successively. In each, the waw principle combines with the substantive ruling to determine which marriage is valid.
The principle is consistent: context and legal maxims govern, not the waw.

بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث أن هذا الترتيب أيضًا لم يجئ من الواو بل من الكلام؛ لأن نكاح الأمتين كان موقوفا على إجازة المولى وإجازة الزوج جميعًا، فإذا أعتق المولى الأولى أولاً كانت الثانية موقوفة والأولى نافذة، فلزم أن يتوقف نكاح الأمة على الحرة وهو غير جائز، كما أن نكاحها على الحرة غير جائز، فلم يبق للثانية محل توقف إلى أن يتكلم بعتقها، ويقول: "وهذه". وهذا كله إذا قبل فضولي آخر من جانب الزوج؛ لأن الفضولي الواحد لا يتولى طرفي النكاح، وقيل: إذا تكلم الفضولي الواحد بكلامين بأن قال: "زوجت فلانة من فلان وقبلت منه يتوقف ولا يبطل، وقيل: لا حاجة إلى قوله: بغير إذن الزوج؛ لأن حكم المسألة لا يتوقف عليه؛ ولهذا لم يقيده شمس الأئمة بهذا القيد، في أصوله. فلزم أن يتوقف إلخ لأنه لما أعتق المولى الأولى صارت حرة فنفذ نكاحها قبل التكلم بعتق الثانية، ونكاح الثانية حين هذا النفاذ موقوف لكونها أمة بعد لم يؤذن بنكاحها، فلزم أن يتوقف إلخ، واللازم غير جائز؛ إذ لا فائدة لهذا التوقف، فإنه لوقوع الجواز عند الإجازة، ولا يجوز نكاح الأمة على الحرة لما روى ابن أبي شيبة عن أمير المؤمنين عليه "لا تنكح الأمة على الحرة". (القمر) غير جائز إلخ وقد يناقش بأن امتناع نكاح الأمة على الحرة إنما هو في الابتداء لا في البقاء كيف ولو تزوج أمتين بعقد واحد ثم أعتقت إحداهما لا يبطل نكاح الأخرى وفي الابتداء إن اعتبر حال الإنشاء والتوقف، ففي تلك الحال كلتاهما أمتان وإن اعتبر حال النفاذ ففيها كلاهما حرتان فلا وجه لفساد ذلك، وأجيب بأن النكاح حقيقة هو النافذ، فإن الموقوف في عرضة أن يكون نكاحًا كيف ولا يحل به ما شرع النكاح لأجله فهو نكاح من وجه دون وجه، فإذا أعتقت الأولى نفذ نكاحها وهي حرة، فلم يبق الأخرى محلاً لإنشاء النكاح، بل لحقت بالمحرمات ما دامت أمة وهذه الحرة تحته، فبطل العقد الموقوف، فلا تنفذ بلحوق الحرية؛ لأن ما بطل لا يعود. (السنبلي) فلم يبق إلخ فبطل نكاح الثانية قبل التكلم بعتقها (القمر) إذا قبل فضولي إلخ: لدفع الاعتراض وهو: أن قوله: يستفاد الأولى وبطلان الثاني باطل؛ لأن نكاح كل واحد من الأمتين باطل بناء على أن الواحد لا يتولى طرفي النكاح. (المحشي) فضولي: هو من يشتغل بما لا يعنيه والمراد ههنا: من ليس بوكيل.(المحشي) لا حاجة إلى قوله إلخ فحينئذ ذكر هذا القول في المتن اتفاقي. (القمر) لا يتوقف عليه: فإنه لو حصل التزوج بإذن الزوج بغير إذن المولى ثم أعتق المولى بهذا الكلام المذكور أي هذه حرة "وهذه يبطل نكاح الثانية أيضًا. (القمر) وإن أعتقهما المولى بلفظ واحد بأن قال: "أعتقهما لا يبطل نكاح واحدة منهما؛ لعدم تحقق الجمع بين الحرة والأمة. وإن أعتقهما بكلام مفصول فأجاز الزوج نكاحهما أو واحدة منهما، جاز نكاح المعتقة الأولى، ويبطل نكاح الثانية فلا تلحقه الإجازة. هذا إذا كان النكاحان في عقدٍ واحدٍ، فأما إذا كانا في عقدين، فإن كان مولى الأمتين واحدًا فالحكم كما ذكرنا وإن كانا اثنين فأعتقت الأمتان على التعاقب، فالنكاحان موقوفان فأيهما أجاز الزوج جاز، وإن أجازهما معًا جاز نكاح المعتقة الأولى. وإذا زوّج رجلا أختين في عقدين بغير إذن الزوج فبلغه الخبر، فقال: أجزت نكاح هذه وهذه بطلا، كما إذا أجازهما معًا، وإن أجازهما متفرقا بطل نكاح الثانية، لعدم تحقق الجمع إلخ أي لا في حال العقد ولا في حال الإجازة، فلزم العقد من جانب المولى؛ لأن حقه ساقط بالإعتاق، وأما الزوج فإن شاء أجاز نكاحهما وإن شاء أجاز نكاح واحدة منهما بعينه. (القمر) الجمع: لا في العقد ولا في وقت الإجازة ولزم العقد. (المحشي بكلام مفصول: أي أعتق إحداهما وسكت ثم أعتق الأخرى. (القمر) ويبطل إلخ لأنه نكاح الأمة على الحرة. (القمر) كما ذكرنا أي في صور الإعتاق بلفظ واحد أو بلفظين بكلام موصول أو بكلام مفصول. (القمر) وإن كانا اثنين أي كان لكل أمة مولى على حدة. (القمر) موقوفان أي على إجازة الزوج؛ لأنهما لو انشئا العقد حال كون إحداهما حرة والأخرى أمة توقف النكاحان على إجازة الزوج؛ إذ لا تضايق في هذا الوقف، فإن أحدهما لا يملك الإجازة أو الرد في ملك الآخر، بخلاف ما إذا كان المولى واحدا فإنه لما أعتق الأولى صار رادًا نكاح الثانية؛ لكونها أمة بعد وأنه بسبيل من هذا الرد كذا في "التلويح". (القمر) وإن أجازهما أي حال الإعتاق على التعاقب. (القمر) جاز إلخ لأن حالة الإجازة كحالة الإنشاء، فيصح نكاح الحرة ويبطل نكاح الأمة كذا في "التلويح". (القمر) في عقدين: إنما قال هذا؛ لأنه لو كان نكاح الأختين في عقد واحد فهذا النكاح باطل من الأصل لا يتوقف على الإجازة كذا قيل. (القمر) بطلا: أي نكاح هذه ونكاح هذه؛ لأنه يلزم الجمع بين الأختين. (القمر) معا كأن يقول: أجزت نكاحهما (القمر) متفرقًا أي في الأزمنة المتفرقة بأن قال: أجزت نكاح هذه ثم بعد زمان قال: أجزت نكاح الأخرى. [فتح الغفار: ١٨٠] (القمر) بطل نكاح الثانية لأن الأول قد صح بلا مزاحم والمبطل إنما جاء على الثاني. (القمر)
Statement of the divisions of the third division: that this arrangement also does not come from the waw but from the speech; because the marriage of the two slave women was suspended on the permission of the master and the permission of the husband together. So when the master emancipated the first one first, the second remained suspended and the first was effective. Thus it became necessary that the marriage of the slave woman be suspended on the free woman, and that is not permissible, just as marrying a slave woman over a free woman is not permissible. So there remained for the second no place for suspension until he speaks of her emancipation and says: 'And this [one].' And all this is if another intervening party accepted from the side of the husband; because a single intervening party does not undertake both sides of the marriage. And it is said: if a single intervening party speaks with two statements by saying: 'I married so-and-so to so-and-so and I accepted from him,' it is suspended and does not become void. And it is said: there is no need for his statement 'without the husband's permission' because the ruling of the issue does not depend on it; therefore Shams al-A'imma did not restrict it with this restriction in his Uṣūl. 'Thus it became necessary that it be suspended, etc.' because when the master emancipated the first, she became free, so her marriage was executed before speaking of the emancipation of the second. And the marriage of the second at the time of this execution was suspended because she was still a slave not yet permitted to be married. Thus it became necessary that it be suspended, etc., and the necessary thing is not permissible; because there is no benefit in this suspension, for it [expects] permission at the time of authorization. And it is not permissible to marry a slave woman over a free woman because Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from the Commander of the Faithful: 'Do not marry a slave woman over a free woman.' (Al-Qamar) 'Not permissible, etc.' It may be objected that the prohibition of marrying a slave woman over a free woman is only at the beginning, not in continuation. For instance, if he married two slave women in one contract and then emancipated one of them, the marriage of the other does not become void. And at the beginning, if one considers the state of inception and suspension, then in that state both are slave women. And if one considers the state of execution, then both are free. So there is no basis for its invalidation. And the answer is that marriage in reality is the executed one; the suspended is on the verge of being a marriage, how could it not? What marriage was legislated for is not permissible through it, so it is a marriage from one aspect and not from another. So when the first was emancipated, her marriage was executed while she was free, so the other did not remain a locus for the inception of marriage, but rather became joined to the prohibited women as long as she is a slave and this free woman is under him. So the suspended contract became void, so it is not executed upon the arrival of freedom; because what has become void does not return. (Al-Sanbali) 'Thus there remained no, etc.' So the marriage of the second became void before speaking of her emancipation. (Al-Qamar) 'If an intervening party accepted, etc.' to repel the objection: that his statement 'the first is benefited and the voidness of the second' is false; because the marriage of each of the two slave women is void based on the principle that a single person does not undertake both sides of the marriage. (Al-Muhashshi) 'Intervening party' is one who busies himself with what does not concern him, and the meaning here is: one who is not an agent. (Al-Muhashshi) 'There is no need for his statement, etc.' So then mentioning this statement in the text is incidental. (Al-Qamar) 'Does not depend on it' for if marriage occurred with the husband's permission without the master's permission, and then the master emancipates them with this mentioned statement, i.e., 'This is free, and this', then the marriage of the second also becomes void. (Al-Qamar) 'And if the master emancipates them with one word by saying: 'I emancipate both', the marriage of neither becomes void because the combination of free and slave woman is not realized. And if he emancipates them with separated speech, and the husband authorized their marriage or one of them, the marriage of the first emancipated is valid, and the marriage of the second becomes void, so authorization does not affect it. This is if the two marriages were in one contract. As for if they were in two contracts, then if the master of the two slave women is one, the ruling is as we mentioned. And if they are two, and the two slave women are emancipated successively, then the two marriages are suspended; whichever the husband authorizes becomes valid, and if he authorizes both together, the marriage of the first emancipated is valid. And if one marries a man two sisters in two contracts without the husband's permission, and the news reaches him, and he says: 'I authorize the marriage of this and this', they both become void, just as if he authorized both together. And if he authorizes them separately, the marriage of the second becomes void. Because the combination is not realized, etc., meaning neither at the time of the contract nor at the time of authorization, so the contract becomes binding from the master's side because his right falls with emancipation. As for the husband, if he wishes, he may authorize both marriages, and if he wishes, he may authorize one of them specifically. (Al-Qamar) 'Combination: not in the contract nor at the time of authorization, and the contract becomes binding.' (Al-Muhashshi) 'With separated speech': meaning he emancipates one and then remains silent, then emancipates the other. (Al-Qamar) 'And becomes void, etc.' because it is marriage of a slave woman over a free woman. (Al-Qamar) 'As we mentioned' meaning in the forms of emancipation with one word or with two words in connected or separated speech. (Al-Qamar) 'And if they are two' meaning each slave woman has a separate master. (Al-Qamar) 'Suspended' meaning on the authorization of the husband; because if they had initiated the contract while one was free and the other a slave, the two marriages would be suspended on the husband's authorization; since there is no strictness in this suspension, because one of them does not have the authority to authorize or reject regarding the property of the other. Unlike if the master is one, for when he emancipated the first, he became a rejector of the second's marriage because she was still a slave and he was on the way to this rejection. This is in Al-Talwih. (Al-Qamar) 'And if he authorizes them' meaning at the time of emancipation successively. (Al-Qamar) 'Becomes valid, etc.' because the state of authorization is like the state of inception, so the marriage of the free woman is valid and the marriage of the slave woman becomes void, as in Al-Talwih. (Al-Qamar) 'In two contracts': he only said this because if the marriage of the two sisters was in one contract, this marriage is void ab initio and does not depend on authorization, as has been said. (Al-Qamar) 'Both become void': meaning the marriage of this and the marriage of this; because the combination of the two sisters becomes necessary. (Al-Qamar) 'Together' as if he says: 'I authorize both their marriages.' (Al-Qamar) 'Separately' meaning at separate times, by saying: 'I authorize the marriage of this' and then after a time saying: 'I authorize the marriage of the other.' (Fath al-Ghaffar: 180) (Al-Qamar) 'The marriage of the second becomes void' because the first became valid without competition, and the invalidating factor only came upon the second. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a usul principle regarding the conjunction 'waw' (واو). The text is demonstrating through fiqh examples that the waw does not itself indicate any sequential order or arrangement (ترتيب); rather, any perceived order comes from the linguistic context and the nature of the rulings involved. The specific examples involve marriage contracts with slave women and their manumission by the master. The discussion arises from a third division (التقسيم الثالث) presented in the base text. The author argues that when a master emancipates two slave women whom he had married to a man (acting as an intervening party, فضولي), the sequence of events—whether the emancipation is done with one word, connected speech, or separated speech—affects the validity of the marriages. The key ruling is that if the first woman is emancipated and her marriage becomes effective, the second marriage (still suspended as she remains a slave) becomes void because it would result in having a slave woman co-wife with a free woman, which is prohibited. This prohibition is supported by a narration from Ibn Abi Shayba. The text then addresses objections: some argue the prohibition is only at the inception of marriage, not in continuation, but the response is that the suspended marriage is not fully real, and once the first becomes effective, the second loses its potential. The passage further distinguishes cases where the marriages are in one contract vs. two, and where the masters are one or two. It also discusses the analogy of marrying two sisters in separate contracts. Throughout, the commentary from Al-Qamar, Al-Sanbali, Al-Muhashshi, and references to Al-Talwih provide clarifications. The central usul lesson is that the waw does not create order; order is derived from the specific context and legal effects.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The conjunction 'and' which does not inherently indicate sequence or arrangement.
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts without authorization, intervening in a contract without being an agent or guardian.
الجمع بين الحرة والأمة (al-jamʿ bayna al-ḥurrah wa al-amah): Combining a free woman and a slave woman in marriage at the same time, which is prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in the text, if a master emancipates two slave women by saying 'I emancipate this and this' (بلفظ واحد), and the husband authorizes both marriages after emancipation, what is the ruling on the marriage of the second woman?
A) Her marriage becomes valid because she is now free.
B) Her marriage becomes void because she was a slave at the time of the first marriage's execution.
C) Her marriage remains suspended until the master says her name specifically.
D) Her marriage is void ab initio because it was a marriage of a slave.

Key Takeaway
The conjunction 'waw' does not create sequence; legal order is derived from context and rulings, and the prohibition of combining a free and slave co-wife determines the validity of marriages in emancipation scenarios.

The Waw and Legal Context: Why Simultaneity is Not Always Implied
Analysis of why waw does not indicate simultaneity in marriage acceptance case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the beginning of speech depends on its end when the end contains a legal modifier, and distinguish between linguistic and legal modification.

Core Idea
The waw does not indicate simultaneity when the end of speech legally modifies the beginning.

When a husband says 'I accept this and this' for two sisters, both marriages are invalidated. At first glance, this seems to confirm that the waw (و) indicates simultaneity. However, the Hanafi usul analysis reveals a deeper principle: the invalidation stems from the legal effect of the second acceptance, which modifies the first from valid to invalid. This lesson unpacks the concept of 'suspension of speech on its end' and distinguishes between linguistic and legal modification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Apparent Implication
Understand the case: a man marries two sisters in separate contracts. When the husband accepts both with connected speech (e.g., 'I accept this and this'), both are invalid. Why? A common assumption is that the waw implies simultaneity, making the actions occur together.
The apparent implication is that the waw indicates muqārana (simultaneity).
2
Recognize the Commentator's Response
The commentator argues that the invalidation is not due to the waw's meaning but because the second acceptance (the end of the speech) changes the first from valid to invalid. Thus, the entire utterance is treated as a single unit taking effect together.
The key phrase: 'صدر الكلام يتوقف على آخره' (the beginning of speech depends on its end).
3
Analyze the Two Types of Modification
Modification can be linguistic (e.g., condition, exception) or legal (where the meaning remains but the ruling changes). The first type is straightforward; the second requires evidence. Here, the prohibition of combining sisters (Quran 4:23) provides that evidence.
Linguistic modification changes the apparent meaning; legal modification changes the ruling despite the meaning.
4
Examine the Quranic Dalil
The verse 'وَأَنْ تَجْمَعُوا بَيْنَ الْأُخْتَيْنِ' is used to show that combining sisters is haram. This legal fact modifies the first acceptance: without it, the first marriage would be valid, but the second makes it impermissible.
The dalil is qati' al-thubut and qati' al-dalala.
5
Distinguish the Role of the Waw
The waw retains its default meaning of conjunction; it does not imply simultaneity here. The simultaneous effect arises from the legal context, not the particle itself. Thus, the ruling is derived from usul principles, not from linguistic analysis alone.
This is consistent with the Hanafi position that the waw is for absolute conjunction unless a qarina (indication) requires another interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث الواو
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث هذا أيضًا جواب سؤال مقدر يرد علينا، وهو: أنه إذا زوج أحد رجلاً أختين معا في عقدين فبلغ الزوج خبر النكاح، فإن أجازهما الزوج بكلام موصول وقال: "أجزت هذه وهذه" بطل النكاحان كأنه أجازهما معًا، فهذا يدل على أن الواو للمقارنة، وإن أجازهما الزوج بكلام مفصول بطل نكاح الثانية بلا شبهة، وهذا استطرادي للأول، فأجاب بأن في هذه الصورة إنما بطل النكاحان كلاهما لا لأن الواو للمقارنة؛ بل لأن صدر الكلام يتوقف على آخره إذا كان في آخره ما يغير أوله كالشرط والاستثناء

إذا تأخرا في الكلام يكون أول الكلام موقوفًا عليهما؛ لأنهما مغيران فكذلك ههنا نكاح الشرط والاستثناء
الأخت الأخيرة يغير أولهما؛ إذ يلزم الجمع بين الأختين بسبب تزويج الأخيرة، من الصحة إلى الفساد
وهذا استطرادي إلخ يعني أن التعرض في المتن عن إجازتهما مفصولاً وقع على سبيل التبعية للأول لا بالأصالة؛ لأنه لا دخل له في السؤال كما لا يخفى. (القمر) فأجاب إلخ خلاصة الجواب: أن هذا ليس لأن مدلول الواو المعية؛ بل لأن الكلام موقوف على آخره، فإن وجد في آخره مغير الأول من صحة إلى فساد مثلاً عمل بالمغير، ويكون الكلام كله بمنزلة كلام واحد، وإن لم يكن فيه مغير الأول يثبت حكم الكلام من حين وجوده كما مر في مسألة الطلاق، وفيما نحن فيه نكاح الثانية مغير لنكاح الأولى من صحة إلى فساد فيتوقف أول الكلام على أخره، ويثبت حكمهما معا، فصار أجزت نكاح هذه وهذه بمنزلة أجزت نكاحهما لهذا لا لأجل دلالة الواو على المقارنة (السنبلي) بل لأن صدر الكلام إلخ يعني أن صدر الكلام وهو إجازة نكاح الأولى لم يؤثر ولم يفد حكما ونفاذاً بل يتوقف على آخره وهو إجازة نكاح الثانية؛ لأنه مغير للأول. (القمر) ما يغير أوله إلخ: قلت ههنا إيراد هو أن التغير نوعان: تغير لدلالة اللفظ كتغير الشرط والاستثناء والصفة والمخصص ونحوها، وتغير لحكمه الشرعي مع بقاء الدلالة بحاله بأن يكون المعنى المستفاد من دون ملاحظة الأخير مستفادًا معها لكن لا يصح شرعًا أي لا يفيد حكمه المسبب فتوقف أول الكلام على آخره المغير بالتغير الأول مسلم وواضح بل من ضرورات العربية، وأما توقفه على الآخر المغير بالنوع الثاني من التغير كما في ما نحن فيه، ففي محل المنع لا بد له من دليل ولم يظهر لي الآن كذا قاله مولانا عبد العلي في بعض تصانيفه. (السنبلي)
إذ يلزم الجمع إلخ: وهو حرام؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَنْ تَجْمَعُوا بَيْنَ الْأُخْتَيْنِ﴾ (النساء: 23) (القمر)

فلذا توقف أول الكلام على آخره، فلا جرم يقترنان في الزمان.
أي إجازة نكاح الأولى إجازة نكاح الثانية
Discussion of the waw. Explanation of the divisions of the third division: This is also an answer to an implied question that arises against us, which is: If someone marries a man to two sisters together in two contracts and the husband receives the news of the marriage, and if he accepts both marriages with connected speech saying 'I accept this and this,' both marriages are invalidated, as if he accepted them together. This indicates that the waw is for conjunction. But if he accepts them with separated speech, the second marriage is invalidated without doubt, and this is a digression from the first. He answered that in this case, both marriages are invalidated not because the waw indicates conjunction, but because the beginning of the speech depends on its end when the end contains something that changes the beginning, like a condition or an exception. ... When they come later in speech, the first part of the speech is suspended upon them because they are modifiers. Similarly here, the marriage of the last sister changes the first because it necessitates combining sisters, from validity to invalidity. This is a digression etc. means that the mention in the text of accepting them separately occurred as a secondary matter, not primarily. (al-Qamar) He answered etc. Summary of the answer: This is not because the waw signifies simultaneity, but because the speech is suspended on its end. If at its end there is something that changes the first from validity to invalidity, the modifier is acted upon, and the whole speech is considered a single speech. If there is no modifier at its end, the ruling of the speech is established from the time of its occurrence as previously in the case of divorce. In our case, the marriage of the second sister modifies the marriage of the first from validity to invalidity, so the first part of the speech depends on its end, and the ruling of both is established together. Thus 'I accepted the marriage of this and this' is equivalent to 'I accepted both marriages,' not because the waw indicates conjunction (al-Sanabili) but because the beginning of the speech etc. means that the beginning of the speech—the acceptance of the first marriage—did not take effect and did not produce a ruling but depends on its end—the acceptance of the second marriage—because it modifies the first. (al-Qamar) What changes its beginning etc.: I say here there is an objection: change is of two types: change due to the indication of the wording, like change by condition, exception, description, specification, and the like; and change due to its legal ruling while the indication remains as it is, meaning the understood meaning without considering the last part is also understood with it but is not valid legally, i.e., it does not produce its caused ruling. The suspension of the first part of speech on its end that modifies by the first type is accepted and clear, and is from the necessities of Arabic. As for its suspension on the other end that modifies by the second type as in our case, it is subject to objection; it requires evidence, and it is not apparent to me now. Thus said Mawlana Abd al-Ali in some of his works. (al-Sanabili) 'Because it necessitates combining sisters' etc.: And that is forbidden due to His saying: 'And that you combine between two sisters' (al-Nisa' 4:23). (al-Qamar) Therefore, the first part of the speech depends on its end, and so they necessarily occur together in time, i.e., the acceptance of the first marriage and the acceptance of the second marriage.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the meaning of the waw (و) in the context of acceptance of marriage contracts. The question is posed: if someone marries a man to two sisters in two separate contracts, and the husband accepts both marriages simultaneously by saying 'I accept this and this' (أجزت هذه وهذه), does the waw indicate that both acceptances occur at the same moment? The initial assumption is that it does, because the husband used connected speech. However, the commentator explains that the invalidation of both marriages is not due to the waw implying simultaneity, but because the first part of the speech is suspended on the last part when the last part modifies the first. Here, accepting the second marriage changes the first marriage from valid to invalid (since marrying two sisters is forbidden), so the entire statement is treated as a single utterance taking effect together. The discussion distinguishes between two types of modification: linguistic modification (like conditions and exceptions) and legal modification (where the meaning remains but the ruling changes). The author notes that while the first type clearly suspends the beginning on the end, the second type requires further evidence, which he does not see. Ultimately, the ruling is based on the Quranic prohibition of combining two sisters in marriage.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The conjunction 'and' which can indicate simultaneity (مقارنة) or circumstance (حال).
مقارنة (muqārana): Simultaneity; that two actions occur at the same time.
صدر الكلام (ṣadr al-kalām): The beginning or first part of an utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are both marriages invalidated when the husband says 'I accept this and this' for two sisters?
A) Because the waw indicates simultaneity, so both acceptances occur together, violating the prohibition against combining sisters.
B) Because the second acceptance modifies the first from valid to invalid, so the entire utterance takes effect together.
C) Because the husband spoke in connected speech, which always invalidates both contracts.
D) Because the first acceptance is conditional on the second, so both are invalid.

Key Takeaway
The beginning of speech depends on its end when the end contains a legal modifier that changes the ruling, even if the linguistic meaning remains unchanged.

Interpreting Waw as Circumstantial in Legal Statements
Analysis of waw used for circumstance in 'Give me a thousand and you are free'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the waw in a legal statement is interpreted as circumstantial (hal) and derive the resulting fiqh ruling using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
When conjunction is impossible, the waw is reinterpreted as circumstantial to avoid meaningless speech.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi usul principle: the figurative use of waw (for circumstance) when literal conjunction leads to absurdity. The example of a master saying to his slave, 'Give me a thousand and you are free,' is a classic case where the waw must be understood as circumstantial to preserve legal coherence. We'll trace the reasoning from the literal impossibility to the figurative interpretation, and see how Hanafi jurists defend this through inversion (qalb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical structure
The sentence is 'أد إلى ألفًا وأنت حر' – an imperative clause (أد إلى ألفًا) followed by waw then an informative clause (أنت حر). Determine the literal relationship.
Literal conjunction would treat both as independent, but the imperative and informative are different sentence types.
2
Assess the possibility of literal conjunction
Ask: is it permissible to conjoin a command and a statement? Some scholars disagree, but here the issue is deeper: literal conjunction would imply the master validly commands payment while the slave is still enslaved, which is impossible because a slave cannot be ordered to give his owner money. Thus literal reading leads to meaningless speech.
The text says 'لا يحسن' (not good) not 'لا يجوز' (not permissible), but jurists prioritize legal meaning over rhetorical beauty.
3
Apply the figurative interpretation: waw li'l-hal
Since literal conjunction fails, the waw is taken as circumstantial, meaning 'while you are free' or 'in the condition of being free.' This turns the clause into a condition: emancipation depends on payment.
The relationship is that the waw indicates association, and the circumstance restricts the action.
4
Address the inversion objection
An objector says: the circumstance is 'you are free,' so payment should depend on freedom, not vice versa. The Hanafi response is qalb (inversion): the waw, though attached to 'you are free,' in meaning belongs to 'payment,' making payment the condition.
Qalb is justified by the speaker's intent and the need to avoid absurdity.
5
Derive the fiqh ruling
The final ruling: the slave is not freed until he pays the thousand. The waw for hal imposes a condition, consistent with the master's intention.
This approach avoids voiding the speech and respects legal capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تكون الواو للحال، هذا بيان المجاز في معنى الواو كما أن كونها للعطف كان بيان الحقيقة كقوله لعبده: "أد إلى ألفًا وأنت حر" حتى لا يعتق إلا بالأداء، فالواو في قوله: وأنت "حر" ليست للعطف؛ إذ لا يحسن عطف الخبر على الإنشاء، فيحمل على الحال، والحال يكون شرطًا وقيدًا للعامل، فينبغي أن يتوقف العتق على أداء الألف. ويرد عليه أن الحال هو قوله وأنت "حر" لا قوله "أدّ إلى ألفًا"، فينبغي أن يكون الأداء موقوفًا على العتق لا العتق موقوفًا على الأداء، وأجيب: بأنه من باب القلب

فلا جرم يقترنان إلخ لأنه لما توقف صدر الكلام على الآخر، فلا يثبت الحكم إلا معا، فلزم إجازة النكاحين معا وهو جمع بين الأختين، فلذا يبطل النكاحان (القمر) إذ لا يحسن عطف الخبر: أي أنت حر على الإنشاء أي أد إلى ألفاً، وإنما قال: لا يحسن ولم يقل: لا يجوز؛ لأن عطف الخبر على الإنشاء قد اختلف فيه، فليس الأمر أنه لا يجوز بل الأمر أنه لا يحسن ثم لا يذهب عليك ما فيه أما أولاً فبأن الفقهاء لا يعتبرون وجوه البلاغة في المسائل، وأما ثانيًا فبأن عدم حسن عطف الخبر على الإنشاء لا يوجب تعذر العطف، فكيف يصار إلى المجاز، فإن المجاز إنما يجوز إذا تعذر الحقيقة وهجرت على ما مر فلا بد من إثبات تعذر العطف، وتقريره أن يقال: إنه لو كان الواو ههنا للعطف لكان مؤدي الكلام إيجاب الألف على العبد ابتداء وليس للمولى ذلك مع قيام رقية العبد فيلغو الكلام فدعت الضرورة إلى أن يجعل الواو للحال تحاميا عن أن يلغو الكلام فتدبر . (القمر) فيحمل على الحال أي مجازاً والعلاقة أن الواو لمطلق العطف، ومن أنواعه العطف بطريق الاجتماع، فجاز أن يراد بالواو الحال المقتضية للجمع مع ذي الحال، فصار هذا من قبيل ذكر المطلق وإرادة المقيد. (القمر) يكون شرطا لكون الحال قيدا كالشرط. (القمر) فينبغي أن يكون إلخ لأن الحال أي الحرية كالشرط والجزاء موقوف على الشرط؛ لأن الشرط موقوف على الجزاء فينبغي أن يكون إلخ (القمر) من باب القلب إلخ: فالواو وإن كان داخلاً في الظاهر على قوله: "أنت حر" لكنها بحسب المعنى داخلة على الأداء، فصار الأداء شرطًا للحرية، فيكون العتق موقوفا على الأداء، وفيه أن القلب خلاف الظاهر لابد له من قرينة ويمكن أن يقال: إن الحمل على القلب بدلالة من قبل المتكلم، فإن غرضه من هذا الكلام ليس إلا إثبات العتق بعد أداء الألف لا قبله وأن التعليق إنما يصح ممن يصح منه التنجيز ، وليس في وسع المتكلم تنجيز الأداء، فكيف يصح تعليقه كذا قيل تدبر . (القمر)
And the waw may be for the circumstantial (hal). This is an explanation of the figurative usage of waw, just as its use for conjunction is the literal usage, as in his saying to his slave: 'Give me a thousand while you are free' so that he is not freed except with the payment. The waw in his saying 'and you are free' is not for conjunction because it is not good to conjoin an informative clause (khabar) with an imperative (insha'), so it is interpreted as circumstantial. The circumstance becomes a condition and restriction for the agent, so emancipation should be suspended on payment of the thousand. An objection is raised: the circumstance is his saying 'you are free,' not his saying 'give me a thousand,' so payment should be suspended on emancipation, not emancipation on payment. It is answered: this is a case of inversion (qalb). ... (al-Qamar) 'It is not good to conjoin the informative' i.e., 'you are free' with the imperative i.e., 'give to a thousand.' He said 'not good' not 'not permissible' because conjoining informative with imperative is disputed. Then you should not be unaware that firstly, jurists do not consider rhetorical aspects in legal issues, and secondly, the lack of goodness does not necessitate impossibility, so how can one resort to figurative interpretation? Figurative interpretation is only allowed when literal meaning is impossible and abandoned. So it is necessary to prove the impossibility of conjunction. Its explanation is that if the waw here were for conjunction, the meaning of the speech would be imposing the thousand on the slave initially, but the master has no such right while the slave is in his possession, so the speech would be meaningless. Necessity therefore demands that the waw be taken for circumstance to avoid meaningless speech. (al-Qamar) So it is interpreted as circumstantial figuratively, and the relationship (between literal and figurative) is that the waw is for absolute conjunction, and one of its types is conjunction by way of association (al-jam'), so it is permissible to intend by the waw the circumstance which necessitates association with the circumstance's owner. This becomes an instance of mentioning the absolute and intending the restricted. (al-Qamar) It becomes a condition because the circumstance is a restriction like a condition. (al-Qamar) 'It should be...' because the circumstance, i.e., freedom, is like a condition, and the consequence depends on the condition; because the condition depends on the consequence, it should be... (al-Qamar) 'It is a case of inversion' etc.: The waw, although apparently attached to his saying 'you are free,' in meaning is attached to 'payment,' so payment becomes a condition for freedom, thus emancipation is suspended on payment. This is subject to the criticism that inversion is contrary to the apparent meaning and requires evidence. It can be said that the interpretation as inversion is indicated by the context of the speaker, for his intention from this speech is only to establish freedom after payment, not before, and that suspension is only valid from one who is capable of unconditional utterance, and the speaker is not capable of unconditionally effecting payment, so how is suspension valid? So it is said: reflect. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage examines the figurative use of the waw for circumstance (حال) rather than conjunction (عطف). The example is a master saying to his slave: 'Give me a thousand and you are free' (أد إلى ألفًا وأنت حر). The literal reading would conjoin two commands, but that would imply the master orders a thousand and simultaneously declares freedom, which is problematic because the slave cannot be ordered to give money while still enslaved. Thus the waw is interpreted as a circumstantial clause: 'Give me a thousand while you are free' meaning 'if you give me a thousand, you will be free.' The commentator discusses the objection that this inverts the relationship, and defends it through the concept of 'qalb' (inversion), where the waw although attached to freedom is semantically linked to payment. The argument emphasizes that this figurative interpretation is necessary to avoid meaningless speech, and that the speaker's intention determines the meaning.

Key Terms
الواو للحال (al-waw li'l-hal): A figurative use of the waw to indicate a circumstantial clause, implying a condition or restriction.
القلب (al-qalb): Inversion: the waw is grammatically attached to one word but semantically belongs to another.
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The consequence or result, dependent on a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the waw in 'أد إلى ألفًا وأنت حر' interpreted as circumstantial rather than conjunctive?
A) Because it is linguistically impossible to conjoin a command and a statement.
B) Because the master cannot order the slave to give money while enslaved, making the literal meaning problematic.
C) Because Hanafi jurists always prefer figurative interpretations.
D) Because the slave is free at the time of speaking.

Key Takeaway
The waw is interpreted as circumstantial when literal conjunction leads to legal absurdity; inversion (qalb) resolves grammatical objections to protect the intended meaning.

Hanafi Interpretation of 'Aw' in the Bandit Verse
Clarifying Abu Hanifa's view on the word 'or' in the verse on highway robbers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul principle regarding the particle 'aw' (or) in a Quranic verse and apply it to derive the ruling that the imam has a choice among penalties.

Core Idea
The Arabic letter 'aw' can indicate choice or distribution; Abu Hanifa opts for choice, giving the imam discretion.

In this lesson, we analyze a classic usul dispute over a single word: 'aw' (or) in the verse prescribing punishments for highway robbers. Abu Hanifa reads it as granting the imam discretion, while his two students assign each penalty exclusively. Understanding this debate sharpens your skill in how a particle's meaning can change a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Locate the operative word
Identify the word 'aw' in the verse (al-Ma'idah 5:33) and note that it connects four penalties. This is the locus of the dispute.
The verse: أَنْ يُقَتَّلُوا أَوْ يُصَلَّبُوا أَوْ تُقَطَّعَ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَأَرْجُلُهُمْ مِنْ خِلَافٍ أَوْ يُنْفَوْا مِنَ الْأَرْضِ
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that 'aw' indicates choice (takhyir), so the imam may select any penalty or combine them. His proof includes the story of the Urayniyyun where the Prophet applied both cutting and killing.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that 'aw' distributes penalties exclusively: crucifixion for killing+theft, killing for killing only, amputation for theft only, exile for intimidation only. Their proof is the hadith of Abu Burda.
4
Compare the usul principles
Identify the Hanafi principle: 'aw' defaults to choice unless a qarina (contextual evidence) restricts it. The Sahibayn saw the hadith as such a qarina, Abu Hanifa did not.
This shows how same text yields different rulings due to usul assumptions.
5
Derive the practical consequence
Apply each view: Under Abu Hanifa, an imam may execute a killer-thief without crucifixion. Under the Sahibayn, crucifixion is mandatory. This thamarah al-khilaf has real legal weight.

(القمر) أخرجه الشافعي في "مسنده"، والإمام محمد بن الحسن في كتاب الآثار، وأخرجه البغوي موقوفا على ابن عباس و لم يسم أبا بردة. [إشراق الأبصار: ١١]
The companions of Abu Burda cut the road, so Gabriel descended with the prescribed punishment that whoever killed and took property should be crucified, whoever killed and did not take property should be killed, whoever took property and did not kill should have his hand and foot cut from opposite sides, and whoever caused fear should be exiled. But Abu Hanifa interpreted his statement: 'whoever killed and took property should be crucified' as specifying crucifixion for this situation, not that this situation is exclusively for crucifixion such that no other is permissible. Rather, he affirmed that the imam has a choice among the four: if he wishes, he may cut and then kill or crucify, and if he wishes, he may kill or crucify without cutting; because the crime admits both unity and multiplicity, so both aspects are considered in it. The meaning of exile is not expulsion from the homeland as the apparent meaning suggests, but rather removal from appearing on the face of the earth, by imprisoning them until they repent.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'aw' (or) in the Quranic verse on the punishment for bandits (al-Ma'idah 5:33). The source of the ruling is the story of the Urayniyyun and the hadith of Abu Burda. Abu Hanifa interprets the verse as giving the imam discretion to choose any of the four penalties (kill, crucify, cut hand and foot, banish), because the conjunction 'aw' signifies alternation. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) assign each penalty exclusively to specific crimes (e.g., crucifixion only for killing and theft). Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the crime can be seen as one (highway robbery) or multiple (theft, murder), so the imam may apply either or both penalties. The text also clarifies that 'exile' (nafy) means imprisonment, not banishment, based on the hadith's context.

Key Terms
أو (aw): A letter of conjunction used for alternation or choice between two or more things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what does the word 'aw' (or) imply in the punishment verse for bandits?
A) The imam may choose any of the four penalties
B) Each penalty is assigned exclusively to specific crimes
C) The hardest penalty must always be applied
D) Exile is always the punishment for all bandits

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of the letter 'aw' in the bandit verse generates a major ikhtilaf in Hanafi fiqh: choice vs distribution.

The Original Meaning of the Letter 'Ba'
Defining the letter 'Ba' as a preposition of adhesion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the original meaning of the Arabic letter 'Ba' and distinguish its literal use from metaphorical extensions in legal texts.

Core Idea
The letter Ba fundamentally means adhesion; other uses are metaphorical.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the literal meanings of Arabic particles is crucial for interpreting legal texts. The preposition 'Ba' is a common particle whose original meaning is adhesion (ilsaq). This lesson establishes that core meaning, which serves as the default when encountering 'Ba' in Qur'anic verses, hadith, or contract language, unless a contextual clue shifts it to a metaphorical sense like instrumentality or exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle
Recognize that 'Ba' is a preposition (harf jar) in Arabic. The passage focuses on its linguistic function.
2
State the original meaning
Memorize that the original meaning is al-ilsaq (adhesion). The phrase 'faba'u lil-ilsaq' explicitly states this.
This is the foundation for all other uses.
3
Distinguish literal from metaphorical
Understand that other meanings (e.g., instrumentality, exchange, companionship) are metaphorical extensions (majaz) of adhesion. The passage says 'wa al-bawaqi majaz fiha'.
Metaphorical meanings require a contextual indicator (qarina) to override the literal.
4
Apply to the price example
In the phrase 'ishtaraytu bi-al-thaman', the 'Ba' on the price indicates exchange, but this is reducible to adhesion: the price adheres to the purchased item.
This shows how the literal meaning underlies even transactional uses.

Discussion of the Ba. So the Ba is for adhesion, so whatever is entered upon by the Ba is the thing adhered to; this is its origin in the language, and the rest are metaphorical in it. And it accompanies prices so that even if he said: 'I bought from you this slave' – in the waw more – for the meaning of 'waw' is originary like a part of the meanings of the remaining conjunctive letters as has passed; reflect (al-Qamar). 'And it was said there is no harm in it etc.' – the sayer is Ibn al-Malik for Allah, and it was said: that the soundness of the weak letter in 'ataghadda' in the jussive case is a dialect of the Arabs (al-Qamar). 'Explanation of the intended meaning etc.' – for the jurists rarely pay attention to aspects of grammatical parsing. Do you not see that if a man said to a man: 'You committed zina' (with kasra of the ta), the hadd of qadhf becomes obligatory; thus said the greatest of scholars, i.e. our master Abd al-Salam al-A'zami, may his secret be sanctified (al-Qamar). The intended meaning: it is the occurrence of the breaking of the oath with coming and eating together. 'And what is imagined' – i.e. in response to the speech (al-Qamar). 'So reflect' – perhaps it is an indication of the aspect of the fall of the imagination: either first: due to the corruption of the meaning; because the meaning would be 'if the coming to you was negated, and eating at your place occurred, then my slave is free', and this is a corrupt meaning, for the occurrence of eating at the addressee's place with the absence of coming to him is inconceivable. And second: because this does not benefit; because then it would be governed by 'in' which is also one of the jussive particles, and then it would also be necessary to drop the alif; so reflect (al-Qamar). 'Among them' – i.e. among the particles of meaning: the prepositions, and they are only called that because they draw the meaning of the verb to the noun (al-Qamar). 'For adhesion' – which is the attachment of one thing to another and its connection with it (al-Qamar). 'Is the thing adhered to' – and the other side is the adherer (al-Qamar). 'This' – i.e. adhesion is the origin of the Ba (al-Qamar). 'And it accompanies' – i.e. the Ba enters, and this Ba entering upon prices is the Ba of exchange, and here the meaning of adhesion is also realized; hence it was said that exchange is reducible to adhesion (al-Qamar). 'Prices etc.' – since the price is not intended but is like a tool, and the Ba is also a tool, so it enters upon the price, as in 'al-Da'ir' (al-Sanbuli).
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage defines the preposition 'Ba' in Arabic as a particle of adhesion (ilsaq). The author states that the original meaning of 'Ba' is to indicate that one thing is attached or adhered to another. For example, in the phrase 'I bought this slave for a price', the 'Ba' on the price indicates that the price is the thing adhered to (i.e., the thing that adheres to the transaction). Other uses of 'Ba', such as for instrumentality or exchange, are metaphorical extensions. The commentary (by al-Qamar) explains that adhesion means the attachment of a thing to another. When 'Ba' enters upon prices (like in a sale contract), it is called the Ba of exchange (muqabala), but the underlying meaning of adhesion remains, because the price is like a tool attached to the transaction. The text also includes a digression (from the previous discussion of 'Hatta') where the commentator emphasizes that jurists (fuqaha) prioritize the intended meaning over strict grammatical parsing, giving the example of qadhf where the pronunciation with kasra still necessitates the hadd. However, this lesson focuses on the definition of 'Ba'.

Key Terms
الباء (al-ba): A preposition whose original meaning is adhesion (ilsaq); other meanings are metaphorical.
الإلصاق (al-ilsaq): The attachment or adhesion of one thing to another; the original meaning of the letter 'Ba'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the original meaning of the letter 'Ba' according to this passage?
A) Exchange (muqabala)
B) Instrumentality (istia'ana)
C) Adhesion (ilsaq)
D) Companionship (musahaba)

Key Takeaway
The letter Ba fundamentally denotes adhesion; other meanings are metaphorical extensions, so in legal texts the default interpretation of Ba is adhesion unless context indicates otherwise.

Interpreting 'على' in Conditional vs Exchange Contexts
Examines the word 'على' in divorce conditional upon payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule for interpreting the word 'على' in non-exchange contexts and explain the practical difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In a non-exchange context, 'على' defaults to condition, not exchange — so no partial payment arises from a partial condition.

The word 'على' carries two possible meanings: condition (shart) or exchange (mu'awada). When a wife says 'Divorce me three times for one thousand dirhams,' the interpretation determines the legal consequence of a partial divorce. Abu Hanifa insists that 'على' indicates a condition, supported by Quranic usage, while the two companions (Sahibayn) see it as exchange due to the involvement of money. This lesson unpacks the linguistic arguments and the practical implications of this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic ambiguity
Recognize that the word 'على' in the phrase 'على ألف' can mean either 'upon condition of' or 'in exchange for'. This ambiguity leads to different legal outcomes.
The context does not specify the intended meaning, so usul principles must be applied.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Note that Abu Hanifa holds 'على' here means condition, citing Quran 60:12 where 'على' is used for a condition. He argues that divorce is not originally an exchange; compensation is incidental, so the word must default to its condition meaning.
The Quranic verse is a naqli dalil with zanni dalala regarding this specific application.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's position and proof
Understand that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) interpret 'على' as exchange because money is mentioned, analogous to sale and lease where 'على' means 'for'. They treat the thousand dirhams as a substitute for the divorces.
Their reasoning is based on the presence of a valuable consideration (mal) indicating mu'awada.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and logical argument
Learn the logical argument: Parts of a condition cannot be divided onto parts of the conditional. Just as half a sunrise does not produce half a day, paying part of the thousand cannot cancel part of the divorce. Therefore, if only one divorce occurs, no payment is due.
This argument relies on the nature of conditions as indivisible prerequisites.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Conclude the practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, one divorce yields no payment; under the Sahibayn, a proportionate payment (e.g., one-third) is required. This affects the financial liability of the husband.
The tarjih (preference) in this text leans toward Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn's view is acknowledged as strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث "على" وعند أبي حنيفة محله للشرط في هذا المثال؛ لأن الطلاق لم يكن من المعاوضات في الأصل، وإنما العوض فيه عارض، فلم يلحق بها، فكأنها قالت على شرط ألف درهم، وكلمة "على" تستعمل بمعنى الشرط قال الله تعالى: يُبَايِعْنَكَ عَلَى أَنْ لَا يُشْرِكْنَ بِاللَّهِ شَيْئًا، لأن الجزاء لازم للشرط، فيكون أقرب إلى معنى الحقيقة من معنى "الباء"، فإن طلقها واحدة لا يجب شيء؛ لأن أجزاء الشرط لا تنقسم على أجزاء المشروط هكذا قالوا. للشرط إلخ عملاً بالحقيقة فإذا قالت للزوج: "طلقني ثلاثًا على ألف" فطلقها واحدة، فعنده لا يجب شيء؛ لأن أجزاء الشرط لا ينقسم على أجزاء المشروط، وتحقيق ذلك أن ثبوت المشروط والشرط بطريق المعاقبة ضرورة توقف المشروط على الشرط من غير عكس، فلو انقسم أجزاء الشرط على أجزاء المشروط لزم تقدم جزء من المشروط على الشرط، فلا يتحقق المعاقبة. (السنبلي) للشرط: بأن يكون ما بعدها شرطًا لما قبلها. [فتح الغفار: ٢٠٦] لم يكن إلخ فإنه يكون بمال وبلا مال (القمر) عارض إلى أي إنما يكون العوض فيه عارضًا بتقييدها لا في أصلها؛ لأن الطلاق لا يتوقف على العوض، بخلاف البيع والإجارة، فإن الأصل فيهما هو العوض ولا تجوز قط . (السنبلي) فلم يلحق إلخ أي فلم يلحق الطلاق بالمعاوضات وكلمة "على" أيضا ليست بعض المعاوضة بخلاف ما إذا قالت بألف درهم؛ فإن الباء نص في المعاوضة، فيحمل على المعاوضة، ولك أن ترجح قول الصاحبين بأن المال صالح للعوضية والطلاق أيضًا يصلح لذلك، فالطلاق إذا قوبل بمال، فالظاهر أنه قصد المقابلة، فصار من المعاوضات فتدبر (القمر) على شرط ألف درهم فيه أن ألف درهم ليس بشرط للطلقات الثلاث؛ لأن الزوج أن يوقع الثلاث من غير توقف شيء، ويمكن أن يقال: إن الكلام محمول على القلب، فالألف مشروط، والطلقات الثلاث شرط. (القمر) على أن لا يشركن إلخ أي بشرط عدم الإشراك (القمر) لأن الجزاء لازم للشرط: كما أن المستعلي يلازم المستعلى عليه وهذا متعلق بقوله: تستعمل. (القمر) لازم للشرط: فمعنى الشرط أوفق وأطبق بمعناه الحقيقي أي الإلزام (المحشي) فيكون إلخ أي فيكون الشرط أقرب إلى المعنى الحقيقي لعلى، وهو الاستعلاء من معنى "الباء". (القمر) لا يجب شيء : أي للزوج على الزوجة، ويكون الطلاق رجعياً. (القمر) لا تنقسم إلخ ألا ترى أن الشرط في قولنا : "إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود" طلوع الشمس، وليس أنه إذا طلع نصف الشمس وجد نصف النهار، والسر فيه أنه لو انقسم أجزاء الشرط على أجزاء المشروط لزم أن يتقدم جزء من المشروط على الشرط بتمامه. (القمر)
Discussion of 'على'. According to Abu Hanifa, its place is for the condition in this example because divorce is not originally from exchanges; rather, the compensation therein is incidental, so it does not join them. It is as if she said 'upon condition of one thousand dirhams.' The word 'على' is used in the meaning of condition. Allah Most High said: 'They pledge to you upon that they will not associate anything with Allah' because the consequence is necessary for the condition, so it is closer to the real meaning than the meaning of 'باء'. If he divorces her once, nothing is obligatory because the parts of the condition are not divided upon the parts of the conditional. Thus they said: 'for the condition etc.' acting upon the real meaning. So if she says to the husband: 'Divorce me three times for one thousand,' and he divorces her once, according to him nothing is obligatory because the parts of the condition are not divided upon the parts of the conditional. The verification of that is that the establishment of the conditional and the condition is by way of concomitance due to the necessity of dependence of the conditional upon the condition without the reverse. So if the parts of the condition were divided upon the parts of the conditional, it would necessitate that a part of the conditional precedes the condition, so concomitance would not be realized. (al-Sunbuli) 'for the condition': that what follows it is a condition for what precedes it. [Fath al-Ghaffar: 206] 'it was not etc.': It occurs with property and without property. (al-Qamar) 'incidental': The compensation is only incidental by her restriction, not in its origin, because divorce does not depend on compensation, unlike sale and lease, for in them compensation is original and they cannot occur without it. (al-Sunbuli) 'so it does not join etc.': Divorce does not join the exchanges, and the word 'على' is also not part of exchange, unlike if she said 'with one thousand dirhams' because 'باء' is explicit in exchange, so it is interpreted as exchange. But you may prefer the statement of the two companions because property is suitable for exchange and divorce is also suitable for that, so when divorce is confronted with property, apparently the intention is exchange, so it becomes from exchanges, so reflect. (al-Qamar) 'upon condition of one thousand dirhams': That one thousand dirhams is not a condition for the three divorces because the husband can execute three without any dependency, and it can be said that the statement is inverted, so the thousand is conditional and the three divorces are the condition. (al-Qamar) 'upon that they will not associate etc.': i.e., upon condition of not associating. (al-Qamar) 'because the consequence is necessary for the condition': just as the one above is concomitant with what is above, and this relates to his saying 'is used'. (al-Qamar) 'necessary for the condition': the meaning of condition is more in harmony and consistent with its real meaning, i.e., obligation. (al-Muhashi) 'so it is etc.': i.e., the condition is closer to the real meaning of 'على', which is superiority, than the meaning of 'باء'. (al-Qamar) 'nothing must be given': i.e., by the husband to the wife, and the divorce is revocable. (al-Qamar) 'it is not divided etc.': Do you not see that the condition in our saying 'If the sun is rising, then the daytime exists' is the rising of the sun, and it is not that if half the sun rises, half the daytime exists. The secret in it is that if the parts of the condition were divided upon the parts of the conditional, it would necessitate that a part of the conditional precedes the condition in its entirety. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'على' in the context of a wife telling her husband: 'Divorce me three times for one thousand dirhams.' The discussion centers on whether 'على' implies a condition (like 'upon condition that') or an exchange (like 'in exchange for'). According to Abu Hanifa, since divorce is not originally an exchange (compensation is incidental), 'على' here means a condition, not an exchange. He supports this by citing the Quranic verse where women pledge 'upon that they will not associate' (60:12), interpreting 'على' as a condition. Consequently, if the husband divorces only once, no payment is due because the condition (the thousand dirhams) is indivisible; you cannot pay part of the condition for part of the divorce. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, viewing 'على' as indicating exchange because the context involves money, so they treat it like the word 'باء'. They argue that the compensation is the intent, so a single divorce would require a proportionate payment. The commentary then discusses further points: the condition must precede the conditional, and the example of the sun rising is given to illustrate that the parts of a condition cannot be divided onto parts of the conditional. The passage also notes that the preferred view (al-mukhtar) leans towards Abu Hanifa, but it acknowledges the strength of the companions' argument.

Key Terms
على (‘alā): In this context, 'على' is analyzed for its meaning: either for condition (shart) or for exchange (mu‘āwaḍa).
شرط (sharṭ): A condition that must be fulfilled for the consequence to occur; here the payment is a condition for divorce.
معاوضة (mu‘āwaḍa): An exchange contract where each party gives something in return; here divorce is considered an exchange if 'على' means for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a wife says 'Divorce me three times for one thousand dirhams' and the husband divorces her once, what is due?
A) Nothing, because the condition (the thousand) is indivisible.
B) One-third of the thousand, because the divorce is divisible.
C) The full thousand, because the husband promised three divorces.
D) Half of the thousand, because it is an exchange.

Key Takeaway
In non-exchange contracts, the word 'على' is interpreted as a condition, making its terms indivisible, so partial performance does not trigger a proportional obligation.

The Word 'إلى' (ila): End Limit and Inclusion Rule
Explains the Hanafi rule for inclusion of a limit (ghaya) based on independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when a limit (ghaya) is included in the scope of a ruling and apply it to textual examples.

Core Idea
If the limit is independent (qā'imah bi nafsihā), it is not included; if dependent, it is included.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the preposition 'ila' (to/until) is key to determining the boundaries of a legal ruling. Does the endpoint itself become part of the rule? The answer depends on the nature of that endpoint—whether it stands independent of the object being limited or is an integral part of it. This lesson unpacks the rule of independence, illustrating with the classical example of walls in an acknowledgment (iqrar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the function of 'ila'
Locate the preposition 'ila' in a legal text and note that it marks the end of a limit (ghaya). Determine whether the limit is spatial or temporal.
2
Assess the limit's independence
Ask: Does the object after 'ila' exist independently of the object before it? Is it a separate entity that can exist without the limited (mughayya)?
Example: a wall is independent; a door integrated into a house is dependent.
3
Apply the inclusion rule
If the limit is independent (qā'imah bi nafsihā), it is excluded from the ruling. If it is dependent (not standing alone), it is included.
The passage states: 'if the limit is standing by itself, it is not included.'
4
Confirm with the wall example
In the statement 'from this wall to that wall' (in an iqrar), both walls are independent limits, so they are not part of the acknowledgment.
The walls exist before the speech act and do not depend on the property for their existence.

Source Text

(القمر) لا تدخل الغايتان أي المبدأ ،والمنتهى، فإن "إلى" لا تدل على الدخول ولا على عدمه فلو كانت الثانية غير مستقلة وتابعة للمغيا تدخل، وإذا كانت مستقلة و لم يوجد سبب آخر لم يتحقق دليل الدخول، فلا تدخل كذا قيل. (القمر) الغايتان أي غاية الابتداء وغاية الانتهاء؛ لأن الغاية حد المغيا والحد لا يدخل في المحدود. (المحشي) أي موجودة أي بوجود منفرد عن المغيا. (القمر) غير مفتقرة إلخ فإن الحائط ليس بمفتقر إلى البيت مثلاً لجواز أن يوجد في الصحراء. (القمر) فلا تدخلان أي الغايتان المبدأ والمنتهى. (المحشي)
Discussion of the word 'ila'. 'And ila' is for the end of the limit, i.e., the end of the distance; the limit is called 'ghaya' as a part-whole metaphor, as it is said. 'The distance' - the speaker is the author of al-Talwih. Then he explained the rule: in which cases the limit is included and in which cases it is not included. He said: if the limit is independently standing, like his saying: 'from this wall to this wall', the two limits are not included in the acceptance (iqrar), because the wall is a limit standing by itself, i.e., existing before the speech act and not dependent for its existence on the limited (al-mughayya), so they are not included in the limited. And we said 'existing before the speech act' to exclude... (al-Qamar) 'there' i.e., in his saying: 'whomever wishes from my slaves, set him free' (al-Qamar) 'i.e., for the end of the distance' etc.: because an objection might arise against the apparent wording of the author that the limit is the end, so there is no meaning to 'end of the limit', the commentator refuted it by saying 'i.e., for the end of the distance' etc. (al-Qamar) 'the limit is included' i.e., in what is before 'ila'. Then know that there are four schools among Arabic scholars regarding 'ila': first, what comes after it is included in the ruling of what comes before it absolutely; second, no inclusion absolutely; third, inclusion if what comes after is of the same kind as what comes before, otherwise not; fourth, inclusion or non-inclusion requires external evidence, and 'ila' does not indicate inclusion nor non-inclusion. The author provided a detail by saying: 'if it is' etc. (al-Qamar) 'standing by itself': it is said that what is meant by standing by itself is that the limit is extrinsic and not part of what is before it. (al-Qamar) 'the two limits are not included' i.e., the starting point and the endpoint, for 'ila' does not indicate inclusion nor non-inclusion. If the second [limit] is not independent and is dependent on the limited, it is included; if it is independent and no other cause exists, the evidence for inclusion is not established, so it is not included, as it is said. (al-Qamar) 'the two limits' i.e., the limit of beginning and the limit of ending, because the limit is the boundary of the limited, and the boundary is not included in the bounded. (al-Muhashi) 'i.e., existing' i.e., existing separately from the limited. (al-Qamar) 'not dependent' etc., for the wall is not dependent on the house, for example, because it can exist in the desert. (al-Qamar) 'so they are not included' i.e., the two limits: the beginning and the end. (al-Muhashi)
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage discusses the preposition 'إلى' (ila) and its function as indicating the end of a limit (ghaya). The text begins by defining 'إلى' as indicating the termination of a distance, using a part-whole metaphor. It then addresses a key rule: when does the limit (the object of 'إلى') itself enter into the ruling of what precedes it? The rule is that if the limit is 'standing by itself' (qā'imah bi nafsihā), meaning it is independent and not dependent on the limited (mughayya) for its existence, then it is not included. The example given is 'from this wall to this wall' in an iqrar (acknowledgment) context: the two walls are not part of the acknowledged property because they are independent entities existing before the statement. The commentary notes that there are four linguistic schools on the inclusion rule: absolute inclusion, absolute exclusion, inclusion if of the same kind, and undetermined. The author of Noorul Anwar adopts the detail that when the limit is independent, it is excluded; if dependent, it is included. This rule is applied in Hanafi usul to determine the scope of terms in legal documents.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): Preposition indicating the end of a limit (ghaya), i.e., terminus.
غاية (ghaya): The limit or endpoint; the boundary of a spatial or temporal range.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, both walls are included.
B) No, neither wall is included.
C) Only the first wall is included.
D) Only the second wall is included.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the inclusion of a limit (ghaya) depends on its independence: independent limits are excluded, dependent limits are included.

The Hanafi Linguistic Analysis of 'Ind' as Custody
Linguistic and legal analysis of 'ind' in declarations of possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the word 'ind' in a legal declaration to determine whether it indicates custody (amāna) or debt (dayn) by applying the Hanafi principle that 'ind' is an adverb of presence, not ownership.

Core Idea
'Ind' in Hanafi usul: presence, not ownership — the linguistic key to distinguishing amāna from dayn.

This lesson examines the Hanafi linguistic analysis of the word 'ind' (at/with). The passage refines the common understanding: 'ind' primarily indicates presence (ḥaḍra), not ownership. By classifying 'ind' as an adverb (ẓarf) rather than a verbal noun, Hanafi scholars derive that its default implication in legal declarations is custody and preservation (amāna), not debt. This distinction has practical consequences in contracts and testimonies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the declaration
Recognize the phrase containing 'ind' (e.g., 'this is with me' or 'I have something' in Arabic: 'hādhā ladayya' or 'indī māl').
The literal preposition 'ind' or its variants like 'ladayya' are common in declarations of possession.
2
Determine literal vs. figurative usage
Assess whether 'ind' refers to sensory presence (physical) or conceptual presence (legal/custodial). For example, 'Zayd is with Amr' is sensory; 'I have wealth' may be conceptual if the wealth is elsewhere.
The passage notes 'ind' can be hadra ḥissiyya or ḥukmiyya.
3
Apply the Hanafi default rule
State the Hanafi principle: 'ind' by default implies amāna (trust) because it indicates custody and preservation, not debt or ownership. This is derived from its linguistic status as a ẓarf (adverb) of place.
The commentator stresses that 'ind' is a ẓarf, not a maṣdar, so it does not inherently imply a transfer of ownership.
4
Check for contextual modification
If the declaration explicitly links 'ind' to a debt (e.g., 'indī dayn' meaning 'I owe a debt'), the presumption shifts to dayn. Otherwise, the presumption of amāna stands.
This nuance is critical in fiqh: a trustee (amīn) is only liable for negligence, while a debtor must repay.
5
Derive the legal effect
Conclude that the person holding the item is presumed to be a trustee unless evidence of debt overrides. This affects burden of proof in disputes.
The passage from Fath al-Ghaffar explains: 'this is with me' means it is preserved in my custody, not necessarily owned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند للحضرة حقيقة كزيد عند عمرو، أو حكمًا كعندي مال وإن كان المال في بيتك، ثم الأولى أن يقول المصنف: "و "عند" لمكان الحضور؛ فإنها ظرف لا مصدر، والأمر في العبارة هين. (القمر) للحضرة إلخ قال في "المغني": عند اسم للحضور الحسي والمعنوي وللقرب كذلك. [فتح الغفار ٢١٣] إن هذا على الحفظ أي على أنها محفوظة في يدي وعندي. (القمر)
And 'ind' is for presence, literally like Zayd is with Amr, or figuratively like 'I have wealth' even if the wealth is in your house. Then it is better that the author says: 'And 'ind' is for the place of presence, for it is an adverb not a verbal noun, and the matter in expression is easy. (al-Qamar) For presence etc., he said in al-Mughni: 'ind' is a name for sensory and conceptual presence and for proximity as well. [Fath al-Ghaffar 213]. This is for preservation, i.e., that it is preserved in my hand and in my possession. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic refinement of the word 'ind' (at/with). The base text likely stated that 'ind' indicates presence (hadra). The commentator explains that 'ind' is used both literally (as in 'Zayd is with Amr') and figuratively (as in 'I have wealth' even if not physically present). He then suggests that the author of the matn should have defined 'ind' as a place of presence (makān al-ḥuḍūr) because it functions as an adverb (ẓarf) not a verbal noun (maṣdar). He quotes al-Mughni to confirm that 'ind' denotes sensory and conceptual presence and proximity. Finally, he clarifies that when someone says 'this is with me' (hādhā ladayya), it means it is preserved in his custody, not necessarily owned. This analysis is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for distinguishing between amāna (trust) and dayn (debt) in legal declarations.

Key Terms
عند (ind): A preposition meaning 'at' or 'with', indicating sensory or conceptual presence. In legal context, it implies custody and preservation, not necessarily ownership.
الحضرة (al-hadra): Presence, both sensory (physical) and conceptual (mental or legal).
ظرف (zarf): An adverb of time or place. The author argues that 'ind' is an adverb, not a verbal noun, affecting its grammatical properties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Debt
C) Presence and custody
D) Proximity only

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'ind' is an adverb of presence that creates a legal presumption of custody (amāna), not ownership or debt, unless context modifies it.

Ghayr and Siwa: The Legal Effect of Case Endings
Grammatical-legal analysis of 'ghayr' and 'siwa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'ghayr' as adjective (ṣifa) and exception (istithnā') based on its case ending (rafe vs. nasb), and apply this to determine the amount owed in a debtor's statement.

Core Idea
The grammatical case of 'ghayr' — rafe for adjective, nasb for exception — determines whether a full or partial amount is owed.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, even a small grammatical shift can change a legal obligation. The word 'ghayr' (other than) can function as an adjective describing a noun or as an exception excluding something. The key is its grammatical case. This lesson examines the classic example of a debtor saying 'I owe a dirham other than a daniq' and how the case of 'ghayr' (rafe or nasb) leads to different outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its case
In the statement, locate 'ghayr' and determine its grammatical case: nominative (rafe) or accusative (nasb). The case ending is visible in Arabic script.
In our example 'غَيرُ' vs 'غَيرَ'.
2
Determine function: adjective or exception
Apply the Hanafi rule: rafe indicates 'ghayr' is an adjective (ṣifa); nasb indicates it is an exception (istithnā').
Adjective describes the noun; exception excludes something.
3
Interpret the legal obligation
If adjective, the debtor owes the full amount mentioned (a dirham) because nothing is excepted. If exception, the debtor owes the amount minus the excepted item (dirham minus daniq).
A daniq is one-sixth of a dirham.
4
Apply to 'siwa'
Recognize that 'siwa' has a similar function but, being an adverb with implied declension (i'rāb taqdīrī), its interpretation relies on the speaker's intention, and a judge may not accept an exception claim if it seems like mitigation.
The passage notes the judge may not believe the speaker in the case of mitigation (taḥfīf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث "غير" يستعمل صفة للنكرة، ويستعمل استثناء، لكن الاستعمال الأول أصل فيه، والثاني تبع فهو أيضًا داخل في الظروف تغليبًا كقوله : له" على درهم غير دائق بالرفع، فيلزمه در هم تام؛ لأنه حينئذٍ ،صفة للدرهم، فيكون المعنى له علي الدرهم الذي هو مغاير للدائق، فلا يستثنى منه شيء فيلزم درهم تام. ولو قال بالنصب كان استثناء، فيلزمه درهم إلا دانقا وهو مقدار سدس الدرهم، أي بنصب غير الدائق و"سوى" مثل "غير" في كونه صفة واستثناء وهو ظرف في الحقيقة، لكن لما كان إعرابه تقديريًا يحال على النية، ولعل القاضي لا يصدقه في صورة التخفيف.
Statement of the divisions of the third division. 'Ghayr' is used as an adjective for the indefinite, and is used as an exception, but the first usage is original in it, and the second is subsidiary, so it is also included among adverbs by preponderance, like his saying: 'He has against me a dirham other than a daniq' with rafe, so a full dirham is binding because at that time it is an adjective for the dirham, so the meaning is: He has against me the dirham which is different from the daniq, so nothing is excepted from it, so a full dirham is binding. And if he said with nasb, it is an exception, so a dirham except a daniq is binding, and a daniq is one-sixth of a dirham, i.e., with nasb of 'ghayr' and 'danq'. And 'siwa' is like 'ghayr' in being an adjective and exception, and it is an adverb in reality, but because its declension is constructive, it is referred to intention, and perhaps the judge does not believe him in the case of mitigation.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and legal analysis of the words 'ghayr' (other than/except) and 'siwa' (except). In Hanafi law, the interpretation of a legal statement containing 'ghayr' depends on its grammatical case. If 'ghayr' is in the nominative case (rafe), it functions as an adjective (ṣifa) describing the preceding noun. For example, 'He has against me a dirham other than a daniq' with rafe means he owes a full dirham that is different from a daniq coin, so no exception is made and he owes a complete dirham. If 'ghayr' is in the accusative case (nasb), it functions as an exception (istithnā'), meaning he owes a dirham minus a daniq (i.e., 5/6 of a dirham). The word 'siwa' is similar in that it can be adjective or exception, but because its declension is implied (iẓhār taqdīrī), it is referred to the speaker's intention, and a judge may not accept an exception claim if the speaker claims an exception with 'siwa' in the case of mitigation (taḥfīf), likely referring to a legal trick.

Key Terms
غير (ghayr): A word meaning 'other than' or 'except'. It can function as an adjective (ṣifa) when in nominative case, or as an exception (istithnā') when in accusative case.
سوى (siwa): A word similar to 'ghayr' meaning 'except', but its declension is constructive (i'rāb taqdīrī), so it is referred to intention.
استثناء (istithnā'): Exception, a grammatical construction that excludes something from the general statement.
صفة (ṣifa): Adjective, a word that describes a noun without excluding anything.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the statement 'He has against me a dirham other than a daniq' with 'ghayr' in the nominative case (rafe), how much is owed?
A) One full dirham
B) One dirham minus a daniq
C) A daniq
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The case ending of 'ghayr' (rafe vs. nasb) dictates whether it is an adjective or exception, and thus the amount owed.
